Al-Ash'ari vs. al-Baqillani on the Effect of Human Power
al-Baqillani explains his view that human power affects the attribute of kasb, not existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between al-Ash'ari's and al-Baqillani's views on the effect of human power on the attributes of actions, and al-Baqillani's argument that human power affects the attribute of kasb (acquisition) but not existence.

Core Idea
Human power affects the moral quality (kasb) of an act, not its existence.

This passage presents a nuanced internal Ash'ari debate. While both agree that God creates all actions, they differ on whether human power contributes anything at all. al-Ash'ari denies any effect, but al-Baqillani argues that human power gives the act its specific moral character—obedience or disobedience—which he calls 'acquisition' (kasb). The following text explains his reasoning and a deeper theoretical justification using the concept of states (ahwal).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Let the author's own argument come through before you interpret.
Notice the use of the term 'kasb' and the example of obedience vs. disobedience.
2
Identify the two positions
Identify al-Ash'ari's position (no effect at all) and al-Baqillani's position (effect on the attribute, not existence). Mark how they differ.
Al-Ash'ari denies effect on both existence and attributes; al-Baqillani allows effect on the attribute called kasb.
3
Analyze al-Baqillani's main argument
Work through his argument using the shared essence vs. distinguishing attribute. Understand why he thinks the moral quality is separate from existence.
The movement that is obedience and the movement that is disobedience share being movement but differ in being obedience/disobedience.
4
Examine the deeper justification with ahwal
Read the footnote section where al-Baqillani extends the argument using the theory of states (ahwal). Notice how he analogizes to Mu'tazilite principles.
He argues that human power can affect a 'state' (hāl) of the act, such as its specific form, just as God's power affects the state of origination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أتباعه . كالقاضي ( أبي بكر الباقلاني (1) وابن فورك ، ثم حصل ههنا بين الأشعري ، وبين ( القاضي ، اختلاف من وجه آخر . فقال الأشعري : قدرة العبد كما لم تؤثر في وجود الفعل البتة، لم تؤثر أيضاً في شيء من صفات ذلك الفعل وقال القاضي : ( قدرة العبد وإن لم تؤثر في وجود ذلك الفعل ، إلا أنها أثرت في صفة من صفات ذلك الفعل ، وتلك الصفة هي المسماة بالكسب ) قال : ( وذلك لأن الحركة التي هي [ طاعة والحركة التي هي (٢) ] معصية قد اشتركا في كون كل منهما حركة ، وامتازت إحداهما عن الأخرى بكونها طاعة أو معصية . وما به المشاركة غير ما به الممايزة. فثبت : ان كونها حركة غير ، وكونها طاعة أو معصية فذات الحركة ووجودها واقع بقدرة الله تعالى . أما كونها طاعة أو معصية فهو صفة واقعة بقدرة العبد ) • هذا تلخيص مذهب و القاضي : على أحسن الوجوه (1) ( والقاضي أبو بكر الباقلاني تخطى عن هذا القدر قليلا . فقال : والدليل قد قام على أن القدرة الحادثة لا تصلح للإيجاد ، لكن ليست تقصر صفات الفعل أو وجوده واعتباراته على جهة الحدوث فقط ، بل ههنا وجوه آخر ، وراء الحدوث من كون الجوهر متحيزاً قابلا للعرض ، ومن كون العرض عرضاً ولوناً وسواداً وغير ذلك . وهذه أحوال عند مثبتي الأحوال ، قال : « فجهة كون الفعل حاصلا بالقدرة الحاثة أو تحتها : نسبة خاصة ، يسمى ذلك كسباً . وذلك هو أثر القدرة الحادثة ، قال : « فإذا جاز على أصل المعتزلة أن يكون تأثير القدرة أو القادرية القديمة في حال هو الحدوث والوجود، أو في وجه من وجوه الفعل . فلم لا يجوز أن يكون تأثير القدرة الحادثة في حال هو صفة للحادث أو في وجه من وجوه الفعل ، وهو كون الحركة مثلاً على هيئة مخصوصة ؟ )
His followers, such as al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani and Ibn Furak. Then here between al-Ash'ari and al-Qadi there occurred a disagreement from another aspect. Al-Ash'ari said: The power of the servant, just as it had no effect on the existence of the act at all, also had no effect on any of the attributes of that act. And al-Qadi said: The power of the servant, even if it had no effect on the existence of that act, yet it did effect an attribute from among the attributes of that act, and that attribute is what is called 'acquisition' (al-kasb). He said: That is because the movement that is obedience and the movement that is disobedience share in that each of them is a movement, and one of them is distinguished from the other by being obedience or disobedience. That by which they share is other than that by which they are distinguished. So it is established: that their being a movement is other than their being obedience or disobedience. So the essence of the movement and its existence occur by the power of Allah the Exalted. As for their being obedience or disobedience, that is an attribute occurring by the power of the servant. This is a summary of the doctrine of al-Qadi in the best manner. (1) (And al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani went a little beyond this measure. He said: The proof has established that the originated power (al-qudra al-haditha) is not fit for bringing into existence, but it is not limited to the attributes of the act or its existence and considerations being only in the mode of origination; rather there are other aspects beyond origination, such as the substance being space-occupying and receptive of accidents, and the accident being an accident, a color, blackness, etc. And these are 'states' (ahwal) according to the affirmers of states. He said: 'So the aspect of the act coming about by or under the originated power is a special relation, which is called kasb. That is the effect of the originated power.' He said: 'If it is permissible according to the principle of the Mu'tazila that the effect of the ancient power or potency should be in a state such as origination and existence, or in an aspect of the aspects of the act, why should it not be permissible that the effect of the originated power should be in a state that is an attribute of the originated thing or in an aspect of the aspects of the act, such as the movement being, for example, in a specific form?'
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage records a disagreement within the Ash'ari school between al-Ash'ari and al-Baqillani regarding the effect of human power (qudra al-'abd) on actions. Al-Ash'ari held that human power has no effect on either the existence of the act or any of its attributes. Al-Baqillani, however, distinguished between the existence of the act and its attribute of being obedience or disobedience. He argued that two movements, one obedience and one disobedience, share the essence of movement but differ in their moral quality. The existence of the movement comes from God, but the attribute of being obedience or disobedience is caused by human power, and this attribute is called 'kasb' (acquisition). The footnote elaborates al-Baqillani's position further: he denies that originated power can create existence, but it can affect certain aspects or states (ahwal) of the act, such as its specific form. He uses the Mu'tazilite principle that ancient power affects the state of origination to analogously argue that originated power can affect the state of being a specific attribute.

Key Terms
قدرة العبد
كسب
طاعة
معصية
صفة الفعل
أحوال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think al-Baqillani grants humans causal power over existence, but he only attributes the attribute of kasb to them.
Correction: Al-Baqillani explicitly states that human power does not affect existence; it only affects the attribute of being obedience or disobedience (kasb).

Checkpoint
According to al-Baqillani, what does human power affect in the action?
A) The existence of the action itself
B) The attribute of the action that makes it obedience or disobedience
C) Both the existence and the attribute
D) Neither the existence nor the attribute

Key Takeaway
Al-Baqillani distinguishes between the existence of an act (from God) and its moral attribute (kasb, from human power), thus preserving human responsibility without compromising divine omnipotence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you see it as something you created by your own power, or as something whose existence is from Allah but whose moral blame is yours through kasb? How does this change your remorse and your turning to Allah?
?
Al-Baqillani says your power affects only the attribute of the act, not its existence. Can you truly accept that your good deeds are entirely from Allah's creation, while your bad deeds are also from His creation but you acquire their evil quality? What does that demand of your gratitude and repentance?
?
If the existence of every act is from Allah alone, are you willing to give up any sense of self-praise for your achievements? What would it take for you to feel, in your heart, that your power is merely an instrument for acquiring moral quality, not for bringing things into being?

The Second External Cause: Desire for Leadership
Al-Razi explains the second external cause: desire for leadership strengthens beliefs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the desire for leadership and worldly status can strengthen adherence to a particular doctrine or action, as explained by al-Razi.

Core Idea
Worldly desires, not just intellect, can anchor beliefs.

Al-Razi continues his analysis of external causes that reinforce conviction. Having covered the first cause, he now turns to a powerful psychological factor: the human love for status and leadership. This passage shows how personal ambition can unconsciously strengthen one's commitment to a belief system, a phenomenon observable in everyday life.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice that al-Razi presents a clear causal chain: leadership → inclination → reinforcement.
The passage is concise; pay attention to the logical connectors.
2
Identify the causal mechanism
Al-Razi states that if a doctrine brings leadership, the heart inclines toward it. Why? Because souls naturally love status, wealth, and pleasure. So the desire for these goods motivates the inclination.
This is not an intellectual argument but a psychological one.
3
Notice the role of repetition
Al-Razi reminds us that repetition strengthens (التكرير سبب للتأكيد). The repeated inclination due to worldly benefit reinforces the belief.
This ties the external cause to a general principle.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this cause operates in real life. Can you think of examples where people adopt or defend beliefs because of social rewards?
This helps internalize the concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السبب الثاني من الأسباب الخارجية : أن يكون تقرير ذلك المذهب مما يفيده رئاسة في الدنيا، وتفوقا على الأقران . فهذا أيضا مما يوجب ميل قلبه إلى تلك العقائد والأخلاق . وذلك لأن النفوس مجبولة على حب الجاه والمال ، واللذة والسرور ، وعلى حب كل ما يكون وسيلة إلى حصول هذه الأمور . فإذا كان تقرير مذهب من المذاهب يقيده ذلك المطلوب مال قلبه إلى تقرير ذلك المذهب. وقد عرفت : أن التكرير سبب للتأكيد . فثبت : أن هذا الذي ذكرناه من جملة الأسباب التي توجب تأكد الاعتقاد في المذهب المخصوص ، والفعل المخصوص
The second of the external causes: that advocating that doctrine brings him leadership in the world and superiority over peers. This also causes his heart to incline toward those beliefs and morals. That is because souls are naturally disposed to love status, wealth, pleasure, and delight, and to love everything that is a means to obtaining these things. So if advocating a certain doctrine secures that desired object, his heart inclines toward advocating that doctrine. And you have known that repetition is a cause of reinforcement. So it is established that this which we have mentioned is among the causes that necessitate the strengthening of belief in a particular doctrine and a particular action.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage enumerates the second external cause that strengthens adherence to specific doctrines and actions. The author, al-Razi, states that when a person's advocacy of a particular doctrine brings him leadership and superiority, his heart naturally inclines toward that doctrine. This is because human souls are created with a love for status, wealth, and pleasure, and they love whatever leads to these. Therefore, if promoting a doctrine is a means to such worldly gains, the heart inclines toward it. He then reminds the reader that repetition reinforces, so this repeated inclination due to worldly benefits strengthens the belief. The passage concludes by asserting that this is one of the causes that necessitate firm conviction in a specific doctrine and action.

Key Terms
السبب الثاني
رئاسة
تفوق
الجاه
المال
التكرير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Beliefs are only strengthened by rational arguments and intellectual evidence.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that external factors like the desire for leadership can also reinforce beliefs, independent of rational justification.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, what is the second external cause that strengthens adherence to a particular doctrine?
A) Habit and custom
B) Practice in the art of reasoning
C) Desire for leadership and superiority
D) Love of pleasure

Key Takeaway
The desire for leadership and worldly status can reinforce belief in a doctrine, as the heart inclines toward what brings worldly benefits, and repetition solidifies that inclination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold strongly. To what extent is your conviction strengthened because holding that belief brings you status or recognition among others? Are you willing to examine your heart and let go of any belief whose sole support is the worldly advantage it brings you?
?
Al-Razi says souls are naturally disposed to love status and wealth. Look at your own heart: Are you advocating any Islamic viewpoint publicly mainly because it gives you a sense of superiority over peers? How can you purify your intention so that your adherence to truth is not tainted by desire for leadership?
?
Repetition reinforces belief, even when the motive is worldly. What repeated actions or associations in your life are strengthening beliefs that may not be from Allah? Are you willing to break the cycle and seek only His pleasure?

Practice in Reasoning: Quantity and Quality
Al-Razi explains the first mechanism: practice in reasoning and its two dimensions of variation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how practice in reasoning affects one's ability to grasp truth, and how variation in quantity and quality of premises leads to unregulated differences in preparedness.

Core Idea
More practice makes truth easier, but quantity and quality of premises vary infinitely.

Al-Razi explains that the third external cause is practice in reasoning. He describes how people differ in how many premises they can bring to mind and how easily they move from premises to conclusions. These differences are unregulated, leading to unbounded variation in people's receptiveness to knowledge and virtue.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two aspects of variation
Read the passage and locate where al-Razi discusses variation according to quantity and quality. Note what each means.
Quantity refers to readiness to bring premises; quality refers to ease of moving from premises to conclusions.
2
Understand the effect of practice
Al-Razi states that more practice makes grasping truth easier. Reflect on how this connects to the two aspects.
3
Grasp why variation is unregulated
The passage explains that these differences vary in intensity and abundance without bound, leading to unregulated preparedness.
This implies that human capacities are not uniform.
4
Connect to the broader discussion
This is the third external cause. Consider how it interacts with other causes mentioned earlier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السبب الثالث من الأسباب الخارجية : أن الإنسان إذا مارس صناعة النظر والاستدلال . فكل من كانت ممارسته لهذه الصناعة أكثر ، كان وقوفه على الحق أسهل . ثم إن التفاوت يقع في هذا الباب بحسب الكم . فإن بعضهم يكون أكثر استحضارا للمقدمات . وأما بحسب الكيف فأن يكون انفعال بعضهم من المقدمات الحاضرة ، إلى النتائج المستحضرة : أسهل . ولما كان التفاوت في هذين البابين ، مما يختلف بالأشد والأضعف ، والأقل والأكثر اختلافا غير مضبوط . لا جرم كان اختلاف الناس في الاستعداد لقبول المعارف اليقينية ، والأخلاق الفاضلة ، اختلافا غير مضبوط . واعلم : أن هذا السبب يوجب اختلاف الناس في العقائد من وجه آخر . وذلك لأن صاحب النظر والاستدلال ، قد ينتج فكره أن القول الفلاني حق صحيح . ولأجل أن ذلك كان من مستنبطات خاطره ، ومن نتائج فكره عظم حبه لذلك . وقد يبلغ ذلك الحب إلى حيث يمنعه عن التأمل في صحته وفساده . وقد تبلغ قوة تلك المحبة إلى أنه لو سمع حجة دالة على فساد تلك الحالة ، فإنه لا يفهمها ، ولا يقف على كيفيتها . كما قيل : وحبك الشيء يعمي ويصم ، ولما كان تفاوت الناس في مراتب هذه المحبة ، غير مضبوطة . لا جرم كان تفاوت الناس في العقائد والأخلاق ، بناء على هذا السبب : تفاوتا غير مضبوط . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن كل واحد من هذه الأسباب السنة : جنس تحته أنواع غير محصورة . وإن اختلافها يوجب اختلاف الناس في العقائد ، وفي الأفعال
The third of the external causes: that when a person practices the craft of contemplation and inference, then the more his practice of this craft, the easier his grasp of truth becomes. Then variation occurs in this regard according to quantity: some of them have a greater readiness to bring premises to mind. As for variation according to quality, it is that some of them are more easily affected by the present premises toward the results they have in mind. And since variation in these two aspects differs in intensity and weakness, in fewness and abundance, in an unregulated manner, inevitably the variation among people in their preparedness to accept certain knowledge and virtuous morals is unregulated. Know that this cause necessitates variation among people in beliefs from another angle. That is because the person of contemplation and inference may produce from his thought that such and such a statement is true and correct. And because it is from the conclusions of his mind and the results of his thought, his love for it becomes great. This love may reach a point where it prevents him from reflecting on its soundness or unsoundness. The strength of that love may even reach such a degree that if he hears an argument proving the unsoundness of that state, he does not understand it nor grasp its nature. As it is said: 'Your love of something blinds and deafens.' And since variation among people in the ranks of this love is unregulated, inevitably variation among people in beliefs and morals based on this cause is unregulated. So it is established from what we have mentioned that each of these six causes is a genus under which are uncountable species, and their variation necessitates variation among people in beliefs and actions.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage explains the third external cause: practice in the art of reasoning. Al-Razi first notes that more practice makes grasping truth easier. However, people differ both in quantity (how many premises they can bring to mind) and in quality (how easily they move from premises to conclusions). Since these differences are unregulated, people's preparedness for certain knowledge and virtue varies without bound. Then he adds another aspect: a thinker may fall in love with his own conclusions. This love can become so strong that it prevents him from examining the truth of his belief, even blinding him to contrary evidence. He quotes the proverb 'love of something blinds and deafens.' This love also varies unregulatedly. Finally, he concludes the entire discussion of external causes (though only the second and third are presented in this window) by stating that each of the six causes is a genus with uncountable species, and their variation causes variation in beliefs and actions.

Key Terms
السبب الثالث
صناعة النظر والاستدلال
الكم
الكيف
المقدمات
النتائج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People assume that practice in reasoning leads to uniform improvement in all individuals.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that differences in quantity of premises and quality of inference are unregulated, causing unbounded variation in readiness for truth.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, what are the two aspects of variation in reasoning practice?
A) Quantity and quality
B) Speed and accuracy
C) Memorization and understanding
D) Theory and practice

Key Takeaway
Practice in reasoning aids truth, but people differ unregulatedly in premises and inference ease, causing unbounded variation in receptiveness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you struggle to grasp a truth in your faith or reasoning, do you honestly attribute that to insufficient practice, or do you recognize that your natural capacity in quantity and quality of inference may differ from others? What would change if you accepted that your limits are partly unregulated?
?
Think of a time you felt certain of a conclusion from reasoning. Was it because you had many premises (quantity) or because the inference came easily (quality)? Which dimension do you rely on more, and how might that blind you to weaknesses in the other?
?
If your preparedness for certain knowledge is unregulated, what does that mean for your responsibility to seek truth? Are you willing to practice more without expecting equal results, trusting that Allah accounts for your capacity? What habit will you commit to starting today?

Love of One's Own Conclusions
Al-Razi explains how love for one's own conclusions causes unregulated variation in beliefs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how love for one's own conclusions can blind a person to truth and cause unregulated variation in beliefs.

Core Idea
Love of one's own reasoning can blind and deafen, preventing correction.

Al-Razi presents a second mechanism within the third external cause: the emotional attachment a thinker develops to his own conclusions. This love can become so strong that it obstructs objective evaluation and even blocks understanding of contrary evidence. He illustrates with the proverb that love blinds and deafens.

Guided Path
1
Identify the cause: love for one's own conclusion
Read the passage and note that the thinker loves his own conclusion because it is his own discovery.
2
Understand how love prevents reflection
Al-Razi states that this love can prevent the thinker from reflecting on the soundness of his belief.
This is a psychological obstacle.
3
Recognize the blinding effect
The love can become so strong that even hearing a contrary argument, the thinker cannot understand it.
The proverb 'love blinds and deafens' is quoted.
4
Grasp the consequence: unregulated variation
Since love varies unregulatedly, beliefs and morals also vary unregulatedly.
This reinforces the conclusion about all six causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السبب الثالث من الأسباب الخارجية : أن الإنسان إذا مارس صناعة النظر والاستدلال . فكل من كانت ممارسته لهذه الصناعة أكثر ، كان وقوفه على الحق أسهل . ثم إن التفاوت يقع في هذا الباب بحسب الكم . فإن بعضهم يكون أكثر استحضارا للمقدمات . وأما بحسب الكيف فأن يكون انفعال بعضهم من المقدمات الحاضرة ، إلى النتائج المستحضرة : أسهل . ولما كان التفاوت في هذين البابين ، مما يختلف بالأشد والأضعف ، والأقل والأكثر اختلافا غير مضبوط . لا جرم كان اختلاف الناس في الاستعداد لقبول المعارف اليقينية ، والأخلاق الفاضلة ، اختلافا غير مضبوط . واعلم : أن هذا السبب يوجب اختلاف الناس في العقائد من وجه آخر . وذلك لأن صاحب النظر والاستدلال ، قد ينتج فكره أن القول الفلاني حق صحيح . ولأجل أن ذلك كان من مستنبطات خاطره ، ومن نتائج فكره عظم حبه لذلك . وقد يبلغ ذلك الحب إلى حيث يمنعه عن التأمل في صحته وفساده . وقد تبلغ قوة تلك المحبة إلى أنه لو سمع حجة دالة على فساد تلك الحالة ، فإنه لا يفهمها ، ولا يقف على كيفيتها . كما قيل : وحبك الشيء يعمي ويصم ، ولما كان تفاوت الناس في مراتب هذه المحبة ، غير مضبوطة . لا جرم كان تفاوت الناس في العقائد والأخلاق ، بناء على هذا السبب : تفاوتا غير مضبوط . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن كل واحد من هذه الأسباب السنة : جنس تحته أنواع غير محصورة . وإن اختلافها يوجب اختلاف الناس في العقائد ، وفي الأفعال
The third of the external causes: that when a person practices the craft of contemplation and inference, then the more his practice of this craft, the easier his grasp of truth becomes. Then variation occurs in this regard according to quantity: some of them have a greater readiness to bring premises to mind. As for variation according to quality, it is that some of them are more easily affected by the present premises toward the results they have in mind. And since variation in these two aspects differs in intensity and weakness, in fewness and abundance, in an unregulated manner, inevitably the variation among people in their preparedness to accept certain knowledge and virtuous morals is unregulated. Know that this cause necessitates variation among people in beliefs from another angle. That is because the person of contemplation and inference may produce from his thought that such and such a statement is true and correct. And because it is from the conclusions of his mind and the results of his thought, his love for it becomes great. This love may reach a point where it prevents him from reflecting on its soundness or unsoundness. The strength of that love may even reach such a degree that if he hears an argument proving the unsoundness of that state, he does not understand it nor grasp its nature. As it is said: 'Your love of something blinds and deafens.' And since variation among people in the ranks of this love is unregulated, inevitably variation among people in beliefs and morals based on this cause is unregulated. So it is established from what we have mentioned that each of these six causes is a genus under which are uncountable species, and their variation necessitates variation among people in beliefs and actions.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage explains the third external cause: practice in the art of reasoning. Al-Razi first notes that more practice makes grasping truth easier. However, people differ both in quantity (how many premises they can bring to mind) and in quality (how easily they move from premises to conclusions). Since these differences are unregulated, people's preparedness for certain knowledge and virtue varies without bound. Then he adds another aspect: a thinker may fall in love with his own conclusions. This love can become so strong that it prevents him from examining the truth of his belief, even blinding him to contrary evidence. He quotes the proverb 'love of something blinds and deafens.' This love also varies unregulatedly. Finally, he concludes the entire discussion of external causes (though only the second and third are presented in this window) by stating that each of the six causes is a genus with uncountable species, and their variation causes variation in beliefs and actions.

Key Terms
السبب الثالث
صناعة النظر والاستدلال
حب الشيء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that loving one's own ideas is harmless or even beneficial for commitment.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that excessive love can obstruct objective evaluation and even block understanding of contrary evidence, leading to entrenchment in error.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, how does a person's love for his own conclusions affect his ability to evaluate them?
A) It helps him see the truth more clearly.
B) It can prevent him from reflecting on their soundness.
C) It has no effect on his judgment.
D) It makes him seek out contrary evidence.

Key Takeaway
Love for one's own conclusions can blind a person to truth, leading to unregulated variation in beliefs and morals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold strongly, especially one you arrived at through your own reasoning. Can you honestly say you would welcome evidence that proves it wrong? Or does the thought of being mistaken disturb you? Are you willing to pray for the ability to see your own blind spots?
?
Al-Razi says this love can become so strong that you do not even understand an argument against your view. Is there a topic where you find yourself unable to comprehend the other side's reasoning? Could it be that your attachment to your conclusion has 'deafened' you? Will you ask someone you trust to point out where you might be biased?
?
Your love for your own conclusions is 'unregulated'—it can grow without you noticing. When was the last time you genuinely doubted a conclusion you were proud of? If you cannot recall, consider that your love may have already blinded you. Will you commit to regularly examining your own beliefs with the same critical eye you use for others'?

The Second Premise: Condition for Action
Razi states the second premise: an agent acts only when he believes the act is better for him.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second premise of Razi's theory of action: that every voluntary action depends on the agent's belief that the act is better for them than its omission.

Core Idea
Action requires belief that the act is beneficial.

Razi sets out a foundational premise for his analysis of human action. He argues that no animal (including humans) undertakes an act unless they believe it is somehow better for them than not doing it. This may seem simple, but it carries profound implications for understanding free choice (ikhtiyar) and the nature of goodness in moral reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice the key terms and how they are used.
The passage is short but dense; each term matters.
2
Identify the key claim
Find the conditional statement: 'if belief, then action; if no belief, then refrain.' This is the core of the premise.
Also note that the agent is called fa‘il because of this seeking behavior.
3
Reflect on the meaning of 'good'
Razi says the 'good' (khayr) is according to the agent's belief and imagination, not an objective standard. Think about how this differs from moral goodness.
This is a common point of misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمقدمة الثانية في بيان كيفية صدور الأفعال عن الحيوانات : اعلم أن كل حيوان يفعل فعلا . فهو إنما يفعل ذلك الفعل . إذا اعتقد أن فعله خير له من تركه . فإن لم يحصل هذا الاعتقاد ، امتنع إقدامه عليه . ولأجل هذا المعنى فإنه يسمى هذا المؤثر فاعلا. لأن المختار هو الذي يكون طالبا . ولا يكون خيرا إلا بحسب اعتقاده وتخيله
The second premise concerning the explanation of how actions issue from animals: Know that every animal that performs an action only does that action if it believes that its action is better for it than its omission. If this belief is not attained, it refrains from undertaking it. Because of this meaning, this agent is called a 'doer' (fa‘il). For the free chooser (mukhtar) is one who seeks, and something is only good according to his belief and imagination.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage presents the second premise in a series, concerning the mechanism of action. Al-Razi asserts that any animal (including humans) only acts when it holds a belief that the action is better for it than not doing it. Without that belief, the action is avoided. This is why the agent is called 'doer'—because choice implies seeking. The goodness of an act is relative to the agent's belief and imagination, not absolute. Thus, action is grounded in a subjective perception of benefit.

Correction: Razi means perceived personal benefit or advantage, not ethical goodness. The agent's belief determines what is 'good' for them.

Key Takeaway
Action is not possible without a belief that the act is better, and 'good' is relative to the agent's perception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Does your life show that you believe obedience to Allah is better than disobedience, or does your postponement of prayer and repentance reveal the opposite? Commit now to align your belief with what you know is truly good.

The Third Premise: Pleasure as Ultimate End
Razi states the third premise: pleasure desired in itself, pain avoided, all else instrumental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's third premise: pleasure and pain are the only intrinsic ends of action, and why this avoids infinite regress of purposes.

Core Idea
Pleasure is sought for itself; all else is a means to it.

Al-Razi now presents the third premise, which identifies the ultimate ends of all actions. He distinguishes between intrinsic desires for pleasure and avoidance of pain, and instrumental desires for means. This distinction is crucial for understanding the nature of human action and the necessity of ultimate ends in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how al-Razi defines the categories of desire.
Pay attention to the terms 'bi al-dhat' (in itself) and 'bi al-gharad' (for a purpose).
2
Identify the two intrinsic ends
Find where al-Razi states what is desired in itself and what is fled from in itself.
These are pleasure (and delight) and pain (and grief).
3
Understand the instrumental category
Note that everything else is desired only as a means to these two intrinsic ends.
This is the key to avoiding infinite regress.
4
Examine the two types of actions
Distinguish between actions that themselves achieve pleasure/remove pain, and actions that are means to those ends.
In the first case, the end is the action itself; in the second, the end is different from the action.
5
Reflect on the regress argument
Understand why al-Razi claims that positing intrinsic ends avoids infinite regress or circularity.
Without intrinsic ends, every action would require a further end, leading to an infinite chain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمقدمة الثالثة : إن المطلوب بالذات لكل حيوان ، هو اللذة والسرور . والمهروب عنه بالذات هو الألم والغم. وكل ماسوى هذين القسمين ، فهو مطلوب بالغرض، لا بالذات فالفعل الصادر عن الإنسان . إن كان هو تحصيل اللذة ، أو إزالة الألم. فهذا الشيء مطلوب. فتكون غايته وغرضه هو عين ذاته. وإن كان الفعل الصادر عن الإنسان يكون وسيلة إلى أحد هذين الأمرين ، كان مطلوبا بالغرض والتبع. وكان غرضه وغايته ، أمرا مغايرا له . فثبت بهذا : أنه لا يجوز أن يقال في كل فعل : إنه إنما فعله بشيء آخر . وإلا لزم إما التسلسل ، وإما الدور
The third premise: That which is desired in itself (al-matlab bi al-dhat) for every animal is pleasure and delight, and that which is fled from in itself is pain and grief. Everything other than these two categories is desired for a purpose (bi al-gharad), not in itself. So the action issuing from a human: if it is the attainment of pleasure or the removal of pain, then this thing is desired, so its end and purpose is identical to its essence. And if the action issuing from a human is a means to one of these two matters, it is desired for a purpose and as a subsidiary, and its purpose and end is something other than it. Thus it is established by this that it is not permissible to say of every action that it is done for the sake of something else, otherwise either infinite regress (tasalsul) or circularity (dawr) would follow.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage presents the third premise: the ultimate ends of all animal (including human) actions are pleasure (sought in itself) and pain (avoided in itself). Everything else is desired only as a means to these ends. Al-Razi distinguishes between actions that are themselves pleasure or pain removal (where end = action itself) and actions that are means to these ends (where the end is distinct). He then draws a logical conclusion: if every action were done for the sake of something else, we would have infinite regress or circularity. Therefore, there must be intrinsic ends—pleasure and pain—which serve as ultimate goals. This argument underlies hedonistic psychology in the context of kalam.

المطلوب بالغرض
التسلسل
الدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The pleasure mentioned is only physical pleasure.
Correction: In the context of kalam, 'pleasure' encompasses any delight, including spiritual and intellectual.

Checkpoint
What does al-Razi claim would follow if every action were done for the sake of something else?
A) All actions would be evil.
B) Infinite regress or circularity would result.
C) Actions would be impossible.
D) No action would be rational.

Key Takeaway
The third premise establishes pleasure and pain as the only intrinsic ends, preventing infinite regress of purposes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider your own daily actions—eating, working, studying, praying. According to Razi, every action you do is either directly pleasure/pain avoidance or a means to them. Is there any action you perform that you truly believe is neither? If you find that all your actions ultimately aim at your own pleasure or comfort, how does that align with your Islamic commitment to worship Allah alone? Does the thought that your deepest motives might be self-serving disturb you? What would it take for you to genuinely seek Allah's pleasure as an end in itself, not just as a means to your own happiness?
?
Razi argues that without an intrinsic end, we face infinite regress. Think about your life's purpose. If you were to list the reasons for your actions, do you ever reach a final answer, or do you keep asking 'why' indefinitely? If you are honest, do you actually live as though pleasure and pain relief are your ultimate ends? If not, what is your true ultimate end? Are you willing to let this passage expose where your real loyalties lie?
?
The premise states that all animals, including humans, desire pleasure and flee pain. But Islam commands actions that may involve pain and sacrifice. How do you reconcile your natural aversion to pain with the call to struggle in the path of Allah? Does this passage challenge you to examine whether your obedience to Allah is truly for His sake or ultimately for the pleasure or relief it brings you? If it is the latter, is that acceptable? Why or why not?

The Sixth Proof: Defining Contradictory Propositions
Author defines contradictory propositions as a preliminary for the sixth proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of contradictory propositions as a necessary preliminary for the sixth proof.

Core Idea
Contradictory propositions are essentially opposed in truth value.

The author begins the sixth proof by stressing that it cannot be established without first understanding what contradictory propositions are. He defines them as two propositions that, by their very essence, require one to be true and the other false. This definition is the logical foundation for the proof that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the broken ending in the OCR—the missing word is likely 'false'.
The text ends abruptly; use the translation to infer the complete meaning.
2
Identify the key terms
Find and understand the terms: برهان (proof), مقدمة (preliminary), متناقضين (contradictories), قضيتان (two propositions), صادقة (true), and the implied كاذبة (false).
These terms are foundational for kalam and logic.
3
Understand the definition's emphasis
Note that the author says 'لذاتهما' (by their very selves). This emphasis shows that the necessity for one to be true and the other false is intrinsic, not accidental.
Contrast with ordinary opposites that might be both false or both true under different conditions.
4
Connect to the sixth proof
Ask yourself why the author needs this definition before presenting the proof. It likely involves the impossibility of both contradictories being true or false simultaneously.
This definition is a logical building block.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان السادس

اعلم : أن هذا البرهان لا يمكن تقريره ، إلا بعد تقديم مقدمة في حقيقة المتناقضين .

فنقول :

إنهما القضيتان اللتان يجب لذاتهما أن تكون إحداهما صادقة ، والأخرى
The sixth proof. Know that this proof cannot be established except after presenting a preliminary on the reality of contradictories. So we say: They are the two propositions which, by virtue of themselves, it is necessary that one of them be true and the other [false].
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage introduces the sixth proof (al-burhān al-sādis) and provides a necessary preliminary definition. The author states that the proof cannot be established without first clarifying the nature of contradictories (mutanāqiḍayn). He then defines them as two propositions (qaḍiyyatān) that, by their very essence (li-dhātayhimā), require that one be true (ṣādiqa) and the other false (kādhiba). This definition is foundational for the logical argument that follows, likely dealing with the impossibility of simultaneous truth or falsehood in statements about divine attributes or actions. The definition here is a standard kalam and logic definition of contradiction (tanāquḍ).

Key Terms

قضيتان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that contradictory propositions are simply opposite statements, but the author specifies that the necessity of one being true and the other false is essential, not accidental.
Correction: The author emphasizes 'لذاتهما' (by their essence), meaning the truth-value opposition is intrinsic to the propositions themselves, not contingent on external factors.

Checkpoint

B) Two propositions of which it is necessary, by their very essence, that one is true and the other false.
C) Two propositions that cannot both be false.

Key Takeaway
Contradictory propositions are two propositions that, by their very nature, necessitate that one is true and the other false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Definition of Contradictories and Truth Across Modalities
Defines contradictories and classifies truth/falsity across three modalities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of contradictories as two propositions that necessarily differ in truth value, and how truth and falsity are determined across the modalities of necessary, impossible, and possible (past/present).

Core Idea
Contradictories are defined by necessary opposition; truth and falsity are real attributes that vary with modality.

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah begins by establishing a foundational logical concept: the nature of contradictories. Before the author can argue about God's knowledge of future events, he must clarify what it means for two propositions to be contradictory. He then extends this to a classification of truth and falsity across different modalities—necessary, impossible, and possible—showing that the truth-value of a proposition is not arbitrary but determined by the nature of its subject and predicate.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of contradictories
Examine how the author defines 'contradictories' as two propositions that necessarily require one true and one false. Note the conditions: same subject, predicate, and time.
The word 'necessarily' (بالضرورة) is key.
2
Understand the classification of truth/falsity
The author then explains how truth and falsity are understood across three modalities: the necessary, the impossible, and the possible (past/present). For each, he gives a rule.
Notice that for the possible past/present, truth is determined by actual occurrence.
3
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself why the author begins with this logical groundwork. How does it set up the later discussion about future contingents?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان السادس
اعلم: أن هذا البرهان لا يمكن تقريره، إلا بعد تقديم مقدمة في حقيقة المتناقضين.
فنقول:
إنهما القضيتان اللتان يجب لذاتهما أن تكون إحداهما صادقة، والأخرى كاذبة. بعينه، أو بغير عينه. وتقريره: إنا إذا عينا الموضوع أمراً واحداً في نفسه، وعينا المحمول أمراً واحداً في نفسه، وعينا الوقت وقتاً واحداً في نفسه. فإذا قلنا: ذلك المحمول، ثبت لذلك الموضوع، في ذلك الوقت. ثم قلنا: ذلك المحمول، ما ثبت لذلك الموضوع، في ذلك الوقت. والعقل قاطع بأنه لا بد في هذا الإيجاب، وهذا السلب، أن يكون أحدهما صادقاً، والآخر كاذباً. ثم قالوا: الصادق والكاذب معنيان في الواجب وفي الممتنع وفي الممكن الماضي والحاضر. أما في الواجب فكل ما هو في جانب الثبوت، فهو الصادق. وكل ما هو في جانب السلب، فهو الكاذب. وأما في الممتنع، فبالضد. وأما في الممكن الماضي والحاضر، فالذي لم يقع هو الكذب في نفسه, والذي وقع في نفسه، فهو الصادق. وأما الممكن المستقبل. فقال بعضهم: الصادق والكاذب غير متعينين، بل أحدهما صادق، والآخر كاذب على التعيين. وعندي: أنه يجب أن يكون الصادق معيناً، والكاذب متعيناً في نفس الأمر، في الممكن المستقبل. والدليل عليه: أن الصدقية والكذبية صفتان حقيقيتان حاصلتان في نفس الأمر. فلا بد لكل واحد منهما من محل موجود في الخارج. وكل موجود في الخارج فهو في نفسه معين لأن كل ما كان موجوداً في نفسه، فهو معين في نفسه، والإيهام لا يحصل إلا في الأذهان. بمعنى أنه لا يعلم أن الموصوف بالصدقية أهو هذه القضية، أم تلك الأخرى؟ فأما أن يكون الذي هو الموجود في الأعيان بينهما في نفسه غير معين بحسب وجوده فهو محال. ولما ثبت أن الصدقية والكذبية صفتان موجودتان، وثبت أن الصفة الموجودة ليست محلاً معيناً في نفس الأمر، يثبت أن
The Sixth Proof. Know that this proof cannot be established except after presenting a preface on the reality of contradictories. So we say: They are two propositions which by themselves necessarily require that one is true and the other false. Identically, or non-identically. And its explanation: If we specify the subject as one thing in itself, and we specify the predicate as one thing in itself, and we specify the time as one time in itself. Then we say: That predicate is established for that subject at that time. Then we say: That predicate is not established for that subject at that time. And reason is decisive that in this affirmation and this negation, one must be true and the other false. Then they said: Truth and falsity are two meanings in the necessary, the impossible, and the possible past and present. As for the necessary, whatever is on the side of affirmation is truth, and whatever is on the side of negation is falsity. As for the impossible, the opposite. As for the possible past and present, that which did not occur is falsity in itself, and that which occurred in itself is truth. As for the possible future, some said: truth and falsity are not determinate, but rather one of them is true and the other false determinately. And in my view: it is necessary that the true be determinate and the false determinate in actual fact, in the possible future. The evidence for it: that truth-ness and falsity-ness are two real attributes subsisting in actual fact. So each of them must have a locus that exists externally. And everything that exists externally is in itself determinate, because everything that exists in itself is determinate in itself, and ambiguity only occurs in minds. Meaning that it is not known whether the attribute of truth belongs to this proposition or that other one. But as for that which exists in external reality between them being in itself indeterminate according to its existence, it is impossible. And since it is established that truth-ness and falsity-ness are two existing attributes, and it is established that an existing attribute is not an indeterminate locus in actual fact, it is established that
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage introduces a sixth proof by first defining contradictories (al-mutanāqidayn) as two propositions that necessarily require one truth and one falsity under the same conditions. The author explains that after specifying subject, predicate, and time, affirmation and negation are contradictory. Then, the author classifies truth and falsity across modalities: for necessary propositions, truth is affirmation and falsity negation; for the impossible, the opposite; for possible past/present, the actual occurrence determines truth; for possible future, some say truth and falsity are not determinate (we don't know which), but the author argues they are determinate in actual fact. The argument is that truth-ness (ṣidqīya) and falsity-ness (kidhbīya) are real attributes existing in actual fact, requiring a determinate locus. Since external existence implies determinacy, the future contingent proposition must be determinate in truth-value, even if unknown to us. The passage ends with the beginning of a conclusion, likely completing the proof that God's knowledge of future contingents is certain.

واجب

ممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Truth and falsity are purely subjective judgments or matters of opinion.
Correction: The passage argues that truth and falsity are real attributes (معنيان) that vary based on modality and actual state of affairs, not subjective judgments.

Contradictories are logically opposed propositions; truth and falsity are real attributes determined by modality and actuality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Determinate Truth of Future Contingents
Argues that future contingent propositions have determinate truth values

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's argument that future contingent propositions have determinate truth values in actual fact, and the distinction between objective determinacy and subjective uncertainty.

Core Idea
Future contingent truth and falsity are real, determinate attributes, even if we do not know which applies.

The author now enters a critical debate: are future contingent propositions (e.g., 'Zayd will travel tomorrow') true or false in a determinate sense? Some scholars held that such propositions are not determinately true or false until the event occurs. The author argues otherwise, grounding his position in the nature of truth and falsity as real attributes requiring a determinate subject. This lesson examines his argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on future contingents
Pay attention to the two opposing views: some say truth and falsity are not determinate (غير متعينين), while the author affirms they are determinate (متعينان) in actual fact.
The author says 'وعندي' (in my view), signaling his own position.
2
Follow the logical argument
The author provides evidence: truth-ness and falsity-ness are real attributes; each requires a locus (محل) that exists externally; everything external exists determinately; therefore, the truth-value must be determinate.
Note the premise that attributes must inhere in something.
3
Understand the distinction between objective and subjective
The author distinguishes between objective determinacy (فِي نَفْسِ الأَمْرِ) and subjective ambiguity (الإيهام في الأذهان). We may not know which is true, but that does not mean the truth-value is indeterminate.
This distinction is crucial for the broader theological argument about God's knowledge.

البرهان السادس
اعلم: أن هذا البرهان لا يمكن تقريره، إلا بعد تقديم مقدمة في حقيقة المتناقضين.
فنقول:
إنهما القضيتان اللتان يجب لذاتهما أن تكون إحداهما صادقة، والأخرى كاذبة. بعينه، أو بغير عينه. وتقريره: إنا إذا عينا الموضوع أمراً واحداً في نفسه، وعينا المحمول أمراً واحداً في نفسه، وعينا الوقت وقتاً واحداً في نفسه. فإذا قلنا: ذلك المحمول، ثبت لذلك الموضوع، في ذلك الوقت. ثم قلنا: ذلك المحمول، ما ثبت لذلك الموضوع، في ذلك الوقت. والعقل قاطع بأنه لا بد في هذا الإيجاب، وهذا السلب، أن يكون أحدهما صادقاً، والآخر كاذباً. ثم قالوا: الصادق والكاذب معنيان في الواجب وفي الممتنع وفي الممكن الماضي والحاضر. أما في الواجب فكل ما هو في جانب الثبوت، فهو الصادق. وكل ما هو في جانب السلب، فهو الكاذب. وأما في الممتنع، فبالضد. وأما في الممكن الماضي والحاضر، فالذي لم يقع هو الكذب في نفسه, والذي وقع في نفسه، فهو الصادق. وأما الممكن المستقبل. فقال بعضهم: الصادق والكاذب غير متعينين، بل أحدهما صادق، والآخر كاذب على التعيين. وعندي: أنه يجب أن يكون الصادق معيناً، والكاذب متعيناً في نفس الأمر، في الممكن المستقبل. والدليل عليه: أن الصدقية والكذبية صفتان حقيقيتان حاصلتان في نفس الأمر. فلا بد لكل واحد منهما من محل موجود في الخارج. وكل موجود في الخارج فهو في نفسه معين لأن كل ما كان موجوداً في نفسه، فهو معين في نفسه، والإيهام لا يحصل إلا في الأذهان. بمعنى أنه لا يعلم أن الموصوف بالصدقية أهو هذه القضية، أم تلك الأخرى؟ فأما أن يكون الذي هو الموجود في الأعيان بينهما في نفسه غير معين بحسب وجوده فهو محال. ولما ثبت أن الصدقية والكذبية صفتان موجودتان، وثبت أن الصفة الموجودة ليست محلاً معيناً في نفس الأمر، يثبت أن
The Sixth Proof. Know that this proof cannot be established except after presenting a preface on the reality of contradictories. So we say: They are two propositions which by themselves necessarily require that one is true and the other false. Identically, or non-identically. And its explanation: If we specify the subject as one thing in itself, and we specify the predicate as one thing in itself, and we specify the time as one time in itself. Then we say: That predicate is established for that subject at that time. Then we say: That predicate is not established for that subject at that time. And reason is decisive that in this affirmation and this negation, one must be true and the other false. Then they said: Truth and falsity are two meanings in the necessary, the impossible, and the possible past and present. As for the necessary, whatever is on the side of affirmation is truth, and whatever is on the side of negation is falsity. As for the impossible, the opposite. As for the possible past and present, that which did not occur is falsity in itself, and that which occurred in itself is truth. As for the possible future, some said: truth and falsity are not determinate, but rather one of them is true and the other false determinately. And in my view: it is necessary that the true be determinate and the false determinate in actual fact, in the possible future. The evidence for it: that truth-ness and falsity-ness are two real attributes subsisting in actual fact. So each of them must have a locus that exists externally. And everything that exists externally is in itself determinate, because everything that exists in itself is determinate in itself, and ambiguity only occurs in minds. Meaning that it is not known whether the attribute of truth belongs to this proposition or that other one. But as for that which exists in external reality between them being in itself indeterminate according to its existence, it is impossible. And since it is established that truth-ness and falsity-ness are two existing attributes, and it is established that an existing attribute is not an indeterminate locus in actual fact, it is established that
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage introduces a sixth proof by first defining contradictories (al-mutanāqidayn) as two propositions that necessarily require one truth and one falsity under the same conditions. The author explains that after specifying subject, predicate, and time, affirmation and negation are contradictory. Then, the author classifies truth and falsity across modalities: for necessary propositions, truth is affirmation and falsity negation; for the impossible, the opposite; for possible past/present, the actual occurrence determines truth; for possible future, some say truth and falsity are not determinate (we don't know which), but the author argues they are determinate in actual fact. The argument is that truth-ness (ṣidqīya) and falsity-ness (kidhbīya) are real attributes existing in actual fact, requiring a determinate locus. Since external existence implies determinacy, the future contingent proposition must be determinate in truth-value, even if unknown to us. The passage ends with the beginning of a conclusion, likely completing the proof that God's knowledge of future contingents is certain.

Truth and Falsehood: Real Qualities or Mental Considerations?
Objection that truth/falsehood are merely mental, answer denies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand an objection against the reality of truth and falsehood and the author's initial denial.

Core Idea
Truth and falsehood are real, not just mental labels.

This passage presents a crucial objection to the previous argument that truth and falsehood are real qualities. The objector suggests they could be mere mental considerations with no external existence. The author begins his response by flatly denying this, setting up a defense of the reality of these qualities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection

The objector uses the phrase 'أمر اعتباري' (mental consideration).
2
Understand the author's denial
Notice how the author responds: 'لا نسلم' (we do not concede). This is a direct rejection of the objection's premise.
3
Reflect on the stakes
Consider why this matters: if truth and falsehood are not real qualities, the entire argument about predestination and determinate loci may break down.

This exchange is crucial as it addresses a foundational epistemological and metaphysical premise.

Understanding the Affirmative Nature of the Last Three Proofs
A brief remark classifying the last three proofs as affirmative

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what 'ijab' (affirmation) means in the context of kalam proofs and how these three proofs differ from refutative arguments.

Core Idea
Affirmation, not refutation: the last three proofs establish a positive claim.

This lesson unpacks a brief but important remark about the final three proofs: they are affirmative in nature. While many theological arguments focus on refuting falsehoods, these proofs take a positive approach, directly establishing a doctrine. Understanding this distinction helps you recognize different argumentative strategies in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation. Notice the key term 'إيجاب'.
This is a very short remark, but it carries significant meaning.
2
Define 'ijab'
Understand that 'ijab' means affirmation or positive assertion. In kalam, proofs are often categorized as affirmative (إيجاب) or negative (سلب).
Affirmative proofs aim to establish what is true, not just disprove what is false.
3
Contrast with refutative proofs
Consider how affirmative proofs differ from refutative ones. Refutative proofs deny an opposing view, while affirmative proofs directly support a doctrine.
The author's note suggests a shift in strategy in the final proofs.
4
Reflect on the context
Think about why the author specifies 'last three'. What might the prior proofs have been? The remark implies a grouping based on argumentative style.
This lesson does not cover the content of those proofs, only their affirmative character.

Why a Servant Cannot Recreate His Act: The Fundamental Principle
Sanusi establishes the impossibility of recreating what has perished.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial premise that what has ceased to exist cannot be brought back, and why this applies to the servant's act.

Core Idea
What has perished cannot return; recreation is not a return of the same.

Before diving into the complex proof, the author establishes a foundational principle: something that has completely ceased to exist cannot be recreated. This sets the stage for the argument that the servant's act, once gone, cannot be reproduced by him.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice how the author defines what it means for something to cease to exist.
The key term is 'ʿadam' (non-existence).
2
Identify the premise
Identify the claim that what has perished cannot be recreated because nothing remains of it.
This is the basis for the rest of the argument.
3
Understand the implication
Consider why this premise is crucial: it means that any attempt at recreation would produce something new, not the original.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يمتنع في حق الله تعالى ، أو في حق العبد . وبيانه : أن الذي عدم فقد فني ولم يبق منه لا ذاته ولا حكم من أحكامه . فإذا حصل بعد ذلك شيء آخر ، فهو مغاير للأول ، وامتنع أن يكون عين ذلك الذي عدم . سلمنا : أن الإعادة في الجملة جائزة . لكن لم قلتم : إن القادر على الابتداء ، يجب أن يكون قادراً على الإعادة ؟ وتقريره : إن الابتداء يمتاز عن الإعادة بأمر من الأمور . فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن ما به حصل الامتياز يكون شرطاً في أحد الطرفين أو مانعا في الطرف الآخر ؟ ثم نقول : الفرق بين الإعادة وبين الابتداء . هو أنه لو صحت الإعادة من العبد ، لكان إما أن يقدر على إعادته بتلك القدرة ، التي بها أوجد ذلك الفعل في الابتداء ، أو بغير تلك القدرة . والقسمان باطلان . أما أنه [ إعادة (١) ] ذلك الفعل بعين القدرة التي بها أوجده في الابتداء . فذلك لأن القدرة لا بد وأن تتعلق في كل وقت بإيجاد فرد من أفراد ذلك النوع . فلو تعلقت أيضا في وقت من الأوقات بإعادة ما عدم ، لكان قد تعلقت في الوقت الواحد ، في المحل الواحد [ من الجنس الواحد (٢) ] بأكثر من مقدور واحد ولو جاز [ ذلك (٣) ] لما كان عدد أولى من عدد . فيلزم جواز (4) تعلقها بما لا نهاية له . وذلك محال . لأنه يلزم أن لا يبقى التفاوت بين القادر وبين الأقدر . فثبت : أن إعادة الفعل بعين القدرة التي حصل بها الإيجاد في الابتداء : محال . وأما أنه لا يمكن إعادته بقدرة أخرى. لأنه لو جاز أن تتعلق قدرتان بإيجاد مقدور واحد ، لجاز قيام كل واحد منهما بقادر آخر . وذلك يفضي إلى حصول مقدور ، بين قادرين. وهو محال . فثبت : أن العبد لو قدر على إعادة فعل نفسه ، لقدر عليه . إما بعين تلك القدرة وإما بقدرة أخرى وثبت أن كل واحد منهما محال فلزم القول : بأن العبد لا يقدر على الاعادة . وهذا العذر غير موجود في القدرة على إيجاد الفعل ابتداء . فثبت : أنه لا يلزم من قدرة العبد على الإيجاد ابتداء ، قدرته على إعادة ذلك الفعل . فهذا هو تقرير هذا الفرق .
It is impossible with respect to God most high, or with respect to the servant. Its explanation: that which has ceased to exist has perished and nothing remains of it, neither its essence nor any of its rulings. So if after that something else occurs, it is different from the first, and it is impossible that it be the very thing that ceased to exist. We concede that recreation (al-iʿāda) in general is permissible. But why do you say: The one who is capable of origination (al-ibtidāʾ) must be capable of recreation? Its exposition: Origination is distinguished from recreation by some matter. So why is it not permissible to say: That by which the distinction occurs is a condition in one of the two sides or an impediment in the other side? Then we say: The difference between recreation and origination is that if recreation by the servant were valid, then either he would be capable of recreating it by that very power (al-qudra) by which he brought that act into existence originally, or by another power. And both divisions are false. As for [recreating] that act by the very power by which he brought it into existence originally, that is because the power must necessarily be related at every time to bringing into existence an individual of the individuals of that type. So if it were also related at a time to recreating what has ceased to exist, it would have been related in one time, in one locus [of one type] to more than one object of power. And if that were permissible, no number would be more appropriate than another. Then it would necessitate the permissibility of its relation to what is infinite. And that is impossible, because it would necessitate that no distinction remain between the capable and the more capable. So it is established that recreating the act by the very power by which origination occurred is impossible. As for that it cannot be recreated by another power, because if it were permissible for two powers to be related to bringing into existence one object of power, it would be permissible for each one of them to subsist in another capable being. And that leads to an object of power occurring between two capable beings. And that is impossible. So it is established that if the servant were able to recreate his own act, he would be able to do so either by that very power or by another power, and it is established that each one of them is impossible. So it necessitates the statement that the servant is not able to recreate. And this excuse does not exist regarding the power to bring the act into existence initially. So it is established that the servant's ability to bring into existence initially does not necessitate his ability to recreate that act. This is the exposition of this distinction.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents a kalam argument proving that a servant (al-ʿabd) cannot recreate an act that he previously performed, even though he was able to originate it. The argument begins by establishing the impossibility of recreating something that has ceased to exist, since the original thing has perished entirely. Then it concedes that recreation in general is possible (sallamnā anna al-iʿāda fī al-jumla jāʾiza), but challenges the claim that anyone capable of origination (al-ibtidāʾ) must also be capable of recreation. The reason given is that origination is distinguished from recreation by some property; that property could be a condition for one or an impediment for the other. The core proof is then given: if the servant were to recreate his act, it would have to be either by the same power (al-qudra) that originated it, or by a different power. Both possibilities are shown to be impossible. First, if by the same power, that power would then be related to two objects of power at the same time and in the same subject, which would lead to the absurdity of relating to an infinite number of objects and erasing the distinction between the powerful and the more powerful. Second, if by a different power, this would imply two powers relating to one object, which would allow two separate agents to share an act, also absurd. Thus the servant cannot recreate. The conclusion is that the ability to originate does not imply ability to recreate; the distinction is sound.

Key Terms
إعادة (iʿāda)
عدم (ʿadam)
فناء (fanāʾ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) It continues to exist in the mind.
D) It can be recreated by God only.

Key Takeaway
What has perished cannot be brought back as the same entity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Are you more aware that every moment is a new creation from Allah, and that you are responsible for the quality of each new act, not for recreating old ones?

The Two Possibilities: Same Power or Different Power
Sanusi presents the two possibilities and refutes the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author presents two possibilities for recreating an act and why the first one (same power) is impossible.

Core Idea
Recreation must be by the same or a different power; the same power leads to absurdity.

The author now presents a structured proof. He concedes that recreation is possible in general but challenges the notion that ability to originate implies ability to recreate. He then argues that if the servant were to recreate his act, it would have to be by the same power as before or another power. The first option is examined and shown impossible due to leading to multiple objects and infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how the author sets up the two divisions.
The key phrase is 'إما بتلك القدرة أو بغير تلك القدرة'.
2
Understand the first division
Understand the first possibility: recreating by the same power. The author argues that if the same power were used, it would have to be related to two objects at once, which is impossible.
This leads to an infinite regress argument.
3
Consider the implication
Consider why this refutes the idea that the servant can recreate his act using the same power.

يمتنع في حق الله تعالى ، أو في حق العبد . وبيانه : أن الذي عدم فقد فني ولم يبق منه لا ذاته ولا حكم من أحكامه . فإذا حصل بعد ذلك شيء آخر ، فهو مغاير للأول ، وامتنع أن يكون عين ذلك الذي عدم . سلمنا : أن الإعادة في الجملة جائزة . لكن لم قلتم : إن القادر على الابتداء ، يجب أن يكون قادراً على الإعادة ؟ وتقريره : إن الابتداء يمتاز عن الإعادة بأمر من الأمور . فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن ما به حصل الامتياز يكون شرطاً في أحد الطرفين أو مانعا في الطرف الآخر ؟ ثم نقول : الفرق بين الإعادة وبين الابتداء . هو أنه لو صحت الإعادة من العبد ، لكان إما أن يقدر على إعادته بتلك القدرة ، التي بها أوجد ذلك الفعل في الابتداء ، أو بغير تلك القدرة . والقسمان باطلان . أما أنه [ إعادة (١) ] ذلك الفعل بعين القدرة التي بها أوجده في الابتداء . فذلك لأن القدرة لا بد وأن تتعلق في كل وقت بإيجاد فرد من أفراد ذلك النوع . فلو تعلقت أيضا في وقت من الأوقات بإعادة ما عدم ، لكان قد تعلقت في الوقت الواحد ، في المحل الواحد [ من الجنس الواحد (٢) ] بأكثر من مقدور واحد ولو جاز [ ذلك (٣) ] لما كان عدد أولى من عدد . فيلزم جواز (4) تعلقها بما لا نهاية له . وذلك محال . لأنه يلزم أن لا يبقى التفاوت بين القادر وبين الأقدر . فثبت : أن إعادة الفعل بعين القدرة التي حصل بها الإيجاد في الابتداء : محال . وأما أنه لا يمكن إعادته بقدرة أخرى. لأنه لو جاز أن تتعلق قدرتان بإيجاد مقدور واحد ، لجاز قيام كل واحد منهما بقادر آخر . وذلك يفضي إلى حصول مقدور ، بين قادرين. وهو محال . فثبت : أن العبد لو قدر على إعادة فعل نفسه ، لقدر عليه . إما بعين تلك القدرة وإما بقدرة أخرى وثبت أن كل واحد منهما محال فلزم القول : بأن العبد لا يقدر على الاعادة . وهذا العذر غير موجود في القدرة على إيجاد الفعل ابتداء . فثبت : أنه لا يلزم من قدرة العبد على الإيجاد ابتداء ، قدرته على إعادة ذلك الفعل . فهذا هو تقرير هذا الفرق .
It is impossible with respect to God most high, or with respect to the servant. Its explanation: that which has ceased to exist has perished and nothing remains of it, neither its essence nor any of its rulings. So if after that something else occurs, it is different from the first, and it is impossible that it be the very thing that ceased to exist. We concede that recreation (al-iʿāda) in general is permissible. But why do you say: The one who is capable of origination (al-ibtidāʾ) must be capable of recreation? Its exposition: Origination is distinguished from recreation by some matter. So why is it not permissible to say: That by which the distinction occurs is a condition in one of the two sides or an impediment in the other side? Then we say: The difference between recreation and origination is that if recreation by the servant were valid, then either he would be capable of recreating it by that very power (al-qudra) by which he brought that act into existence originally, or by another power. And both divisions are false. As for [recreating] that act by the very power by which he brought it into existence originally, that is because the power must necessarily be related at every time to bringing into existence an individual of the individuals of that type. So if it were also related at a time to recreating what has ceased to exist, it would have been related in one time, in one locus [of one type] to more than one object of power. And if that were permissible, no number would be more appropriate than another. Then it would necessitate the permissibility of its relation to what is infinite. And that is impossible, because it would necessitate that no distinction remain between the capable and the more capable. So it is established that recreating the act by the very power by which origination occurred is impossible. As for that it cannot be recreated by another power, because if it were permissible for two powers to be related to bringing into existence one object of power, it would be permissible for each one of them to subsist in another capable being. And that leads to an object of power occurring between two capable beings. And that is impossible. So it is established that if the servant were able to recreate his own act, he would be able to do so either by that very power or by another power, and it is established that each one of them is impossible. So it necessitates the statement that the servant is not able to recreate. And this excuse does not exist regarding the power to bring the act into existence initially. So it is established that the servant's ability to bring into existence initially does not necessitate his ability to recreate that act. This is the exposition of this distinction.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents a kalam argument proving that a servant (al-ʿabd) cannot recreate an act that he previously performed, even though he was able to originate it. The argument begins by establishing the impossibility of recreating something that has ceased to exist, since the original thing has perished entirely. Then it concedes that recreation in general is possible (sallamnā anna al-iʿāda fī al-jumla jāʾiza), but challenges the claim that anyone capable of origination (al-ibtidāʾ) must also be capable of recreation. The reason given is that origination is distinguished from recreation by some property; that property could be a condition for one or an impediment for the other. The core proof is then given: if the servant were to recreate his act, it would have to be either by the same power (al-qudra) that originated it, or by a different power. Both possibilities are shown to be impossible. First, if by the same power, that power would then be related to two objects of power at the same time and in the same subject, which would lead to the absurdity of relating to an infinite number of objects and erasing the distinction between the powerful and the more powerful. Second, if by a different power, this would imply two powers relating to one object, which would allow two separate agents to share an act, also absurd. Thus the servant cannot recreate. The conclusion is that the ability to originate does not imply ability to recreate; the distinction is sound.

Key Terms
قدرة (qudra)
ابتداء (ibtidāʾ)
إعادة (iʿāda)
مقدور (maqdūr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first possibility considered for recreating an act?
A) Using a new power created for that purpose.
B) Using the same power that originated the act.
C) Using a power from another being.
D) Using no power at all.

Key Takeaway
Using the same power for both origination and recreation would require it to have multiple objects at once, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever rely on your own limited power to maintain or revive your spiritual state, forgetting that the same power that began your journey cannot sustain it? How does this argument call you to depend on God for both initiation and renewal?
?
Think of a past moment of spiritual strength that you tried to recreate by your own effort. What does Sanusi's division of possibilities teach you about the source of true renewal in your faith?
?
In what area of your life are you assuming that your ability to start something means you can also restore it? How will you actively entrust both the beginning and the continuation to God starting today?

The Second Possibility and the Final Conclusion
Sanusi refutes the second possibility and concludes the argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the second possibility (different power) is also impossible, and how the argument concludes that the servant cannot recreate his own act.

Core Idea
Two powers cannot act on one object; therefore the servant cannot recreate.

Having refuted the first possibility, the author now addresses the second: recreating by a different power. He argues that if two powers could be related to the same object, then two agents could share an act, which is absurd. He then concludes that since both possibilities are impossible, the servant cannot recreate his act. Finally, he reiterates that this does not affect the ability to originate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the argument against the second possibility.
Key phrase: 'لو جاز أن تتعلق قدرتان بإيجاد مقدور واحد'.
2
Understand the second refutation
Understand why two powers cannot combine on one object: it would allow two agents to share an act, which is impossible.
This is based on the principle that one act cannot have two agents.
3
Conclude
See how the author concludes that the servant cannot recreate, and note the distinction that this does not apply to origination.

يمتنع في حق الله تعالى ، أو في حق العبد . وبيانه : أن الذي عدم فقد فني ولم يبق منه لا ذاته ولا حكم من أحكامه . فإذا حصل بعد ذلك شيء آخر ، فهو مغاير للأول ، وامتنع أن يكون عين ذلك الذي عدم . سلمنا : أن الإعادة في الجملة جائزة . لكن لم قلتم : إن القادر على الابتداء ، يجب أن يكون قادراً على الإعادة ؟ وتقريره : إن الابتداء يمتاز عن الإعادة بأمر من الأمور . فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن ما به حصل الامتياز يكون شرطاً في أحد الطرفين أو مانعا في الطرف الآخر ؟ ثم نقول : الفرق بين الإعادة وبين الابتداء . هو أنه لو صحت الإعادة من العبد ، لكان إما أن يقدر على إعادته بتلك القدرة ، التي بها أوجد ذلك الفعل في الابتداء ، أو بغير تلك القدرة . والقسمان باطلان . أما أنه [ إعادة (١) ] ذلك الفعل بعين القدرة التي بها أوجده في الابتداء . فذلك لأن القدرة لا بد وأن تتعلق في كل وقت بإيجاد فرد من أفراد ذلك النوع . فلو تعلقت أيضا في وقت من الأوقات بإعادة ما عدم ، لكان قد تعلقت في الوقت الواحد ، في المحل الواحد [ من الجنس الواحد (٢) ] بأكثر من مقدور واحد ولو جاز [ ذلك (٣) ] لما كان عدد أولى من عدد . فيلزم جواز (4) تعلقها بما لا نهاية له . وذلك محال . لأنه يلزم أن لا يبقى التفاوت بين القادر وبين الأقدر . فثبت : أن إعادة الفعل بعين القدرة التي حصل بها الإيجاد في الابتداء : محال . وأما أنه لا يمكن إعادته بقدرة أخرى. لأنه لو جاز أن تتعلق قدرتان بإيجاد مقدور واحد ، لجاز قيام كل واحد منهما بقادر آخر . وذلك يفضي إلى حصول مقدور ، بين قادرين. وهو محال . فثبت : أن العبد لو قدر على إعادة فعل نفسه ، لقدر عليه . إما بعين تلك القدرة وإما بقدرة أخرى وثبت أن كل واحد منهما محال فلزم القول : بأن العبد لا يقدر على الاعادة . وهذا العذر غير موجود في القدرة على إيجاد الفعل ابتداء . فثبت : أنه لا يلزم من قدرة العبد على الإيجاد ابتداء ، قدرته على إعادة ذلك الفعل . فهذا هو تقرير هذا الفرق .

?

Will you accept that only Allah has power over recreation, and that your power is only over what you do now?
?

?
