Objection and Answer on the Servant's Ability to Recreate
Objection and answer on the servant's ability to recreate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection against the impossibility of recreation and the answer's defense based on possibility as a concomitant of quiddity, refutation of the distinction, and appeal to consensus.

Core Idea
Recreation is impossible for the servant, proven by consensus and the nature of possibility.

In this passage, the author presents an objection that the earlier proof is corrupt, then offers a detailed answer. The answer establishes that possibility is a necessary property of a thing's essence, refutes the opponent's principles, and finally appeals to the consensus of the Muslim community. This lesson unpacks the logical moves.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection carefully. The objector argues that the earlier proof is circular: either the distinction is sound (invalidating the combination) or unsound (forcing denial of the original ruling).
Notice the objector also claims the proof assumes a concession that the servant cannot recreate.
2
Study the answer's first part
The answer begins by establishing the permissibility of recreation in general: possibility is a necessary concomitant of a thing's quiddity. If an act is possible at some time, it remains possible always.
3
Understand the refutation of the non-existent objection
The objector says a non-existent (pure negation) cannot receive a ruling. The answer points out that this statement itself is a ruling, so it is self-refuting; there is no incoherence in ruling that recreation is possible.
4
Note the appeal to consensus
Finally, the answer refutes the opponent's principles (one object of power cannot be between two agents, etc.) as corrupt, and then invokes consensus: the Community unanimously agrees the servant cannot recreate his own act.
This seals the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : هذا الذي ذكرتم قياس فاسد . لأن الفرق الذي ذكرناه . إن صح ، فقد بطل الجمع . وإن فسد ، منعنا الحكم في الأصل ، وقلنا : العبد يقدر على الإعادة . فهذا القياس دائر بين ظهور الفارق بين الأصل والفرع. وبين منع الحكم في الأصل (۱) ] ثم نقول : إنكم بنيتم هذه الحجة على أنا سلمنا أن العبد لا يقدر على إعادة فعل نفسه . ولو منعنا ذلك ، وحكمنا بأنه يجوز منه إعادة فعله ، فحينئذ لا يبقى دليلكم البتة والجواب : أما بيان أن الإعادة جائزة فهو أن جواز الوجود من لوازم الماهية ، فلما ثبت جواز إيجاده في بعض الأحوال ، وجب أن يدوم ذلك في كل الأحوال . قوله : ( إنه نفي محض ، فكيف يمكن الحكم عليه بهذا الحكم المخصوص ) ؟ قلنا : قولكم : بأنه لا يصح الحكم عليه : حكم عليه بامتناع الحكم عليه. ولما كان عدمه غير مانع من هذا الحكم ، فلم لا يجوز أن أيضاً من الحكم الذي ذكرناه ؟ وأما الفرق الذي ذكروه فهو بناء على أصولهم الفاسدة في أنه يمتنع حصول مقدور واحد ، بين قادرين. وفي أن القدرة الواحدة في الوقت الواحد ، في المحل الواحد ، من الجنس الواحد ، لا تتعلق إلا بمقدور واحد . وكل هذه الأصول عندنا فاسدة . قوله : ( لو ثبت فساد هذا الفرق ، منعنا الحكم وقلنا : العبد يقدر على الإعادة قلنا : هذا لا سبيل إليه لأن الأمة مجمعة على أن العبد لا يقدر على إعادة فعل نفسه، فالقول بأنه يقدر على هذه الإعادة يكون خرقاً للإجماع .
Then we say: This analogy you have mentioned is corrupt. For the distinction we have mentioned, if it is sound, then the combination is invalidated; and if it is unsound, we deny the ruling in the original and say: The servant is able to recreate. So this analogy oscillates between establishing the difference between the original and the branch, and denying the ruling in the original. Then we say: You have built this proof on our conceding that the servant is not able to recreate his own act. But if we deny that and rule that recreation of his act is permissible from him, then your proof remains not at all. The answer: As for the demonstration that recreation is permissible, it is that the possibility of existence is among the necessary concomitants of the quiddity (al-māhiyya). So when the possibility of bringing it into existence in some states is established, it must continue in all states. His saying: 'It is a pure negation, so how can it be subject to this specific ruling?' We say: Your statement that it is not correct to rule upon it is itself a ruling upon it by the impossibility of ruling upon it. And since its non-existence does not prevent this ruling, why would it not also be permissible for the ruling we have mentioned? As for the distinction they mentioned, it is based on their corrupt principles: that it is impossible for one object of power to occur between two capable beings, and that the single power in one time, in one locus, of one type is related only to one object of power. All these principles are corrupt according to us. His saying: 'If the corruption of this distinction is established, we deny the ruling and say: The servant is able to recreate.' We say: There is no way to this, because the Community (al-umma) is unanimous that the servant is not able to recreate his own act, so saying that he is able to this recreation would be a breach of consensus (al-ijmāʿ).
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage contains an objection to the previous proof and its answer. The objector first attacks the distinction between origination and recreation as corrupt, arguing that the proof is circular: either the distinction is valid and the opponent's combination is false, or it is invalid and the original ruling (that the servant cannot recreate) must be denied. The objector also points out that the earlier proof assumed the opponent's concession that the servant cannot recreate; but if that is denied, the proof collapses. The answer begins by establishing the permissibility of recreation in general: possibility (jawāz) is a necessary concomitant of the quiddity (al-māhiyya); once an act is possible at some time, it must remain possible at all times. Then the answer addresses the objection that a non-existent (a pure negation) cannot receive a ruling: the answer points out that the objector's own statement that it cannot be ruled upon is itself a ruling, so it is not incoherent to rule that it is possible. Next, the answer refutes the specific distinction (al-farq) that the first argument used, declaring that it relies on corrupt principles (uṣūl fāsida), namely the impossibility of a single object of power being between two agents, and the impossibility of one power being related to more than one object. These principles are rejected by the answerer. Finally, the answer appeals to consensus (ijmāʿ): the Community is agreed that the servant cannot recreate his own act, so to claim otherwise would break consensus.

Key Terms
إعادة (iʿāda)
قياس فاسد (qiyās fāsid)
جواز وجود (jawāz al-wujūd)
ماهية (māhiyya)
إجماع (ijmāʿ)
قدرة (qudra)
مقدور (maqdūr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a non-existent (pure negation) cannot be subject to any ruling at all.
Correction: The answerer points out that the objector's own statement that it cannot be ruled upon is itself a ruling, so it is not incoherent to rule that recreation is possible.

Checkpoint
How does the answerer refute the objection that a non-existent cannot receive a ruling?
A) By arguing that the objector's own statement is a ruling, so a non-existent can be ruled upon.
B) By citing a verse from the Qur'an.
C) By claiming that consensus alone decides the matter.
D) By redefining the non-existent as something that can exist.

Key Takeaway
The servant's inability to recreate is defended by logical arguments (possibility, refutation of principles) and the binding consensus of the Muslim community.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face an objection to your belief, do you immediately dismiss it or do you honestly examine the principles behind it? The answerer here refutes corrupt principles—are there any beliefs you hold that are built on unexamined principles?
?
The Community is unanimous that the servant cannot recreate his own act. Does this consensus challenge any areas where you assume human beings are independent creators of their actions? How does this affect your daily sense of reliance on Allah?
?
In your own life, do you act as if you can 'recreate' past deeds—whether good or bad—as if they are under your power? How does the lesson's conclusion that recreation belongs only to Allah shift your perspective on repentance and moving forward?

Al-Razi's Third Proof: Concepts Come Only from Senses, Self, or Composition
Al-Razi enumerates three sources of concepts and concludes none are acquired.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's third argument that all concepts are either sensory, internal, or combinations thereof, and therefore none are acquired.

Core Idea
The mind cannot create new simple concepts; it only combines what it already has.

In this passage, al-Razi rounds off his case against the acquisition of concepts by turning to introspection. He argues that when we examine our own minds, we find that every concept we can think of belongs to one of three categories. This self-evident observation, he claims, settles the matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three categories
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the three kinds of concepts al-Razi lists: sensory, internal, and composite.
The categories are explicitly enumerated.
2
Observe how al-Razi uses self-observation as proof
Notice that al-Razi bases his argument on introspection ('if we turn to ourselves'). He claims this is known intuitively (bil-badiha).
This is a key methodological move.
3
Understand the conclusion
Al-Razi concludes that no concepts are acquired (ghayr muktasab). Ask yourself what 'acquired' means in this context.
Acquired means produced by the mind independently.
4
Reflect on the epistemological implication
Consider that if all concepts are from senses, inner states, or their composites, then human knowledge is fundamentally limited to these sources.
This has implications for what we can know about abstract realities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث في بيان أن الأمر كما ذكرناه : هو أنا إذا رجعنا إلى أنفسنا ، علمنا أنه لا يمكننا أن نتصور أمراً من الأمور ، إلا التصورات ، التي أدركناها بأحد الحواس الخمس، أو التصورات التي وجدناها من أنفسنا . كعلمنا بالألم واللذة ، والفرح والغم ، وأشباهها . أو ما يركبه العقل أو الخيال من أحد هذه الأمور . فأما أن نتصور أمراً وراء هذه الأقسام . فلا سبيل لنا البتة إليه . وهذا المعنى معلوم بالبديهة عند اعتبار أحوال النفس . فثبت بهذه الوجوه الثلاثة : أن شيئاً من التصورات غير مكتسب
The third aspect in explaining that the matter is as we stated: that if we turn to ourselves, we know that we cannot imagine any matter except concepts that we have perceived through one of the five senses, or concepts that we have found within ourselves, such as our knowledge of pain and pleasure, joy and grief, and the like, or what the intellect or imagination compounds from one of these matters. As for imagining a matter beyond these divisions, we have absolutely no way to it. And this meaning is known by intuition upon considering the states of the soul. Thus it is established by these three aspects that none of the concepts are acquired.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof (al-wajh al-thalith) for the author's thesis that no concepts (tasawwurat) can be acquired by the human mind. The author argues by a self-observation: when we turn to ourselves, we realize that we cannot conceive any matter except three kinds of concepts: first, concepts perceived through one of the five external senses (such as colors, sounds); second, concepts found within ourselves (such as pain, pleasure, joy, grief); third, compounds of these simple concepts formed by the intellect or imagination. Anything beyond these three divisions is utterly inaccessible to us. The author states that this is known intuitively (bil-badiha) when we consider the states of the soul. He then concludes that with these three proofs (the first two being mentioned earlier) it is established that no concept is acquired. The implication is that all concepts are either immediate or composed from immediate ones; none can be produced ex nihilo by the mind.

Key Terms
تصور (tasawwur)
اكتساب (iktisab)
حواس خمس (hawaas khams)
بديهة (badiha)
نفس (nafs)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mind can create entirely new concepts from nothing.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that the mind can only combine or compound concepts from sensory or internal sources; it cannot create fundamentally new simple concepts.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, which of the following is NOT a source of concepts?
A) The five senses
B) Internal sensations
C) Imagination combining data
D) Intellect creating new simple concepts

Key Takeaway
Every concept originates from senses, internal states, or their combination; no concept is acquired independently by the mind.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Can you identify one belief you hold that you assumed came solely from your own reasoning, but upon reflection, it actually originated from something you sensed or felt? How does this passage challenge your view of where your ideas come from?
?
This passage claims you cannot conceive anything beyond senses, internal states, or their combination. Where does your knowledge of the unseen (al-ghayb) fit? Does this make you rely more on revelation? Why or why not?
?
Consider a time you felt proud of an 'original' idea. According to al-Razi, that idea was merely a recombination of past experiences. How does this humble your sense of intellectual originality, and what does it teach you about the role of your senses and inner states in shaping your worldview?

Intuitive vs. Theoretical Affirmations
Al-Razi defines the two types of affirmations with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between intuitive and theoretical affirmations, and how each is defined by the role of mental concepts.

Core Idea
An affirmation is intuitive if the mere presence of its subject and predicate concepts compels the judgment; otherwise it is theoretical.

Al-Razi begins his first proof that no affirmations are acquired by establishing a fundamental classification. He argues that every affirmation requires two concepts, and the key difference between intuitive and theoretical affirmations lies in whether those concepts alone force the mind to judge. This classification is the basis of the rest of his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
2
Identify the two categories
The passage presents two types of affirmations: intuitive (badihiyyat) and theoretical (nazariyyat). Notice how each is defined. Intuitive: mere presence of two concepts compels the judgment. Theoretical: mere presence does not compel.
The definitions are given right after the two possibilities.
3
Examine the examples
Al-Razi gives examples: 'one is half of two' is intuitive, 'the world is originated' is theoretical. Reflect on why the first forces the judgment while the second does not.
This helps solidify the distinction.
4
Summarise the classification
In your own words, describe the two types and state what distinguishes them. This prepares you for the proof that neither is acquired.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أن شيئاً من التصديقات غير مكتسب . فيدل عليه أيضاً وجوه : الأول : إن كل تصديق ، فلا بد فيه من تصورين . أحدهما : تصور الموضوع. والآخر : تصور المحمول. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إما أن يكون مجرد حضور هذين التصورين في الذهن ، مستقلا بإيجاب أن يحكم الذهن بذلك التصديق ، أو لا يكون . والأول : هو البديهيات والثاني : هو النظريات . مثال الأول : إنا إذا تصورنا أن الواحد ما هو ؟ وتصورنا أن نصف الاثنين ما هو ؟ فمجرد حضور هذين التصورين في الذهن ، يوجب جزم الذهن بأن الواحد نصف الاثنين. فهذا هو البديهي . ومثال الثاني إنا إذا تصورنا : أن العالم ما هو ؟ وأن الحادث ما هو ؟ لم يكن مجرد حضور هذين التصورين، موجباً جزم الذهن بأن العالم حادث ، أو ليس بحادث . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : أما التصديقات البديهية ، فشيء منها غير مكتسب . لأن ذينك التصورين . إن حضرا ، كانا موجبين لذلك التصديق - والإنسان لا قدرة له في تحصيل ذينك التصورين - وعند حضورهما فلا قدرة له في استلزامهما لذلك التصديق . بل إن حضرا لكان عند حضور ذلك التصديق واجباً . وإن لم يحضر إلا واحداً منهما ، كان حضور ذلك التصديق ممتنعاً . فثبت : أن الإنسان لا قدرة له البتة على التصديقات البديهية . وأما التصديقات النظرية . فلا قدرة له أيضاً على شيء منها . لأن تلك البديهيات ، إن كانت متجمعة للأمور المعتبرة في استلزام تلك النظريات ، كان حصول تلك النظريات عقيب تلك البديهيات واجباً ، فلم يكن للإنسان قدرة عليها . وإن لم تكن مستجمعة للأمور المعتبرة في ذلك الاستلزام ، امتنع كونها مستلزمة لتلك النظريات . والممتنع لا قدرة عليه . وتمام تقرير هذا الكلام هو الذي سبق ذكره في البرهان الأول . إلا أن هذا الوجه في الحقيقة غير مختص بالتصديقات النظرية ، بل هو عام في كيفية المكتسبات . علم من علم يتقدمه ، سواء كان ذلك العلم ، علما تصورياً أو تصديقياً .
As for the statement that none of the affirmations are acquired, it is also indicated by aspects: The first: Every affirmation necessarily involves two concepts: one is the concept of the subject, and the other is the concept of the predicate. If you know this, we say: either the mere presence of these two concepts in the mind is sufficient to obligate the mind to judge that affirmation, or it is not. The first is the intuitive affirmations (badihiyyat), and the second is the theoretical affirmations (nazariyyat). An example of the first: if we conceive what 'one' is, and we conceive what 'half of two' is, then the mere presence of these two concepts in the mind necessitates the mind's certainty that one is half of two. This is the intuitive. An example of the second: if we conceive what 'the world' is and what 'the originated' is, the mere presence of these two concepts does not necessitate the mind's certainty that the world is originated or not originated. If you know this, we say: as for the intuitive affirmations, none of them are acquired, because those two concepts, if they are present, they necessitate that affirmation — and man has no power to acquire those two concepts, and when they are present, he has no power over their necessitating that affirmation. Rather, if both are present, then the affirmation is necessary upon their presence. If only one of them is present, the presence of that affirmation is impossible. Thus it is established that man has absolutely no power over intuitive affirmations. As for theoretical affirmations, he also has no power over any of them, because if those intuitive [affirmations] include the conditions considered in necessitating those theoretical [affirmations], then the occurrence of those theoretical [affirmations] after those intuitive ones is necessary, so man has no power over them. If they do not include the conditions for that necessitation, then it is impossible for them to necessitate those theoretical [affirmations], and what is impossible is not within one's power. And the complete establishment of this statement is what was previously mentioned in the first proof, except that this aspect is in truth not specific to theoretical affirmations, but is general regarding how acquisitions occur: any knowledge that is preceded by knowledge, whether that knowledge is conceptual or affirmational.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof that no affirmations (tasdiqat) are acquired. The author begins by noting that every affirmation requires two concepts: the concept of the subject and the concept of the predicate. Then he distinguishes two cases: either the mere presence of these two concepts in the mind is sufficient to compel the mind to make the affirmation (these are the intuitive affirmations, badihiyyat), or it is not (these are the theoretical affirmations, nazariyyat). He gives examples: the judgment that 'one is half of two' is intuitive because merely understanding the concepts of 'one' and 'half of two' forces the mind to affirm it. In contrast, the judgment that 'the world is originated' is theoretical because merely understanding 'world' and 'originated' does not force the judgment. Next, the author argues that the intuitive affirmations are not acquired because man has no power to obtain the two concepts themselves (they are given) and no power to prevent the necessary judgment that follows from them. If both concepts are present, the judgment is necessary; if only one is present, the judgment is impossible. Man has no control. For theoretical affirmations, the author explains that they depend on intuitive affirmations that serve as premises. If those premises contain the conditions for necessitating the theoretical conclusion, then the conclusion is necessary and not within man's power; if they do not, then the conclusion cannot be reached. Thus man has no power over theoretical affirmations either. The author adds that this proof is general: any knowledge that is preceded by another knowledge—whether conceptual or affirmational—is not truly acquired in the sense of being freely produced; it is necessitated by what precedes it.

Key Terms
تصديق
بديهي
نظري
تصور الموضوع
تصور المحمول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think theoretical affirmations are based on nothing; they require no concepts.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that theoretical affirmations also require two concepts, but the judgment is not forced by their mere presence.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of an intuitive affirmation according to al-Razi?
A) The world is originated
B) One is half of two
C) Fire is hot
D) Water is wet

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi classifies affirmations into intuitive (where the concepts compel judgment) and theoretical (where they do not).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, which of your beliefs about yourself, others, or the world are merely intuitive (you feel they are obvious) and which are theoretical (you hold them because you've reasoned or been told)? Does this lesson challenge you to examine whether your intuitive beliefs are truly self-evident or just comfortable assumptions?
?
When you read the example 'the world is originated', do you find that your mind immediately assents to it, or do you feel the need for proof? What does that reveal about how you approach truth claims in your own life, especially about matters of faith?
?
If every theoretical affirmation requires active reasoning and reflection, then which areas of your religious knowledge have you merely accepted without the hard work of thinking through the proofs? Will you commit today to investigating one such belief until you can see the evidence clearly?

The Argument That No Affirmation Is Acquired
Al-Razi proves that intuitive and theoretical affirmations are not acquired.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Razi argues that both intuitive and theoretical affirmations are not acquired but are necessitated by the presence of concepts.

Core Idea
Man has no power over affirmations—they are either necessary or impossible given the concepts present.

Having classified affirmations, al-Razi now argues that neither type is acquired through human power. He shows that intuitive affirmations are unavoidable when their concepts are present, and theoretical affirmations are either necessitated by previous intuition or impossible. Thus, all affirmations are outside human control.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument for intuitive affirmations
Read the part starting with 'As for the intuitive affirmations...' and notice the reasoning: neither the concepts nor the necessitation are within human power.
The key phrase: 'man has absolutely no power over intuitive affirmations.'
2
Extend to theoretical affirmations
Read the argument for theoretical affirmations. They depend on intuitive premises; either the premises necessitate the conclusion or they don't. In both cases, no power.
The logic is that if the intuitive premises are sufficient, the conclusion is necessary; if not, it's impossible.
3
Understand the general principle
The last part states that this proof is general: any knowledge preceded by other knowledge (conceptual or affirmational) is not acquired freely.
This ties the argument to broader epistemology.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Summarize why al-Razi believes no affirmation is acquired. Consider whether you find the reasoning convincing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you cannot control whether you affirm something—like the truth of a mathematical theorem or a religious belief—then what is the nature of your certainty? Are you truly responsible for your beliefs, or are they forced upon you by the evidence you have? How does that make you feel about the beliefs you hold most firmly?
?
Think of a belief you changed your mind about. According to this argument, that change was not your doing; it was necessitated by new concepts or premises that became present to you. Does that thought make you more humble or more anxious about your intellectual journey?
?
If all your affirmations are either necessary or impossible given your current concepts, what is the point of pursuing knowledge? Why bother seeking evidence or reasoning? What does this argument imply about the value of intellectual effort—does it matter, or is it just a passive unfolding of what must be?

Logic and Technical Terms

8 min
Human cannot acquire knowledge or ignorance by themselves
Proof that humans cannot make themselves know or ignorant, with objection and answer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that humans cannot generate knowledge or ignorance independently, and why all cognitive states are ultimately from God.

Core Idea
You cannot make yourself know or ignorant without circularity — all knowledge is from God.

This lesson unpacks a profound kalam argument against human self-determination of knowledge. Using the concepts of belief, correspondence, and infinite regress, the author demonstrates that neither knowledge nor ignorance can be self-acquired. Even chains of reasoning terminate in a first cause, which is God. The passage includes an objection and its refutation, showing the conclusiveness of the proof.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definitions
Read the passage and note how the author defines belief as knowledge if it corresponds to its object, and ignorance if it does not.
2
Trace the knowledge proof
Follow the argument that to intend knowledge, one must already distinguish knowledge from ignorance, which requires prior knowledge of the object, leading to circularity.
This circularity shows self-acquisition is impossible.
3
Apply the same proof to ignorance
Notice the parallel reasoning: to become ignorant, one must know the object first, making ignorance dependent on prior knowledge.
4
Consider the objection
The objector suggests that knowledge could be produced through prior intermediate knowledges. See how this is addressed.
5
Analyze the two-part answer
The answer argues that the chain of ignorances cannot regress infinitely (ends at a first ignorance from God) and the implications are necessary, so all is from God.
This reinforces the conclusion that all cognitive states are divinely created.

التعيين . فنقول : هذا أيضاً محال . وذلك لأن اعتقاد أن الشيء كذا ، قد يكون علماً - إذا كان مطابقاً لذلك المعتقد وقد يكون جهلاً ، وهو إذا كان غير مطابق . فالعلم إنما يتميز عن الجهل، إذا عرف كونه مطابقاً لذلك المعتقد وقد يكون جهلا ، وهو إذا كان غير مطابق (۱) فعلى هذا لا يمكن أن يقصد إلى العلم دون الجهل، إلا إذا ميز بين العلم وبين الجهل، ولا يمكنه أن يعرف هذا الامتياز إلا إذا عرف أن هذا الاعتقاد مطابق للمعتقد، ولا يمكنه أن يعرف هذه المطابقة، إلا إذا عرف أولا حال ذلك المعتقد في نفسه . فثبت : أنه لا يمكن أن يجعل نفسه عالماً بذلك الشيء، إلا إذا كان قد عرف أولاً حال ذلك الشيء . وذلك يقتضي كون الشيء مشروطاً بنفسه . وهو محال . فثبت : أن إقدار الانسان على جعل نفسه، عالماً بشيء : أمر محال . وبهذا البرهان يظهر أيضاً : أن الإنسان لا يمكنه أن يجعل نفسه جاهلاً بشيء . لأن الجهل إنما يتميز عن العلم، بكونه غير مطابق للمعتقد. ولا يمكنه أن يعرف كونه غير مطابق للمعتقد، إلا إذا عرف قبل ذلك، حال ذلك المعتقد في نفسه . فثبت : أنه لا يمكنه تحصيل الجهل لنفسه، إلا إذا كان عالماً بذلك الشيء . وهذا يقتضي كون أحد الضدين مشروطاً بالآخر، وإنه محال
فثبت بهذه البراهين القاطعة : أن كل ما حصل في قلوب الخلق وعقولهم
من العلوم والجهلات، فالكل من الله [ وبإيجاد الله (۲) ] .
فإن قالوا : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه وإن كان لا يمكنه إحداث العلوم والجهالات في نفسه ابتداء، إلا أنه يمكنه إحداثها بواسطة علوم متقدمة عليها،
أو جهالات متقدمة عليها ؟
قلنا : الجواب من وجهين :
الأول : إن تلك الجهالات لا تتسلسل، بل تنتهي إلى جهل أول .
فيكون خالقه هو الله سبحانه
والثاني : إن استلزام تلك العلوم المتقدمة لتلك العلوم التي هي النتائج يجب أن تكون ضرورية. وكذلك استلزام تلك الجهالات المتقدمة لتلك الجهالات التي هي النتائج، يجب أن تكون ضرورية . وعلى هذا التقدير يكون
الكل من الله تعالى
The assignment. We say: this too is impossible. That is because belief that something is such-and-such may be knowledge – if it corresponds to that believed thing – and may be ignorance, when it does not correspond. Knowledge is only distinguished from ignorance if one knows its correspondence to the believed thing. So therefore one cannot intend knowledge without ignorance unless one distinguishes between knowledge and ignorance, and he cannot know this distinction unless he knows that this belief corresponds to the believed thing, and he cannot know this correspondence unless he first knows the state of that believed thing in itself. So it is established: he cannot make himself knowledgeable of that thing unless he first knew the state of that thing. That requires the thing to be conditioned by itself, which is impossible. So it is established: enabling a human to make himself knowledgeable of a thing is impossible. And by this proof it also appears: a human cannot make himself ignorant of a thing. Because ignorance is only distinguished from knowledge by being non-correspondence to the believed thing. And he cannot know its non-correspondence to the believed thing unless he knew before that the state of that believed thing in itself. So it is established: he cannot acquire ignorance for himself unless he was knowledgeable of that thing. This requires one of the two opposites to be conditioned by the other, and that is impossible. So it is established by these conclusive proofs: whatever occurs in the hearts and minds of creatures of knowledges and ignorances, all of it is from God [and by God's creation]. If they say: Why may it not be said that although he cannot initiate knowledges and ignorances in himself, he can produce them through prior knowledges or prior ignorances? We say: the answer is from two aspects: First: those ignorances do not regress infinitely, but end at a first ignorance, so its creator is God the Exalted. Second: the implication of those prior knowledges to those resultant knowledges must be necessary, and similarly the implication of those prior ignorances to those resultant ignorances must be necessary. On this estimation, all is from God the Exalted.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage contains a multi-part proof that humans cannot create knowledge or ignorance in themselves, followed by an objection and its answer. The author begins by analyzing the concept of belief: a belief may be knowledge if it corresponds to its object, or ignorance if it does not. To intentionally acquire knowledge, one must distinguish it from ignorance by knowing that the belief corresponds. But knowing correspondence requires prior knowledge of the object itself. This leads to an infinite regress or circularity—the thing is conditioned by itself, which is impossible. Hence, self-generation of knowledge is impossible. By a parallel argument, self-generation of ignorance is also impossible because distinguishing ignorance requires prior knowledge of the object. The author concludes that all knowledge and ignorance in creatures is from God. An objection is then raised: perhaps a person can produce knowledge or ignorance through intermediate prior sciences or ignorances, even if not directly. The answer has two parts: (1) The chain of ignorances cannot regress infinitely; it must end at a first ignorance, which God creates. (2) The implication of those prior states to the resultant ones must be necessary, meaning they are ultimately caused by God. Thus, even through intermediaries, all knowledge and ignorance are from God.

Key Terms
علم
جهل
اعتقاد
مطابقة
برهان
تسلسل
ضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think humans can produce knowledge from prior knowledge without divine causation.
Correction: The argument shows that the chain terminates in a first state created by God, so all knowledge is from God.

Checkpoint
Why is it impossible for a human to make himself knowledgeable of a thing according to this proof?
A) Because knowledge is purely a gift from God without any prior condition.
B) Because it requires prior knowledge of the same thing, leading to circularity.
C) Because humans lack the faculty to intend knowledge.
D) Because belief never corresponds to reality.

Key Takeaway
All knowledge and ignorance in creatures are created by God, as self-acquisition leads to circularity or infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every piece of knowledge you have is a direct gift from God, how does that change the way you hold onto your opinions? When you are certain about something, do you still remain open to correction, knowing that God could guide you to the truth at any moment? This week, catch yourself feeling proud of your understanding and instead thank God for granting it.
?
You cannot make yourself ignorant—so every time you fail to seek understanding, are you not defaulting to a state that God alone creates? That means your apathy is also from Him. How does that reality push you to beseech Him more earnestly for guidance and knowledge? Resolve today to start each study session with a prayer for divine opening.
?
If God creates both knowledge and ignorance, then which one are you inviting through your choices? Are you surrounding yourself with people and materials that nurture ignorance? What step can you take tonight to cultivate knowledge as a God-given blessing?

The Fourth Proof: Ignorance Must Be from God
Fourth proof: ignorance's occurrence against intention shows it is from another

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fourth proof establishes that ignorance is from God, using the fact that it occurs contrary to human intention and the regress argument against the objection.

Core Idea
Ignorance is against our natural aim, so it must come from beyond us.

This lesson presents the fourth proof from al-Matālib al-ʻĀliyah, which argues that ignorance must be created by God because human beings naturally intend only knowledge. When ignorance occurs, it is against our intention and choice, so it must originate from an external agent. The proof also addresses the objection that we might mistake ignorance for knowledge, showing that such a mistake itself depends on prior ignorance, ultimately tracing back to a first ignorance from God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice that the passage has three parts: the main proof, an objection, and the answer.
2
Identify the core claim
Find the statement that explains why ignorance must be from another: 'its occurrence is contrary to his intention and choice'. This is the key insight.
The author assumes that man's natural intention is to acquire knowledge.
3
Examine the objection and answer
Read the objection: ignorance might be chosen because it is mistaken for knowledge. Then see how the answer uses a regress argument to show that such a mistake itself requires a prior ignorance, leading ultimately to God.
This refutes the idea that ignorance could be entirely self-caused.
4
Reflect on the chain of ignorances
Consider the infinite regress: each mistaken choice requires a prior ignorance. The chain cannot go on forever, so it must end at a first ignorance created by God.
This shows that all ignorance, even from mistakes, is ultimately from God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الرابع
إن الانسان لا يقصد إلا تحصيل العلم . فلما حصل الجهل، كان حصوله على خلاف قصده واختياره. فوجب أن يكون من غيره .
فإن قالوا : ذلك لأنه اشتبه عليه هذا الجهل بالعلم . فتقول : فهو إنما اختار ذلك الجهل، لتقدم جهل آخر . ولا بد وأن تنتهي تلك الجهالات إلى الجهل الأول، الواقع بخلق الله .
The fourth proof: Man intends only to acquire knowledge. So when ignorance occurs, its occurrence is contrary to his intention and choice, so it must be from another. If they say: that is because this ignorance was mistaken for knowledge. So we say: he only chose that ignorance due to a prior ignorance, and those ignorances must end at the first ignorance, which occurs by God's creation.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof: that ignorance must come from God. The author states that man's natural intention is to acquire knowledge. Therefore, when ignorance occurs, it is against his intention and choice. So it must come from an external agent – God. An objection is then considered: perhaps the ignorance occurred because the person mistook ignorance for knowledge, thus choosing it inadvertently. The answer is that this mistaken choice itself is due to a prior ignorance, and that chain of ignorances must lead back to a first ignorance that is created by God. Thus, the original ignorance is ultimately from God.

Key Terms

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People might think that ignorance is entirely self-caused due to voluntary mistakes.
Correction: The proof shows that even mistaken choices presuppose prior ignorance, and the chain of ignorances cannot go back infinitely; it must end at a first ignorance created by God.

Checkpoint

A) Because man cannot create ignorance at all.
B) Because man only intends knowledge, so ignorance occurs contrary to his intention.
C) Because all actions are predetermined.
D) Because ignorance is always a punishment from God.

Key Takeaway
Ignorance ultimately traces back to God as its creator, because human choices cannot explain the initial ignorance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The proof shows that every mistaken choice is caused by a prior ignorance. Think of a recent mistake you made due to ignorance. Are you willing to trace that back to its root and ask Allah to remove that root ignorance? What is the first thing you can learn today to break that chain?

The Fifth Proof: Disagreement About Knowledge Shows It Is Not Self-Created
Fifth proof: perplexity about knowledge's essence and subject shows it is not self-created

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the disagreement among scholars about the essence and subject of knowledge serves as evidence that knowledge is not self-created by humans.

Core Idea
Disagreement about knowledge proves we did not create it ourselves.

The author presents a fifth proof: the very fact that people are perplexed and disagree about what knowledge is and where it resides shows that they did not create it. If they had, they would know its true nature. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two areas of perplexity
The passage mentions confusion about the essence (māhiyya) of knowledge and confusion about its subject (mawḍūʿ). Notice how these two separate issues are introduced.
2
Understand the three opinions on the essence of knowledge
Some say knowledge is merely a relation (taʿalluq), some say it is a real attribute entailing that relation, and some say it is a form in the knower matching the known. These are the main positions.
3
Follow the argument from self-creation
The author argues: if knowledge were self-created, the knower would know the purpose of his creation. Since intending what is not conceived is impossible, and confusion remains, knowledge cannot be self-created.
4
Consider the disagreement about the subject of knowledge
Similar disagreement exists about where knowledge resides—heart, brain, etc.—further showing it is not self-created.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الخامس
وهو المطلوب
إن الناس تحيروا في ماهية العلم وفي موضوعه . أما حيرتهم في ماهية العلم . فلأن بعضهم يقول : العلم ليس إلا مجرد هذه النسبة المسماة بالتعلق . وبعضهم يقول : أنه صفه حقيقية مستلزمة لهذه النسبة والإضافة . وبعضهم يقول : إنه عبارة عن صورة مساوية لماهية المعلوم، حاصلة في ذات العالم . ولو كان حصول هذا العالم بإيجادي وخلقي، لكنت عالماً بأني لماذا خلقت ؟ لأن القصد إلى ما لا يكون متصوراً : محال . ولو كنت أعلم أني لماذا خلقت ؟ لما بقي هذا الاشتباه وأما حيرتهم في موضع العلم . فلأن بعضهم قال : موضعه هو القلب وبعضهم قال: إن موضعـه هـو الدماغ (۱)] وبعضهم قال: موضعه هو
The fifth proof, and it is the desired one: People have been perplexed about the essence of knowledge and its subject. As for their perplexity about the essence of knowledge: some say knowledge is nothing but this relation called 'relation' (talluq). Some say it is a real attribute entailing this relation and addition. Some say it is a form equal to the essence of the known, existing in the essence of the knower. If this knowledge were [acquired] by my own creation and making, I would know why I was created? Because intending what is not conceived is impossible. And if I knew why I was created, this confusion would not remain. And as for their perplexity about the subject of knowledge: some say its subject is the heart, some say it is the brain, some say it is...
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof: the fact that people disagree about the essence (māhiyya) and subject (mawḍūʿ) of knowledge shows that knowledge is not created by humans themselves. The author describes three opinions on the essence: (1) knowledge is merely a relation (taʿalluq), (2) it is a real attribute that entails this relation, (3) it is a form in the knower corresponding to the known. Then the author argues: if knowledge were created by the human knower, he would know the purpose of his own creation – because intending something unknown is impossible. Yet people remain perplexed, which proves they did not create their own knowledge. Similarly, regarding the subject (location) of knowledge, there are differing opinions: some say it is the heart, some the brain, etc. This disagreement would not exist if knowledge were self-created. The proof is incomplete in this window but the argument is clear.

Misconception: One might think that disagreement about knowledge simply reflects human ignorance and has no bearing on whether knowledge is self-created.
Correction: The proof uses disagreement as evidence: if knowledge were self-created, the knower would have direct and certain knowledge of its essence and subject, leaving no room for disagreement.

Checkpoint

B) The impossibility of creating ignorance.

D) The inability to distinguish knowledge from ignorance.

Key Takeaway
Disagreement about the nature and location of knowledge reveals that knowledge is not a product of human creation.

?
The proof rests on the fact that intention requires conception. You cannot intend what you do not know. Look at your own intentions: do you know why you are seeking knowledge in this lesson? If you do not, then your knowledge is not self-made. Will you ask Allah to reveal to you the purpose He has for your learning?

The Fifth Condition: Absence of Forwarding and Delaying
Lists the fifth condition and its epistemological status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth condition for textual evidence: the need for absence of forwarding and delaying, and why its absence is only speculative.

Core Idea
Word order is crucial, but we can never be certain it hasn't been altered.

The author now introduces the fifth condition that a text must meet to serve as reliable evidence. He explains that the absence of forwarding (taqdim) and delaying (ta'khir) in the wording is necessary because such reordering would change the meaning. However, he immediately qualifies this: we cannot be absolutely certain that no such reordering has occurred; it remains a matter of probability. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own words come through.
The passage is very concise.
2
Identify the condition and reason
Notice that the fifth condition is the absence of forwarding and delaying. The reason given is that such reordering changes meaning.
3
Understand the distinction between speculative and certain
The author explicitly states that the absence of reordering is only speculative (maznun), not certain (ma'lum). This is an epistemological point about the reliability of textual evidence.
Think about why it is only speculative: human transmission can always introduce changes.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider what this means for using texts as evidence: they are probabilistic, not definitive. This is a key concept in kalam methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : ويتوقف أيضاً على عدم التقديم والتأخير . لأن بسببهما يتغير المعنى . لكن عدمهما مظنون .
The fifth: It also depends on the absence of forwarding and delaying, because by them the meaning changes. But their absence is speculative (maznun), not known (ma'lum).
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth condition required for textual evidence to be fully reliable. The author states that the evidence also depends on the absence of tqdim (forwarding) and ta'khir (delaying) in the wording. The reason given is that such reordering can change the meaning. However, the absence of such reordering is only speculative (maznun), not certain (ma'lum). This highlights that even if a text appears in a certain order, we cannot be certain that it has not been altered, so the evidence remains probabilistic.

Key Terms
تقديم
تأخير
مظنون
معلوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the absence is only speculative, then the condition makes textual evidence useless.
Correction: The condition does not make evidence useless; it simply acknowledges that textual evidence is probabilistic, not certain. It still carries weight as a reasonable basis for belief.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth condition, why is the absence of forwarding and delaying only speculative?
A) Because the original text has been lost.
B) Because such reordering is known to change meaning, but its absence cannot be absolutely verified.
C) Because the Quran forbids reordering.
D) Because all texts are ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
The fifth condition shows that textual evidence is inherently probabilistic because we cannot be certain that the original word order has been preserved.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you assume that the words you read or hear are exactly as originally intended? This lesson shows that even in transmitted texts, word order may be speculative. Does that humble you? Will you now be more careful in accepting interpretations without questioning the possibility of reordering?
?
Think of a time when a small change in order caused a misunderstanding in your own life. How does this lesson challenge you to seek certainty before relying on what you hear? What personal discipline can you adopt to verify the integrity of knowledge?

The Sixth Condition: Absence of a Specifier
Sanusi states the sixth condition and illustrates it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why theological arguments based on general Quranic or prophetic statements are only probable, not certain, due to the possibility of a specifier.

Core Idea
General texts need no specifier, but that absence is only speculative.

After laying out conditions for valid textual evidence, the author presents the sixth: the absence of a specifier (mukhasis). This condition highlights a critical limitation: most general statements in revelation are actually specified, so a claim that a general statement applies universally cannot be certain. The lesson examines this condition through the example of the verse 'Creator of everything.'

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Focus on the author's concise formulation of the condition and its significance.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the condition and its implication
Notice that the author says textual evidence 'depends on' the absence of a specifier. Then note that this absence is speculative, not known with certainty.
This distinguishes probable from certain evidence.
3
Reflect on the example
Consider the verse 'Creator of everything' (Q. 6:102). The author explains it only proves creation of human actions if we know the generality is not specified for them. Since we don't know that, the argument is only speculative.
Think of other general verses that might be specified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : ويتوقف على عدم المخصص . فإن أكثر عمومات القرآن والسنة ، مخصوص بعدم كون العام مخصوصاً مظنوناً ، لا معلوماً . ومثاله : قوله تعالى : ( خالق كل شي ء ) فهذا إنما يدل على أنه تعالى خالق لأعمال العباد [ لو علمنا أن هذا العموم، غير مخصوص في أفعال العباد ] لأن بتقدير أن يكون قد وجد ما يدل على كونه مخصوصاً ، لم يكن التمسك به . إلا أن عدم المخصص : مظنون لا معلوم
The sixth: It depends on the absence of a specifier (mukhasis). For most general statements ('umumat) of the Quran and Sunnah are specified. The absence of the general being specified is speculative (maznun), not known (ma'lum). Its example: His statement (Creator of everything). This only indicates that He is the Creator of the actions of servants, if we knew that this generality is not specified concerning the actions of servants. For if it were supposed that there existed something indicating its specification, then adherence to it would not be valid. But the absence of a specifier is speculative, not known.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage explains the sixth condition: textual evidence depends on the absence of a mukhasis (specifier) that would limit a general statement. The author notes that most general statements in the Quran and Sunnah have been specified, so assuming a general remains general is only speculative. The example is the verse 'Creator of everything' (Q. 6:102). This verse is used to argue that Allah creates human actions, but only if we know that the generality is not specified regarding human actions. If a specifier exists, the argument fails. However, the absence of a specifier is not certain but only probable. Thus, textual arguments based on general statements are not certain.

Key Terms
مخصص
عمومات
مظنون
معلوم
خالق كل شيء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A general verse like 'Creator of everything' is automatically certain and applies to all cases without exception.
Correction: The author emphasizes that we cannot be certain the verse is not specified concerning human actions, so its use is only speculative.

Checkpoint
Why is the verse 'Creator of everything' not a certain proof that Allah creates human actions?
A) Because the verse is not authentic.
B) Because the verse might have a specifier that excludes human actions, and its absence cannot be verified.
C) Because human actions are not created.
D) Because the verse is metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
The absence of a specifier for general texts is only probable, so arguments from such texts are not certain but speculative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that even a clear verse like 'Creator of everything' may not give certainty without checking for specifiers. Where in your own understanding of Allah's attributes have you assumed a general verse applies unconditionally? Will you revisit that belief today and ask whether it rests on speculation or certainty?
?
This lesson shows that most general texts have been specified. How does this affect your confidence in arguments you have heard or made that rely on a general Quranic statement? Are you willing to investigate the existence of any specifier before building your faith on that verse?
?
If the absence of a specifier is only speculative, then many theological arguments are not certain. Does this uncertainty trouble you? What concrete step can you take to move from speculative belief to certain knowledge about a specific attribute of Allah?

The Seventh Condition: No Contradictory Textual Evidence
States the seventh condition and its rationale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the absence of contradictory transmitted evidence is a necessary condition for textual evidence to yield certainty.

Core Idea
Contradictory textual evidence reduces certainty to mere probability.

This lesson focuses on the seventh condition for textual evidence to produce certainty: the absence of any contradictory transmitted evidence (al-mu'arid al-naqli). Al-Isfahani explains that verbal proofs can conflict, and when they do, scholars must rely on preponderances that only yield speculative knowledge, not certainty. Understanding this condition clarifies why many textual arguments are considered probabilistic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then the literal translation. Let the author's concise wording sink in before interpreting.
Notice the logical flow: condition → reason → consequence.
2
Identify the condition and its justification
Identify the seventh condition: 'absence of contradictory transmitted evidence.' Then identify the justification: verbal evidences may conflict, leading to preponderances that yield only speculation.
The key terms are al-mu'arid al-naqli, al-ta'arud, al-tarjihat, and al-zann.
3
Reflect on the implications
Think about why this condition matters for kalam and usul al-fiqh. If a contradictory text exists, can certainty be claimed? Why or why not?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع : ويتوقف على عدم المعارض النقلي . لأن الدلائل اللفظية قد يقع فيها التعارض ، ويصار فيها إلى الترجيحات التي لا تفيد إلا الظن .
The seventh: It depends on the absence of contradictory transmitted evidence (al-mu'arid al-naqli). For verbal evidences may have conflict (ta'arud) occurring in them, and recourse is made to preponderances (tarjihat) that only yield speculation (zann).
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh condition: the textual evidence must be free from any contradictory transmitted evidence (naqli). The reason is that verbal evidences (dalail lafziyya) can conflict with each other, and in such cases scholars resort to preponderances (tarjihat) that only produce speculative knowledge (zann), not certainty. Therefore, the absence of such conflict cannot be known with certainty, so the evidence remains probabilistic.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: When two textual evidences conflict, scholars can always determine which one is correct with certainty.
Correction: The passage states that when verbal evidences conflict, recourse is made to preponderances which yield only speculation (zann), not certainty. Therefore, certainty cannot be attained in such cases.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The absence of contradictory transmitted evidence is essential for textual evidence to produce certainty; otherwise, preponderances lead only to speculation.

?

?

Types of Indication in Quranic Expressions
Classification of Quranic indication types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two categories of Quranic indication introduced in the tenth condition and why the author initially rejects one of them.

Core Idea
Quranic wording either precludes its opposite or allows it; the first is false.

The author continues his conditions for theological argumentation from the Quran. Here, he introduces a crucial classification: the indication of Quranic words regarding a claim is either 'an indication from the opposite' (meaning the wording itself prevents its opposite) or 'not preventing from it' (meaning the wording allows its opposite). He immediately declares the first type false, but the argument is cut short. This lesson will help you recognise this classification and grasp its theological significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
The Arabic is brief and incomplete. Read it and the translation to understand the two options presented.
The passage ends mid-sentence; focus on the classification and initial assertion.
2
Identify the two types of indication

'Indication from the opposite' means the text itself automatically excludes the opposite interpretation.
3
Reflect on why the first type might be false
The author says 'the first is false' but the reasoning is cut off. Consider possible reasons: maybe no Quranic verse is so unambiguous that it precludes all alternative readings.
This is a common theme in kalam: Quranic texts often require interpretation and do not mechanically exclude opposites.
4
Connect to the broader argument
This tenth condition is part of a larger discourse on how Quranic verses can be used as evidence. Understanding the author's position helps in evaluating theological proofs.

Arbitrary Commands Imply a World Without a Creator
Additional argument: Jabariyya denial leads to arbitrary commands and negation of Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Jabariyya's denial of rational good and evil logically leads to the absurd conclusion that the possible can exist without a cause, thus negating the existence of God.

Core Idea
Arbitrary divine commands abolish the need for a Creator.

In this passage, the author presents a powerful reductio ad absurdum against the Jabariyya position. By denying intrinsic moral value, they are forced to admit that God's commands are without rationale—specification without a specifier. This, in turn, implies that the contingent universe could exist without a preponderating cause, a direct denial of the argument from contingency for God's existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Let the author's own conclusion sink in before analyzing.
The passage is short but dense; each phrase carries weight.
2
Trace the logical steps
Identify the chain: Jabariyya denies moral good/evil → commands become arbitrary → possible independent of a preponderant → negation of the Maker.
This is a cascade of consequences.
3
Reflect on the absurdity
Consider why the conclusion (negation of the Maker) is unacceptable. The author uses it as a reductio against the Jabariyya view.
4
Assess the objection's strength
Think about whether the Jabariyya could reply. The author claims no one has answered this argument.
This shows the argument's perceived force.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And these two aspects we have singled out for them. And we have not seen anyone from among them circling around them.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage presents an additional objection. The jabariyya deny that actions have intrinsic moral value (no good or evil in the intellect). Therefore, when God commands some actions as obligatory, some as recommended, and some as prohibited, there is no reason for the distinction—it is 'specification without any specifier.' This implies that the possible can occur without a preponderant cause, which leads to the negation of a Creator. The author notes that these two aspects (this and the previous) are ones he has focused on and that no jabari has addressed them.

Correction: The argument is not about God's justice, but about the logical consequence of denying rational moral foundations: it leads to the absurdity that the universe could exist without a cause, i.e., atheism.

Key Takeaway
The denial of objective moral values undermines the rational foundation for divine commands and ultimately the argument for God's existence via contingency.

The Two Meanings of 'Khalq' and Its First Quranic Proof
The author introduces the term 'khalaq' with two meanings and begins the first proof from Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Arabic term 'khalq' carries two distinct meanings—origination and estimation—and see the beginning of its first scriptural proof.

Core Idea
'Khalq' means both bringing into existence and measuring by decree.

This lesson opens a new chapter that examines Quranic evidence for divine creation. Before diving into the verses, the author clarifies the very term 'khalq' (creation), which in Arabic and in theological discourse has two primary senses: bringing something into being and determining its measure. Recognizing this lexical duality is crucial for correctly interpreting the proofs that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then consult the translation. Notice how the author first states the two meanings before presenting evidence.
The passage is incomplete; it cuts off mid-sentence after the first proof begins.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'khalq'
The passage explicitly gives two meanings: iḥdāth (bringing into existence) and taqdīr (estimation/predetermination). Note that these are presented as alternative usages of the same word.
3
Examine the first proof for the first meaning
For the meaning of origination (iḥdāth), the author begins listing proofs. The first proof quoted is Quran 54:49: 'Indeed, We have created everything by a decree.' Reflect on how this verse links creation with predestination.
The proof is not fully presented in this passage, but the verse shows that 'khalq' here includes both aspects.
4
Reflect on the importance of lexical precision
Consider why the author spent time clarifying the term. In kalam, definitions matter because ambiguous terms lead to confused arguments. Knowing that 'khalq' can mean either origination or estimation helps avoid misunderstanding later proofs.
This connects to the broader methodology of ilmul kalam.

Semantic Foundations of 'Khalq' – Four Proofs that it Means Origination
Author presents four proofs that 'khalq' means origination (ihdath) rather than determination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four Qur'anic and traditional proofs that establish the primary meaning of 'khalq' as origination (ihdath) rather than determination (taqdir).

Core Idea
The word 'khalq' primarily means bringing into existence, not just measuring.

Before delving into the theological implications of creation and divine action, the author establishes a crucial linguistic foundation. He argues that the Arabic word 'khalq' is used in two senses, but its primary and most frequent meaning is 'ihdath' (origination). He provides four proofs from the Qur'an and the consensus of early Muslims (Salaf) to demonstrate that 'khalq' in its essential sense refers to bringing something into existence, which is exclusive to Allah. This lesson walks through each proof and its logical force.

Guided Path
1
Understand the twofold usage of 'khalq'
The author states that 'khalq' can mean either ihdath or taqdir. Recognize these two meanings before the proofs.
2
Analyze the first proof: Qur'an 54:49
The verse says 'We created everything with a measure'. If 'khalq' meant taqdir, it would be redundant. Therefore it must be ihdath.
3
Examine the second proof: Qur'an 25:2
The verse pairs 'khalaqa' and 'qaddara'. If they meant the same, it would be redundant, so 'khalaqa' must be ihdath.
4
Consider the third proof: Qur'an 59:24
The restrictive structure 'He is Allah, the Creator' implies that only Allah is the Creator. But if 'khalq' meant taqdir, that would be false because humans also determine.
5
Review the fourth proof: Consensus of the Salaf
The phrase 'There is no creator but Allah' is a foundational tawhid statement. It only makes sense if 'khalq' means bringing into existence, as only Allah does that absolutely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في تقرير هذا المطلوب ، يستدعي تقديم مقدمة في لفظ « الخلق » فنقول : لفظ الخلق ، جاء بمعنى الإحداث تارة ، وبمعنى التقدير أخرى أما الأول : فيدل عليه أمور : الأول : قوله تعالى : ( إنا كل شيء خلقناه بقدر (۱) ) ولو كان الخلق هو التقدير ، لصار معنى الكلام : إنا قدرنا كل شيء . أن الخلق ههنا ليس هو التقدير ، فوجب أن يكون هو الإحداث ، ضرورة أنه لا قائل بالفرق . الثاني : قوله تعالى : ( وخلق كل شيء ، فقدره تقديراً ) والاستدلال كما تقدم . الثالث : قوله تعالى : ( هو الله الخالق (۲) ) وهذا التركيب يفيد الحصر. والخلق بمعنى التقدير غير منحصر ، فوجب أن يكون المدلول عليه بهذا الحصر، هو الخلق . بمعنى الإحداث . الرابع : قول سلف الأمة : لا خالق إلا الله . وهذا الحصر لا يصح إلا إذا كان الخلق بمعنى التكوين والإحداث .
Discussion on establishing this sought matter requires presenting a preliminary on the word 'al-khalq'. So we say: The word 'al-khalq' is used to mean al-ihdath (origination, bringing into existence) at times, and to mean al-taqdir (determination, measuring) at other times. As for the first [meaning], evidence for it includes: First: His statement, Exalted is He: 'Indeed, We have created everything according to a measure' (1). And if 'khalq' were al-taqdir, the meaning of the statement would be 'We have measured everything'. [But] [the apparent meaning] here is not al-taqdir, so it must be al-ihdath, necessarily because there is no proponent of a distinction [without proof]. Second: His statement, Exalted is He: 'And He created everything and determined it with [precise] determination' (2). The inference is as previously. Third: His statement, Exalted is He: 'He is Allah, the Creator' (3). This construction conveys restriction (hasr). And 'khalq' in the sense of al-taqdir is not exclusive [to Allah], so what is indicated by this restriction must be 'khalq' in the sense of al-ihdath. Fourth: The statement of the Salaf of the Ummah: 'There is no creator but Allah.' This restriction is valid only if 'khalq' is in the sense of al-takwin (bringing into being) and al-ihdath.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof passage in which the author lays a semantic foundation. He begins by stating that the discussion requires a preliminary on the word 'khalq'. He then gives the two meanings: al-ihdath (origination) and al-taqdir (determination). He then provides four proofs that 'khalq' is used for the first meaning (ihdath).

First proof: Q. 54:49 – 'Indeed, We have created everything according to a measure.' If 'khalq' meant taqdir, the verse would simply say 'We have measured everything', which is redundant because the verse already contains 'biqadr' (with a measure). So 'khalq' here must mean ihdath. He adds that no one claims a difference between the two meanings without proof.

Second proof: Q. 25:2 – 'And He created everything and determined it with determination.' Here both 'khalaqa' and 'qaddara' appear together. If 'khalaqa' meant taqdir, it would be merely repeating the same idea. So 'khalaqa' must be ihdath.

Third proof: Q. 59:24 – 'He is Allah, the Creator.' The Arabic structure 'Huwa Allahu al-Khaliq' implies restriction (only God is the Creator). But if 'khalq' meant taqdir, then humans also engage in measuring and determination, so the restriction would be false. Therefore 'khaliq' must mean the one who brings into existence (ihdath).

Fourth proof: The consensus statement of the Salaf: 'There is no creator but Allah.' This famous declaration of tawhid would be meaningless if 'khalq' meant taqdir, because then humans could be called creators (in a limited sense). The Salaf's usage confirms that 'khalq' primarily means bringing into being.

Thus, the author establishes that the fundamental meaning of 'khalq' is ihdath, though it can also be used metaphorically for taqdir.

Key Terms
الخلق
الإحداث
التقدير
الحصر
السلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think 'khalq' only means 'taqdir' (determination) because of verses like Q. 3:59, but the author shows that its primary and common meaning is 'ihdath' (origination), and 'taqdir' is a secondary meaning.
Correction: The author demonstrates through four proofs that the fundamental sense of 'khalq' in the Qur'an and Salaf usage is bringing into existence, not merely measuring or determining.

Checkpoint
What is the fourth proof that 'khalq' means ihdath?
A) The verse 'He created everything and determined it'
B) The consensus of the Salaf that 'There is no creator but Allah'
C) The verse 'Indeed, We have created everything with a measure'
D) The restrictive form of 'He is Allah, the Creator'

Key Takeaway
The primary meaning of 'khalq' is origination (ihdath), as shown by four scriptural and traditional proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Salaf declared 'There is no creator but Allah.' Reflect on areas of your life where you might be acting as if you are the creator of your own success, opportunities, or identity. Where do you need to surrender the illusion of self-creation and return to the reality that only Allah brings things into being?
?
The author used logical reasoning and Quranic precision to establish the meaning of a single word. Are you willing to learn the precise meanings of Allah's names and attributes, even when it challenges casual understandings? How might your worship change if you understood 'al-Khaliq' with the depth this lesson provides?

Two Qur'anic Proofs that 'Khalq' Means Taqdir
Author presents two proofs from the Qur'an that 'khalq' can mean determination (taqdir) rather than origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two Qur'anic arguments showing that the word 'khalq' can mean determination (taqdir) rather than origination.

Core Idea
The Qur'an itself proves that 'khalq' includes planning, not just bringing into existence.

This lesson examines two Qur'anic proofs that the word 'khalq' (creation) can carry the meaning of determination (taqdir). The author argues that in certain contexts, 'khalq' must refer to a prior act of measuring or planning, distinct from the act of bringing into existence. By analyzing the verses about Prophet Adam (AS) and the phrase 'best of creators,' we see that 'khalq' encompasses both divine and human creative acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Pay attention to how the author uses each verse to support his point.
2
Examine the first proof from Surah Al-Imran
Focus on Q. 3:59. Notice that 'Be, and he was' refers to origination (ihdath), which occurs after 'khalq'. Therefore 'khalq' must be a prior act of determination.
The author argues that no one posits a third meaning, so it must be taqdir.
3
Examine the second proof from Surah Al-Mu'minun
Focus on Q. 23:14. The phrase 'best of creators' implies others create. But only Allah originates existence. So 'khalq' here must mean determination, in which humans can participate.
This shows 'khalq' has a broader semantic range.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Both proofs end with 'because there is no proponent of a distinction' – meaning the only alternative meaning is taqdir.
This establishes a legitimate secondary meaning without negating the primary meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما مجيء لفظ الخلق بمعنى التقدير . فيدل عليه وجوه : الأول : قوله تعالى : ( إن مثل عيسى عند الله ، كمثل آدم ، خلقه من تراب ، ثم قال له : كن فيكون (۳) ) ولا شك أن قوله : ( كن فيكون ) استعارة عن الإحداث والإيجاد . ثم إنه تعالى نص على أن هذا الإحداث والإيجاد متأخر عن الخلق ، فوجب أن يكون المراد من لفظ الخلق ههنا : شيئاً مغايراً للإحداث والإيجاد . فيكون هو التقدير . لأنه لا قائل بالفرق . الثاني : قوله تعالى : ( فتبارك الله أحسن الخالقين (٤) ) وهذا التركيب يدل على كون غيره خالقاً . وقد دل الدليل على أنه لا موجد إلا الله ، فلا بد وأن يكون المراد من الخلق في هذه الآية : شيئاً سوى الإحداث . فيكون هو التقدير . لأنه لا قائل بالفرق .
As for the occurrence of the word 'al-khalq' in the sense of al-taqdir, evidence for it includes: First: His statement, Exalted is He: 'Indeed, the example of Jesus to Allah is like that of Adam; He created him from dust, then He said to him, "Be," and he was' (3). There is no doubt that His saying, 'Be, and he was' is a metaphor for al-ihdath and al-ijad (origination and bringing into existence). Then He, Exalted, explicitly stated that this al-ihdath and al-ijad is posterior to 'khalq', so what is meant by the word 'khalq' here must be something other than al-ihdath and al-ijad. It is therefore al-taqdir, necessarily because there is no proponent of a distinction [without proof]. Second: His statement, Exalted is He: 'Blessed is Allah, the best of creators' (4). This construction indicates that others besides Him are 'creators' (khaliq). And the evidence has established that there is none who brings into existence (mujid) except Allah, so the intended meaning of 'khalq' in this verse must be something other than al-ihdath. It is therefore al-taqdir, necessarily because there is no proponent of a distinction [without proof].
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is a proof passage in which the author provides evidence for the second meaning of 'khalq' – al-taqdir (determination). He gives two proofs from the Qur'an.

First proof: Q. 3:59 – 'Indeed, the example of Jesus to Allah is like that of Adam; He created him from dust, then said, "Be," and he was.' The author notes that the phrase 'Be, and he was' is a metaphorical expression for actual origination (ihdath and ijad). Allah explicitly states that this origination happened after 'khalq'. Therefore, 'khalq' in this verse must refer to something preceding the existence of Adam – that is, the determination or planning of his creation (taqdir). Since no one posits a third meaning, 'khalq' here must be taqdir.

Second proof: Q. 23:14 – 'Blessed is Allah, the best of creators.' The phrase 'ahsan al-khaliqin' (best of creators) implies that there are other 'creators' besides Allah. But we know from definitive evidence that only Allah truly brings things into existence (mujid). So the word 'khaliq' in this plural form cannot refer to origination; it must refer to something else, namely taqdir (determination), in which humans can participate (e.g., measuring, shaping, designing). Thus, 'khalq' has a meaning other than ihdath, and that is taqdir.

Both proofs conclude with the same phrase: 'because there is no proponent of a distinction' – meaning there is no valid reason to deny this semantic range. The author thus establishes that 'khalq' has a legitimate secondary meaning of determination, without negating its primary meaning of origination.

التقدير
الإحداث
الإيجاد
الخالقون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the word 'khalq' exclusively means creation ex nihilo.
Correction: The author shows that 'khalq' can also refer to the act of determining or measuring, as demonstrated by these two Qur'anic proofs.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that 'khalq' in Q. 3:59 must mean taqdir?
A) Because the verse uses the word 'taqdir' explicitly
B) Because the origination (ihdath) is mentioned after 'khalq', so 'khalq' must be a prior act
C) Because Adam was created from dust, which is a form of measuring
D) Because the verse says 'Be, and he was' which is a metaphor for taqdir

Key Takeaway
The word 'khalq' in the Qur'an can refer to determination (taqdir), as demonstrated by its distinction from origination in two key verses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you plan your day, do you recognize that even your planning is a form of creation (taqdir) that Allah has enabled? How does that change the way you approach your decisions?
?
The verse 'Blessed is Allah, the best of creators' implies that you also create in a sense. Are you using your creative capacity in ways that please Allah, or are you wasting it on what does not matter?
?
If the Qur'an can use the same word 'khalq' to mean both origination and determination, what does that tell you about the depth of Allah's involvement in your life—from the grand act of creation to the fine details of your daily affairs?
