Linguistic Evidences for the Term 'Khalq'
Al-Razi presents three linguistic usages of 'khalq' from Quran and Arabic speech

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three linguistic contexts in which the Arabic term 'khalq' is used, setting the foundation for its theological classification.

Core Idea
The term 'khalq' in Arabic has multiple linguistic shades before it becomes a theological term.

Before diving into the complex theological debates about creation and human actions, Al-Razi first establishes the ordinary Arabic usage of the word 'khalq'. By examining three distinct linguistic contexts—the Quranic story of Jesus creating a bird, the use of 'khalq' for lying, and the everyday speech of the Arabs—he shows that 'khalq' does not always mean 'bringing into existence from nothing'. This lesson explores those three evidences, which are essential for understanding the theological classification that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the three evidences
Read the Arabic passage containing the three linguistic evidences for 'khalq'. Notice the structure: each is introduced with a number (third, fourth, fifth) and consists of a Quranic verse or a linguistic example followed by a brief explanation.
2
Understand the first evidence: Jesus creating birds
The Quran uses 'takhluq' for Jesus' action of forming a bird from clay. This shows 'khalq' can mean shaping or forming from existing material.
3
Understand the second evidence: lying as 'ikhtilaq'
Lying is called 'khalq' and 'ikhtilaq' because the liar mentally 'estimates' or 'fashions' the non-existent as existent. So 'khalq' can mean mental estimation or fabrication.
4
Understand the third evidence: linguistic usage of making garments
Arabs say 'khalaqtuhu' for making something from a material, like shoes from leather. This is consistent with the idea of forming or producing from existing matter.
Notice that none of these examples imply creation from nothing (ex nihilo). This prepares for the next lesson's distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : قوله تعالى : ( وإذ تخلق من الطين كهيئة الطير ) والاستدلال كما تقدم
الرابع : إن الكذب يسمى خلقاً واختلاقاً . قال تعالى : ( إن هذا إلا خلق الأولين ) وقال : ( إن هذا إلا اختلاق ) وقال : ( وتخلقون إفكا ) وما ذاك إلا لأن الكاذب يقدر المعدوم موجوداً في ذهنه أولاً ، ثم يعبر عنه بلفظه
الخامس : إن أهل اللغة يقولون : خلقت من الأديم خفا ، ومن الشقة قميصاً . قال الشاعر : ولا يبط بأيدي الخالقين ، ولا أيدي الخوالق إلا جيد الأدم
وقال : ولأنت تفري ما خلقت ، وبعد ض القوم ، يخلق ، ثم لا يفري وقال الحجاج في خطبته : ( أي لا أهم إلا أمضيته ، ولا أخلق إلا فريت )
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : أما الخلق بمعنى الإحداث والإيجاد . فعندنا : أنه - سبحانه - منفرد به . وأما بمعنى التقدير، فهو أيضاً على ضربين : أحدهما : إحداث الشيء على مقدار مخصوص . والخلق بهذا التفسير يرجع حاصله إلى كيفية مخصوصة في الاحداث. فإذا لم يصح الإحداث إلا من الله تعالى ، فكذلك التقدير بهذا التفسير ، وجب أيضاً أن لا يصح إلا من الله تعالى . والثاني : إن حكم الحاكم بأن ذلك الشيء وقع على ذلك المقدار يسمى تقديراً أيضاً . يقال : السلطان قدر لفلان من الرزق كذا ، ومن المملكة كذا . والتقدير بهذا التفسير يصح صدوره عن العبد
Third: His statement, Exalted: 'And [remember] when you create from clay the likeness of a bird' and the inference as before. Fourth: Lying is called 'khalq' and 'ikhtilaq'. He, Exalted, said: 'This is nothing but the creations of the ancients' and said: 'This is nothing but fabrication' and said: 'And you invent falsehood'. And that is only because the liar estimates the non-existent as existent in his mind first, then expresses it with his word. Fifth: The linguists say: 'I made from hide a khuff, and from a garment a shirt.' The poet said: 'And hands of creators do not spoil, nor hands of creatures except good leather.' And he said: 'And you split what you created, but the people wear out, then do not split.' And al-Hajjaj said in his sermon: 'I do not intend anything except I execute it, and I do not create except I split.' If you understand this, we say: As for 'khalq' in the sense of bringing into existence and origination, we hold that He—Exalted—is unique in it. As for in the sense of estimation, it is also of two types: One: bringing a thing into existence with a specific measure. 'Khalq' in this interpretation essentially reduces to a specific modality in bringing into existence. So if bringing into existence is not valid except from God, then estimation in this interpretation also must not be valid except from God. The second: the judgment of the judge that that thing occurred on that measure is also called 'taqdir'. It is said: 'The sultan apportioned for so-and-so such-and-such provision, and such-and-such kingdom.' Estimation in this interpretation can issue from the servant.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the term 'khalq' (creation/fabrication) as it appears in linguistic and scriptural usage, divided into several points. The author first gives three linguistic evidences: (1) the Qur'anic verse 'And when you create from clay the likeness of a bird' (5:110), where 'takhluq' is used for forming; (2) that lying is called 'khalq' and 'ikhtilaq' because the liar mentally estimates the non-existent as existent; and (3) that Arabs use 'khalaqtu' for making something from a material, as in 'I made a khuff from hide' and in poetry. Then the author summarizes that 'khalq' in the sense of absolute origination (ihdath and ijad) is exclusive to God. But 'khalq' in the sense of estimation (taqdir) divides into two: (a) bringing something into existence with a specific measure, which is essentially a modality of origination and thus also exclusive to God; (b) the judgment that something occurred on that measure (e.g., the sultan apportions provision), which can issue from the servant. This classification is key to the theological debate on human actions: the opponents may accept that God estimates actions but claim humans create them, so the author clarifies the precise meanings.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'khalq' always refers to creating something from nothing (creatio ex nihilo).
Correction: This lesson shows that in ordinary Arabic and even in the Quran, 'khalq' can mean shaping, estimating, or fabricating from existing materials.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is lying called 'khalq' or 'ikhtilaq'?
A) Because the liar creates new realities with words
B) Because the liar estimates the non-existent as existent in his mind first
C) Because lying is a form of art
D) Because the word 'khalq' only applies to negative actions

Key Takeaway
In its linguistic origins, 'khalq' includes shaping, fabricating, and estimating, not only bringing into existence from nothing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about your achievements, do you attribute the 'creation' of outcomes to your own effort alone, or do you recognize that you merely shape what Allah has already decreed? How will you adjust your speech today to reflect His sovereignty?
?
The liar fabricates a reality in his mind before lying. How often do you fabricate excuses or justifications in your own thoughts before acting? Will you commit to examining your inner estimations and aligning them with truth, even when it is uncomfortable?

Theological Classification of Khalq: Creation and Estimation
Al-Razi classifies 'khalq' into absolute creation and estimation, with two subtypes of estimation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi theologically classifies 'khalq' into creation ex nihilo (exclusive to God) and two types of estimation (taqdir), one exclusive to God and one permissible for humans.

Core Idea
Allah alone creates from nothing, but estimation has two kinds: one tied to creation, one open to human judgment.

After establishing the linguistic breadth of 'khalq', Al-Razi now moves to its theological classification. He distinguishes the meaning of absolute origination (ihdath and ijad), which belongs solely to God, from the meaning of estimation (taqdir). Estimation, he says, is further divided into two types: one is the act of bringing something into existence with a specific measure—which, being a modality of creation, is also exclusive to God. The other is the human judgment that something has occurred on a certain measure—like a sultan assigning provisions. This second type can issue from humans. This classification is crucial for the debate on whether humans create their own actions, because it clarifies that human 'creation' is merely estimation and judgment, not true origination.

Guided Path
1
Read the theological classification passage
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'إذا عرفت هذا فنقول' to the end. Identify the main division: khalq as ihdath/ijad and khalq as taqdir.
2
Understand khalq as absolute origination
This meaning (إحداث وإيجاد) is exclusive to God. No human can truly create from nothing.
3
Understand the first type of taqdir
This type is 'bringing something into existence with a specific measure'. Since it's a modality of creation, it also belongs only to God.
4
Understand the second type of taqdir
This is a judgment (hukm) that something occurred on a certain measure. Humans can issue such judgments, e.g., a sultan's apportionment.
This distinction is subtle but crucial: human actions are not creation but estimation in the sense of judgment, while the actual creative act and its precise measure are from Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

Or do you slip into thinking you have control over your own creation? What would it look like to submit your plans to Him as the only one who brings things into being?
?

But do you ever judge yourself or others as if you are the creator of your own reality, when in fact all existence and measure belong to Allah alone? How can you practice seeing your judgments as humble estimations, not absolute decrees?

On the Word 'Every' and the Sunni Response to the Mu'tazila
The author objects to a claim about 'كل' and sets up the issue of specification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dialectical argument about the word 'kull' (every/all) and its implications for the Mu'tazila's use of general verses in the issue of threat.

Core Idea
Probabilistic reasoning about metaphorical words cannot overturn certain matters.

This lesson begins a complex discussion about the word 'kull' (every/all). The author engages with opponents who claim 'kull' is predominantly metaphorical. Through a series of concessions and objections, he establishes the foundations for the Sunni response to the Mu'tazila's argument from generalities regarding divine threat.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses a dialectical style with concessions and objections.
2
Identify the two initial objections
The author raises two objections to the claim that 'kull' is metaphorical. Find them and note how he demands evidence and then concedes but qualifies.
3
Understand the concession about generality
The author concedes that 'kull' indicates generality but argues that the verse is specified because God is a thing and cannot be creator of Himself.
This sets up the need for the discussion about whether God is called 'thing'.
4
Connect to the Mu'tazila's threat argument
The author notes that this reasoning is the basis for Ahl al-Sunna's response to the Mu'tazila's use of generalities in the matter of threat.
This is a key practical implication of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : لا نسلم أن لفظ "كل" مجاز في الأغلب. فما الدليل عليه؟
الثاني : سلمنا أنه كذلك، إلا أنه مجاز متعارف مشهور. فالقول بأنه لا يجوز حمل هذا اللفظ على مثل هذا المجاز، لا يفيد إلا الظن. وهذه المسألة قطعية. والدليل الظني لا يجوز التمسك به في المسألة القطعية. وعلى هذا الحرف تعويل أهل السنة والجماعة، في الجواب عن استدلال المعتزلة بالعمومات في مسألة الوعيد.
سلمنا أن لفظ "كل" يفيد العموم. إلا أن هذه الآية عام، دخله التخصيص، فوجب أن لا يكون حجة. إنما قلنا: إنه عام. دخله التخصيص، لأنه تعالى شيء. ويمتنع أن يكون خالقاً لنفسه. لا يقال: لا تسلم أن هذه الآية قد دخلها التخصيص. وبيانه من وجهين:
الأول: لا نسلم أن اسم الشيء يقع على الله تعالى. ويدل عليه وجوه:
الأول: قوله تعالى: (ليس كمثله شيء) والكاف معناه: المثل. فصار تقدير الآية: ليس مثل مثل الله شيء. ومثل مثل الشيء: عين ذلك الشيء. فكانت هذه الآية دالة على أن الله تعالى لا يقع عليه اسم الشيء وقولهم: الكاف زائدة: ضعيف. لأن الأصل: صون كلام الله عن اللغو.
الثاني: لو كان تعالى مسمى بالشيء، لزم دخول التخصيص، في قوله: (الله خالق كل شيء) بالنسبة إلى الله تعالى. إلا أن مثل هذا التخصيص لا يجوز. وذلك لأن التخصيص إنما يجوز في الشيء الحقير، الذي لا يؤبه به، كما ضربنا من المثال في قولنا: كل هذا الثور أبيض. وإن كان قد حصلت شعيرات سود في جلده. فأما إذا كان الخارج عن صيغة العموم، هو الأعظم الأشرف، فإنه لا يجوز. ومعلوم أن اسم الشيء لو وقع على الله، لكان أشرف الأشياء هو الله. بل كان سائر الأشياء بالنسبة إليه - سبحانه - كالعدم. وعلى هذا التقدير يكون الخارج عن هذا العموم، هو الأكمل الأشرف، والباقي فيه، هو الأخس الأقل. ومثل هذا يكون كذباً.
فثبت: أنه تعالى لو كان مسمى بالشيء، لكانت هذه الآية كذباً. ولما بطل ذلك علمنا أنه تعالى غير مسمى بهذا الاسم.
الثالث: قوله تعالى: (والله الأسماء الحسنى فادعوه بها) دل هذا النص: على أنه يجب أن يدعى الله بالأسماء الحسنة. ولو كان لفظ "الشيء" واقعاً على الله، لجاز أن يدعى الله بهذا الاسم. لكن هذا الاسم ليس من الأسماء الحسنى. فإنه كونه شيئاً، لا يدل على صفة من صفات الكمال والجلال. فجواز دعوة الله بهذا الاسم ينافي ما دل عليه قوله: (والله الأسماء الحسنى فادعوه بها).
فثبت بهذه الوجوه الثلاثة: أنه تعالى لا يطلق عليه اسم الشيء، فلم يلزم كون هذه الآية مخصوصة.
First: We do not concede that the word 'kull' is metaphorical in most cases. What is the evidence for that? Second: We concede that it is so, except that it is a well-known, widespread metaphor. So to say that it is not permissible to apply this word to such a metaphor yields nothing but probability. And this matter is certain. And a probabilistic proof cannot be adhered to in a certain matter. And upon this point relies Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jamā'a in answering the Mu'tazila's argument from generalities in the matter of threat (al-wa'īd). We concede that the word 'kull' indicates generality. Except that this verse is general, specification has entered into it, so it must not be a proof. We only said: it is general, specification has entered into it, because He, exalted, is a thing (shay'), and it is impossible that He be the creator of Himself. It cannot be said: we do not concede that specification has entered this verse. Its explanation is from two aspects: First: We do not concede that the name 'thing' applies to God, exalted. And it is indicated by several proofs: First: His statement (there is nothing like Him) and the kāf means likeness. So the meaning of the verse becomes: there is no likeness of the likeness of God. And the likeness of a thing is the very thing. So this verse indicates that God, exalted, is not called by the name 'thing'. And their saying that the kāf is redundant is weak because the principle is to protect God's speech from futility. Second: If He, exalted, were named by 'thing', then specification would necessarily enter into His statement (God is the creator of every thing) with respect to God, exalted. But such specification is not permissible. That is because specification is only permissible in a lowly thing that is not regarded, as we gave the example in our saying: 'All of this bull is white,' even if there are black hairs on its skin. As for when what is excluded from the generality formula is the greatest, noblest, then it is not permissible. And it is known that if the name 'thing' applied to God, then God would be the noblest of things. Rather, all other things relative to Him—glorified—would be as non-existence. On this estimation, what is excluded from this generality would be the most perfect, noble, and what remains in it would be the most base, insignificant. And such would be false. Thus it is established: if He, exalted, were named by 'thing', this verse would be false. Since that is invalid, we know that He, exalted, is not named by this name. Third: His statement (And to God belong the Most Beautiful Names, so call upon Him by them) indicates that it is obligatory to call upon God by the beautiful names. And if the word 'thing' applied to God, it would be permissible to call upon God by this name. But this name is not among the Most Beautiful Names, because its being a thing does not indicate any attribute of perfection and majesty. So the permissibility of calling upon God by this name contradicts what His statement (And to God belong the Most Beautiful Names, so call upon Him by them) indicates. So by these three proofs it is established that He, exalted, is not called by the name 'thing', and thus it does not follow that this verse is specified.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument concerning the word 'kull' (every/all) and the specification of Quranic verses. The author (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) begins by raising two objections to an opponent's claim that the word 'kull' is predominantly metaphorical. First, he demands evidence for that claim. Second, he concedes that even if it were metaphorical, it is a well-known metaphor, and dismissing it yields only probability, whereas the matter at hand is certain; probabilistic proof cannot be used in certain matters. This, he notes, is the basis of Ahl al-Sunna's response to the Mu'tazila's use of generalities in the issue of threat (al-wa'īd). Then the author concedes that 'kull' indicates generality, but argues that the verse in question (likely 'Allah is the creator of every thing') is general and has been specified. The reason for specification is that God is a thing (shay'), and cannot be the creator of Himself. An objector then says: 'We do not concede that specification has entered this verse.' The author explains the reply from two faces. The first face: we do not concede that the name 'thing' applies to God. He then gives three proofs. (1) The verse 'There is nothing like Him' (42:11): the kaf (like) indicates likeness, so the meaning is 'there is no likeness of the likeness of God'. The likeness of a thing is the thing itself, so the verse denies that God is called 'thing'. The claim that the kaf is redundant is weak because God's speech is free from futility. (2) If God were named 'thing', then the verse 'Allah is the creator of every thing' would require specification excluding God from being created. But such specification is invalid because it would exclude the noblest and most perfect Being, leaving only base things. That would make the verse false. Hence, God is not called 'thing'. (3) The verse 'And to God belong the Most Beautiful Names' obliges calling God by beautiful names. The word 'thing' is not a beautiful name as it implies no perfection. So it cannot be used to call upon God, proving it does not apply to Him. All three proofs establish that God is not called 'thing', so the objection that specification is needed is invalid.

Key Terms

مجاز
عموم
تخصيص
الوعيد

Checkpoint
What is the author's second objection to the claim that 'kull' is metaphorical?
A) The claim has no evidence whatsoever.
B) Even if it is metaphorical, it is a well-known metaphor, so dismissing it yields only probability.
C) It contradicts the Quranic usage.
D) It is only metaphorical in a few cases.

Key Takeaway
The word 'kull' may be metaphorical, but even so, probabilistic arguments cannot overturn certain theological matters; this is the basis for the Sunni response to the Mu'tazila on threat.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological argument that feels convincing, do you ever stop to ask whether its evidence is certain (qatʿī) or merely probable (ẓannī)? How would you distinguish between the two in your own reasoning about Allah's attributes?
?
The author insists that probabilistic reasoning cannot overturn certain matters. Is there any belief about Allah that you hold based on something less than certain—perhaps a common saying or a 'well-known' interpretation? What would it cost you to examine that belief with the same critical spirit as the author?
?
The Mu'tazila relied on a linguistic generality to argue about God's threat. Are there any areas of your relationship with Allah where you have built your hope or fear on a general understanding of a verse, without considering the specifics scholars have derived? Will you commit today to seeking deeper knowledge of those verses so that your certainty is grounded in the full truth?

Three Proofs That God is Not Called 'Thing'
The author gives three proofs that God is not called 'thing'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three proofs that the name 'thing' does not apply to God and why this is important for the doctrine of divine transcendence.

Core Idea
God is not called 'thing' because that implies no perfection and contradicts the Quran.

In this part of the passage, the author responds to the objection that the verse 'Allah is the creator of every thing' requires specification if God is a thing. He argues that God is not called 'thing' at all, and presents three powerful proofs from the Quran and rational reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the three distinct proofs introduced by 'الأول', 'الثاني', 'الثالث'.
2
Examine the first proof from 'There is nothing like Him'
The author argues that the kāf in 'ليس كمثله شيء' means likeness, so the verse denies a likeness of God's likeness, which implies God is not a 'thing'. Understand the reasoning step by step.
The author rejects the interpretation that the kāf is redundant.
3
Examine the second proof concerning the creation verse
If God were called 'thing', then the verse 'Allah is the creator of every thing' would need to specify that God is not created, but specifying the noblest being is impermissible, making the verse false. Hence God is not called 'thing'.
This is a rational argument based on the principle of specification.
4
Examine the third proof from the Most Beautiful Names
The verse commands calling God by the Most Beautiful Names. 'Thing' is not a beautiful name because it implies no perfection. Therefore, God is not called 'thing'.
5
Synthesize the conclusion
All three proofs together establish that God is not called 'thing', so the objection about specification of the creation verse is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : لا نسلم أن لفظ "كل" مجاز في الأغلب. فما الدليل عليه؟
الثاني : سلمنا أنه كذلك، إلا أنه مجاز متعارف مشهور. فالقول بأنه لا يجوز حمل هذا اللفظ على مثل هذا المجاز، لا يفيد إلا الظن. وهذه المسألة قطعية. والدليل الظني لا يجوز التمسك به في المسألة القطعية. وعلى هذا الحرف تعويل أهل السنة والجماعة، في الجواب عن استدلال المعتزلة بالعمومات في مسألة الوعيد.
سلمنا أن لفظ "كل" يفيد العموم. إلا أن هذه الآية عام، دخله التخصيص، فوجب أن لا يكون حجة. إنما قلنا: إنه عام. دخله التخصيص، لأنه تعالى شيء. ويمتنع أن يكون خالقاً لنفسه. لا يقال: لا تسلم أن هذه الآية قد دخلها التخصيص. وبيانه من وجهين:
الأول: لا نسلم أن اسم الشيء يقع على الله تعالى. ويدل عليه وجوه:
الأول: قوله تعالى: (ليس كمثله شيء) والكاف معناه: المثل. فصار تقدير الآية: ليس مثل مثل الله شيء. ومثل مثل الشيء: عين ذلك الشيء. فكانت هذه الآية دالة على أن الله تعالى لا يقع عليه اسم الشيء وقولهم: الكاف زائدة: ضعيف. لأن الأصل: صون كلام الله عن اللغو.

الثالث: قوله تعالى: (والله الأسماء الحسنى فادعوه بها) دل هذا النص: على أنه يجب أن يدعى الله بالأسماء الحسنة. ولو كان لفظ "الشيء" واقعاً على الله، لجاز أن يدعى الله بهذا الاسم. لكن هذا الاسم ليس من الأسماء الحسنى. فإنه كونه شيئاً، لا يدل على صفة من صفات الكمال والجلال. فجواز دعوة الله بهذا الاسم ينافي ما دل عليه قوله: (والله الأسماء الحسنى فادعوه بها).

So to say that it is not permissible to apply this word to such a metaphor yields nothing but probability. And this matter is certain. Except that this verse is general, specification has entered into it, so it must not be a proof. We only said: it is general, specification has entered into it, because He, exalted, is a thing (shay'), and it is impossible that He be the creator of Himself. It cannot be said: we do not concede that specification has entered this verse. Its explanation is from two aspects: First: We do not concede that the name 'thing' applies to God, exalted. And it is indicated by several proofs: First: His statement (there is nothing like Him) and the kāf means likeness. So the meaning of the verse becomes: there is no likeness of the likeness of God. And the likeness of a thing is the very thing. So this verse indicates that God, exalted, is not called by the name 'thing'. And their saying that the kāf is redundant is weak because the principle is to protect God's speech from futility. Second: If He, exalted, were named by 'thing', then specification would necessarily enter into His statement (God is the creator of every thing) with respect to God, exalted. But such specification is not permissible. That is because specification is only permissible in a lowly thing that is not regarded, as we gave the example in our saying: 'All of this bull is white,' even if there are black hairs on its skin. As for when what is excluded from the generality formula is the greatest, noblest, then it is not permissible. And it is known that if the name 'thing' applied to God, then God would be the noblest of things. Rather, all other things relative to Him—glorified—would be as non-existence. On this estimation, what is excluded from this generality would be the most perfect, noble, and what remains in it would be the most base, insignificant. And such would be false. Thus it is established: if He, exalted, were named by 'thing', this verse would be false. Since that is invalid, we know that He, exalted, is not named by this name. Third: His statement (And to God belong the Most Beautiful Names, so call upon Him by them) indicates that it is obligatory to call upon God by the beautiful names. And if the word 'thing' applied to God, it would be permissible to call upon God by this name. But this name is not among the Most Beautiful Names, because its being a thing does not indicate any attribute of perfection and majesty. So the permissibility of calling upon God by this name contradicts what His statement (And to God belong the Most Beautiful Names, so call upon Him by them) indicates. So by these three proofs it is established that He, exalted, is not called by the name 'thing', and thus it does not follow that this verse is specified.

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument concerning the word 'kull' (every/all) and the specification of Quranic verses. The author (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) begins by raising two objections to an opponent's claim that the word 'kull' is predominantly metaphorical. First, he demands evidence for that claim. Then the author concedes that 'kull' indicates generality, but argues that the verse in question (likely 'Allah is the creator of every thing') is general and has been specified. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'thing' is a neutral term applicable to God, as He is a 'thing' in the sense of an existent.
Correction: The author argues that 'thing' is not a name of God because it implies no perfection and contradicts the Quranic verses that deny His likeness and command using beautiful names.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a reason the author gives that 'thing' is not among the Most Beautiful Names?
A) It is a borrowed term from creation.
B) It does not indicate any attribute of perfection or majesty.
C) It is a metaphor.
D) It implies that God is created.

Key Takeaway
God is not called 'thing' because the Quran and reason show that this name implies no perfection and would lead to contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If calling God 'thing' is impermissible because it implies no perfection, then what names do you use for God in your daily life? Are they the most beautiful names or generic terms? Examine your own du'ā' and dhikr: do you call upon Him with names that reflect His majesty, or do you take His greatness for granted? Commit to learning one new beautiful name this week and using it consciously.
?
The author argues that specification is only permissible for lowly things. In your own life, do you sometimes treat God as just another 'thing' among many, giving Him a place but not the highest? Where have you allowed your attention to be consumed by what is lesser, forgetting that He is the Greatest? Identify one area where you prioritize worldly concerns over your relationship with God and make a concrete change.
?
God commands you to call upon Him by His most beautiful names. When was the last time you paused to reflect on the meaning of a name like Al-Azeez (The Mighty) or Al-Jabbar (The Compeller)? How does that name change how you approach Him in supplication and in your struggles? This week, before a prayer, meditate on one of His names and let it shape your conversation with Him.

The Inclusiveness of 'Kull'
Author defends the inclusiveness of 'kull' using a master-slave example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the logical force of the word 'kull' (all) in indicating total inclusion, using the example of a master freeing all slaves.

Core Idea
The word 'kull' necessarily indicates all-inclusiveness, as shown by everyday usage.

The opponent begins by denying that the word 'kull' (all) implies total inclusion of every individual. The author responds with a concrete example: if a master says 'All my slaves are free,' then clearly every slave is freed. This establishes that the word does carry the meaning of total inclusion. However, the opponent then objects that such indication is merely conjectural, but the author accepts this, noting that all verbal indications yield only conjecture.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage where the opponent denies the inclusiveness of 'kull' and the author gives the example of the master freeing slaves.
Notice how the author uses a simple everyday scenario as proof.
2
Understand the opponent's second objection
The opponent then says the indication is only conjectural. The author agrees, explaining that all verbal evidences are conjectural.
This is important: the author does not claim certainty from wording alone.
3
Reflect on the author's strategy
The author does not refute the conjectural nature but frames it as acceptable within the methodology of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: ولا نسلم أن لفظة كل تفيد الاستغراق. قلنا: والدليل عليه: أن السيد إذا قال: كل عبيدي أحرار: عنق الكل. قوله: (دلالة لفظ كل على الاستغراق دلالة ظنية). قلنا: عندنا: أن الدلائل اللفظية لا تفيد إلا الظن. قوله: (هذا عام، دخله التخصيص، فلا يكون حجة). قلنا: لا نسلم أنه دخله التخصيص، ولا نسلم وقوع لفظ الشيء على الله سبحانه وقد سبق تقريره. سلمنا: أنه عام دخله التخصيص، لكن لا نسلم أنه لا يبقى حجة. ويدل عليه وجهان: الأول: إن العام هو الذي تناول صورتين فصاعداً من غير حصر. ودلالته على هذه الصورة وعلى تلك الصورة إما أن تتوقف كل واحدة منهما على الأخرى فيلزم الدور، أو تتوقف إحداهما على الأخرى ولا تتوقف الأخرى على الأولى فيلزم الترجيح لا المرجح، أو لا تتوقف واحدة منهما على الأخرى. وحينئذ لا يلزم من زوال إحداهما زوال الأخرى. وهو المطلوب. الثاني: إن المقتضى لثبوت الحكم قائم في غير محل التخصيص. لأن الصيغة العامة متناولة له، وتلك الصيغة مقتضية لذلك الحكم، والعارض الموجود وهو التخصيص لا يصلح معارضاً لهذا المقتضى. لأن انتفاء الحكم في إحدى الصورتين لا ينافي ثبوته في الصور الأخرى، فوجب الحكم بانتفاء دلالة العام المخصوص على الحكم في غير محل التخصيص. قوله: (الدلائل المخصصة المتصلة موجودة). قلنا: لا نسلم. قوله: (هذه الآية مذكورة في معرض المدح والثناء، وكونه تعالى خالقاً لأعمال العباد يوجب الذم). قلنا: لا نسلم أن كونه تعالى خالقاً لأعمال العباد يوجب الذم. وسيأتي الاستقصاء في هذا المقام عند الجواب عن الشبه العقلية.
His statement: And we do not concede that the word 'kull' indicates inclusiveness. We say: The evidence for it is that if the master says: 'All my slaves are free,' then all are freed. His statement: (The indication of the word 'kull' for inclusiveness is a conjectural indication). We say: According to us, verbal indications only produce conjecture. His statement: (This is general, it has been specified, so it is not a proof). We say: We do not concede that it has been specified, and we do not concede that the word 'thing' is applied to God, and its confirmation has preceded. We concede: that it is general and has been specified, but we do not concede that it remains not a proof. Two aspects indicate this: First: The general is that which includes two or more cases without restriction. Its indication for this case and that case either each depends on the other, leading to circularity; or one depends on the other and not vice versa, leading to preponderance without a preponderant; or neither depends on the other. Then removal of one does not necessitate removal of the other. That is what is sought. Second: The basis for establishing the ruling exists in the non-specified part. Because the general form encompasses it, and that form requires that ruling, and the existing obstacle, which is specification, is not suitable to oppose this requirement. Because the negation of the ruling in one case does not negate its existence in other cases, so it is necessary to judge that the specified general does not indicate the ruling in the non-specified part. His statement: (The connected specifying evidences exist). We say: We do not concede. His statement: (This verse is mentioned in the context of praise and commendation, and God being the Creator of the servants' actions necessitates blame). We say: We do not concede that God being the Creator of the servants' actions necessitates blame. And a detailed investigation will come on this topic when responding to the rational doubts.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is a series of objections and answers about the use of the word 'kull' (all) and the nature of general rulings ('umum). The opponent challenges that 'kull' does not imply inclusiveness, but the author gives the example of a master freeing all slaves as evidence. When the opponent says the indication is only conjectural, the author accepts that verbal indications are conjectural. The opponent then argues that a general text that has been specified is no longer a proof. The author refutes with two reasons: first, the general's application to each case is independent, so specification for one does not affect others; second, the basis for the ruling exists in the unspecified part, and specification does not contradict that. Finally, objections about specifying evidences and the context of praise are rejected, with a promise of further discussion.

كل
الاستغراق
دلالة ظنية
دلائل لفظية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the word 'kull' (all) does not necessarily imply every individual.
Correction: The author demonstrates with the master-slave example that 'kull' does indicate total inclusion, and the opponent's concession that the indication is conjectural does not negate its implication.

Checkpoint
What example does the author give to prove that 'kull' indicates inclusiveness?
A) A master freeing all his slaves
B) A king addressing his subjects
C) A general commanding his army
D) A father speaking to his children

Key Takeaway
The word 'kull' inherently indicates all-inclusiveness, even though its indication is conjectural like all verbal proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author admits verbal indications are only conjectural. In your own life, do you treat Allah's words as certain directives or as open to convenient reinterpretation? How would your daily actions change if you took every 'all' in the Quran as unequivocally binding?
?
If a master's word 'all' frees every slave, what does your use of 'all' in promises to Allah and to others release or bind? Are you as careful with your words as you demand Allah's words to be?

Specified General Texts Remain Proofs
Author gives two reasons why specified general texts remain proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two arguments the author provides to prove that a general text that has been specified (mukhassas) still serves as a proof for the remaining cases.

Core Idea
Specification does not strip a general text of its probative force for the unspecified cases.

The opponent argues that if a general text has been specified, it can no longer be used as a proof. The author first denies that the text has been specified, but then concedes the point for the sake of argument. He then presents two powerful arguments to show that specification does not invalidate the general text as a proof for the remaining cases. The first argument is based on the independence of each case included in the general, and the second is based on the continued existence of the basis for the ruling in the unspecified part.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
Read where the opponent claims the general text is no longer a proof after specification, and the author's initial denial.
Note that the author then concedes for argument's sake.
2
Study the first argument
The author presents the first argument: the general includes multiple cases, and the indication for each case is independent. So specifying one case does not affect the others.
Think of the logical possibilities: dependence, circularity, etc.
3
Study the second argument
The second argument: the basis for the ruling (al-muqtada) remains in the non-specified part, and specification does not contradict that.
This is a more direct argument from legal theory.
4
Reflect on the overall conclusion
The author concludes that the specified general still indicates the ruling for the unspecified cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: ولا نسلم أن لفظة كل تفيد الاستغراق. قلنا: والدليل عليه: أن السيد إذا قال: كل عبيدي أحرار: عنق الكل. قوله: (دلالة لفظ كل على الاستغراق دلالة ظنية). قلنا: عندنا: أن الدلائل اللفظية لا تفيد إلا الظن. قوله: (هذا عام، دخله التخصيص، فلا يكون حجة). قلنا: لا نسلم أنه دخله التخصيص، ولا نسلم وقوع لفظ الشيء على الله سبحانه وقد سبق تقريره. سلمنا: أنه عام دخله التخصيص، لكن لا نسلم أنه لا يبقى حجة. ويدل عليه وجهان: الأول: إن العام هو الذي تناول صورتين فصاعداً من غير حصر. ودلالته على هذه الصورة وعلى تلك الصورة إما أن تتوقف كل واحدة منهما على الأخرى فيلزم الدور، أو تتوقف إحداهما على الأخرى ولا تتوقف الأخرى على الأولى فيلزم الترجيح لا المرجح، أو لا تتوقف واحدة منهما على الأخرى. وحينئذ لا يلزم من زوال إحداهما زوال الأخرى. وهو المطلوب. الثاني: إن المقتضى لثبوت الحكم قائم في غير محل التخصيص. لأن الصيغة العامة متناولة له، وتلك الصيغة مقتضية لذلك الحكم، والعارض الموجود وهو التخصيص لا يصلح معارضاً لهذا المقتضى. لأن انتفاء الحكم في إحدى الصورتين لا ينافي ثبوته في الصور الأخرى، فوجب الحكم بانتفاء دلالة العام المخصوص على الحكم في غير محل التخصيص. قوله: (الدلائل المخصصة المتصلة موجودة). قلنا: لا نسلم. قوله: (هذه الآية مذكورة في معرض المدح والثناء، وكونه تعالى خالقاً لأعمال العباد يوجب الذم). قلنا: لا نسلم أن كونه تعالى خالقاً لأعمال العباد يوجب الذم. وسيأتي الاستقصاء في هذا المقام عند الجواب عن الشبه العقلية.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The first proof shows that each case's indication is independent. In your daily worship, is there an area where you let one deficiency (like imperfect prayer) affect your whole attitude toward all acts of obedience? How can you begin to treat each act of worship as having its own independent basis, free from the influence of others?

Objections on Specifying Evidences and Praise Context
Author briefly rejects two objections about specifying evidences and praise context.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's brief rejections of two further objections: that specifying evidences exist and that the verse's context of praise negates God being the creator of actions.

Core Idea
The author rejects without elaboration the claims that specifying evidences exist and that God creating actions leads to blame.

After the detailed defense of specified general texts, the opponent raises two quick objections. First, he claims that there are already specifying evidences connected to the text. Second, he argues that the verse under discussion is set in a context of praise, so it cannot imply that God is the creator of bad actions, which would warrant blame. The author simply denies both assertions, promising a more thorough treatment later when discussing rational doubts.

Guided Path
1
Read the first objection and response
The opponent claims there are connected specifying evidences. The author simply says 'We do not concede.'
This is a placeholder response; no detailed refutation here.
2
Read the second objection and response
The opponent argues that the verse's context of praise conflicts with God creating blameworthy actions. The author also denies that God creating actions necessitates blame.
The author promises a full discussion later.
3
Reflect on the author's strategy
The author defers detailed responses to later sections, showing that these objections are not fully addressed here.
This indicates the interconnectedness of the arguments.

The Second Objection: Creation as Estimation Only by God
Presents the second objection: creation as estimation only by God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the definition of creation as estimation (taqdīr) implies that only God can truly create, and human actions fall short.

Core Idea
True creation requires perfect estimation, which only God possesses.

In defending the uniqueness of divine creation, the author introduces a crucial definition: creation is precise estimation suited to purpose. This sets a standard that only the all-knowing, all-powerful Creator can meet. Let's see how this argument challenges human claims to creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection passage
Carefully read the Arabic passage presenting the second objection. Notice how it defines creation as estimation.
The definition is key to the argument.
2
Understand the definition of creation
Creation is defined as executing an action according to a precise measure (taqdīr) consistent with purpose and benefit.
This is a technical definition in kalam.
3
See why only God qualifies
Only God, being omniscient and omnipotent, can always achieve perfect estimation. Humans often estimate but fail to execute accordingly.
4
Grasp the implication
Therefore, human actions cannot truly be called 'creation' in the same sense as God's. This objection undermines any argument that humans create like God.
This sets up the need for answers later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن الخلق عبارة عن التقدير - على ما تقدم ذكره ـ والتقدير عبارة عن إيقاع الفعل على مقدار مخصوص مطابق للغرض والمصلحة . وهذا إنما يتأتى في حق العالم ، الذي لا يجهل شيئا ، والقادر الذي لا يعجز عن شيء . أما العبد فإنه في الأكثر يقدر شيئاً ، ويجيء فعله بخلاف ما قدره . وحينئذ لا يكون خلق العبد ، مثلا لخلق الله

الثالث : إن قوله تعالى : ( خلقوا كخلقه ( إنما يتناول من كان خلقه ، مساوياً لكل ما كان خلقاً الله تعالى . لأن قوله : ( كخلقه ) يتناول جميع مخلوقات الله تعالى. ومعلوم أن العبد ليس كذلك ، فوجب أن لا يندرج تحت النص

أعلم .

•

وبقية الأسئلة على هذه الحجة، كما تقدم على الحجة الأولى . والله

والجواب عن السؤال الأول : إن الحركة عبارة عن حصول الجوهر في حيز ، بعد أن كان في حيز آخر . هذا هو المعقول من ماهية الحركة وحقيقتها . فإذا فعل العبد مثل هذا الشيء ، ثم إنه تعالى أيضا فعله مثله ، فحينئذ يكون خلق العبد مثلاً لخلق الله تعالى ، على هذا التقدير

وأما السؤال الثاني . فجوابه : إن أفعال العباد ، وإن كانت لا توجد على وفق تقديرهم على الدوام، إلا أنها قد توجد على وفق تقديرهم في بعض الأوقات . وحينئذ يكون ذلك الخلق في تلك المرة ، مثلاً لخلق الله

وأما السؤال الثالث . فجوابه : إن قوله : « كخلقه ، لا يوجب العموم . لأنه إشارة إلى المصور، فيكفي في العمل به ثبوته في صورة واحدة. فإذا كان خلق العبد مثلاً لخلق الله تعالى مرة واحدة ، وقد صدق عليه في تلك المرة أنه خلق مثل خلق الله تعالى، فحينئذ يندرج تحت النص . والله أعلم .
Second: Verily, 'creation' (al-khalq) is an expression for 'estimation' (al-taqdīr) – as previously mentioned – and 'estimation' is an expression for executing an action according to a specified measure consistent with purpose and benefit. This is only attainable in the case of the Knower who is ignorant of nothing, and the Powerful who is incapable of nothing. As for the servant (al-ʿabd), most of the time he estimates something, yet his action comes out contrary to what he estimated. Hence, the creation of the servant is not, for example, like the creation of God.

Third: Verily, His saying, exalted: 'They create as His creation' (khalaqū ka-khalqihi) only includes one whose creation is equal to everything that was God's creation. Because His saying 'as His creation' encompasses all of God's creations. It is known that the servant is not like that, so he must not be included under the text.

[A note: 'I know.' Then a bullet.]

And the rest of the questions on this proof, as preceded on the first proof. And God [knows best].

And the answer to the first question: Movement (al-ḥarakah) is an expression for a substance (jawhar) occupying a space after it was in another space. This is the intelligible meaning of the essence and reality of movement. So if the servant does such a thing, and then God also does the like of it, then on this estimation the servant's creation is an example of the creation of God.

As for the second question, its answer: The actions of servants, though they do not always exist according to their estimation, yet they may exist according to their estimation at some times. Then that creation at that time is an example of the creation of God.

As for the third question, its answer: His saying 'as His creation' does not entail generality, because it refers to the form (al-muṣawwar); so it suffices for acting upon it that it is established in one instance. So if the servant's creation is an example of God's creation once, and it is true of him at that time that he created like God's creation, then he is included under the text. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage presents two objections (the second and third) to an earlier argument that human actions are not truly created because creation is estimation (taqdīr). The author first defines creation as precise estimation suited to purpose, which only God, who is omniscient and omnipotent, can achieve. Humans often estimate but produce contrary results, so their acts are not truly 'creation' like God's. The third objection cites the Quranic verse 'They create as His creation' (implying anyone whose creation is equal to all God's creations), but humans do not meet that standard. Then the answers are given: The answer to the first question (not stated) is that movement is defined as a substance changing location; if both God and the servant do the same movement, then the servant's creation matches God's. The second answer concedes that though humans often fail, they sometimes succeed in acting according to their estimation; in such cases their creation resembles God's. The third answer argues that 'as His creation' does not require universal equality; it refers to a specific form, so even one instance suffices for the verse to apply. The passage defends the possibility of human creation being comparable to God's in some respects, but the overall context (from other arguments) still denies that humans are true creators in the absolute sense.

Key Terms
خلق
تقدير
عبد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that human estimation and action, when they match, constitute creation equal to God's.
Correction: The passage argues that humans generally fail to match estimation and action, so even when they do, it is not a standard like God's perfect and constant match.

Checkpoint
According to the second objection, why is human creation not like God's creation?
A) Because humans do not have the power to create.
B) Because humans often estimate one thing but their action comes out differently.
C) Because creation is impossible for humans.
D) Because the Quran says so.

Key Takeaway
Creation as estimation is a divine prerogative because only God's knowledge and power are perfect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you carefully planned something, yet the outcome was completely different from what you intended. How does this experience help you understand the difference between human 'creation' and God's perfect estimation described in this lesson? What does this teach you about relying on your own plans versus trusting Allah's decree?
?
If only God can perfectly estimate and execute according to purpose and benefit, then what does that say about your own attempts to control the outcomes of your life? Are there areas where you are still trying to be the 'creator' instead of surrendering to the true Creator? What would it look like to hand over that control today?
?
This lesson defines creation as precise estimation that matches purpose and benefit. God's creation of you was not accidental — it was exactly estimated for a purpose. How does this truth challenge any feelings of meaninglessness or self-doubt you may carry? Will you live today as someone who was perfectly estimated by the All-Knowing and All-Powerful?

The Third Objection: The Verse 'As His Creation' Implies Universal Equality
Presents the third objection: Quranic verse requires equality with all of God's creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection based on the Quranic verse 'They create as His creation' and why it excludes human acts from being truly like God's creation.

Core Idea
The verse 'as His creation' requires equality with all of God's creation, which humans cannot achieve.

The second objection uses a Quranic verse to argue that if humans were creators, their creation would have to be equal to God's creation in every respect. Since this is impossible, the verse actually supports the idea that humans are not creators. Let's examine this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the third objection passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Note the Quranic citation and how it is interpreted.
The verse is from the Quran, but it's used as a proof text.
2
Understand the interpretation of 'as His creation'
The phrase is taken to encompass all of God's creations. So any claim of 'creating like God' must match every aspect of divine creation.
This is a specific interpretation, not the only one.
3
Apply the logic to humans
Humans cannot create all types of things, nor can they create with the same perfection. Therefore, humans are not included under this verse as creators equal to God.
4
Draw the conclusion
Thus, the verse does not support the idea that humans create like God; rather, it sets a standard they cannot meet.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن الخلق عبارة عن التقدير - على ما تقدم ذكره ـ والتقدير عبارة عن إيقاع الفعل على مقدار مخصوص مطابق للغرض والمصلحة . وهذا إنما يتأتى في حق العالم ، الذي لا يجهل شيئا ، والقادر الذي لا يعجز عن شيء . أما العبد فإنه في الأكثر يقدر شيئاً ، ويجيء فعله بخلاف ما قدره . وحينئذ لا يكون خلق العبد ، مثلا لخلق الله

الثالث : إن قوله تعالى : ( خلقوا كخلقه ( إنما يتناول من كان خلقه ، مساوياً لكل ما كان خلقاً الله تعالى . لأن قوله : ( كخلقه ) يتناول جميع مخلوقات الله تعالى. ومعلوم أن العبد ليس كذلك ، فوجب أن لا يندرج تحت النص

أعلم .

•

وبقية الأسئلة على هذه الحجة، كما تقدم على الحجة الأولى . والله

والجواب عن السؤال الأول : إن الحركة عبارة عن حصول الجوهر في حيز ، بعد أن كان في حيز آخر . هذا هو المعقول من ماهية الحركة وحقيقتها . فإذا فعل العبد مثل هذا الشيء ، ثم إنه تعالى أيضا فعله مثله ، فحينئذ يكون خلق العبد مثلاً لخلق الله تعالى ، على هذا التقدير

وأما السؤال الثاني . فجوابه : إن أفعال العباد ، وإن كانت لا توجد على وفق تقديرهم على الدوام، إلا أنها قد توجد على وفق تقديرهم في بعض الأوقات . وحينئذ يكون ذلك الخلق في تلك المرة ، مثلاً لخلق الله

وأما السؤال الثالث . فجوابه : إن قوله : « كخلقه ، لا يوجب العموم . لأنه إشارة إلى المصور، فيكفي في العمل به ثبوته في صورة واحدة. فإذا كان خلق العبد مثلاً لخلق الله تعالى مرة واحدة ، وقد صدق عليه في تلك المرة أنه خلق مثل خلق الله تعالى، فحينئذ يندرج تحت النص . والله أعلم .
Second: Verily, 'creation' (al-khalq) is an expression for 'estimation' (al-taqdīr) – as previously mentioned – and 'estimation' is an expression for executing an action according to a specified measure consistent with purpose and benefit. This is only attainable in the case of the Knower who is ignorant of nothing, and the Powerful who is incapable of nothing. As for the servant (al-ʿabd), most of the time he estimates something, yet his action comes out contrary to what he estimated. Hence, the creation of the servant is not, for example, like the creation of God.

Third: Verily, His saying, exalted: 'They create as His creation' (khalaqū ka-khalqihi) only includes one whose creation is equal to everything that was God's creation. Because His saying 'as His creation' encompasses all of God's creations. It is known that the servant is not like that, so he must not be included under the text.

[A note: 'I know.' Then a bullet.]

And the rest of the questions on this proof, as preceded on the first proof. And God [knows best].

And the answer to the first question: Movement (al-ḥarakah) is an expression for a substance (jawhar) occupying a space after it was in another space. This is the intelligible meaning of the essence and reality of movement. So if the servant does such a thing, and then God also does the like of it, then on this estimation the servant's creation is an example of the creation of God.

As for the second question, its answer: The actions of servants, though they do not always exist according to their estimation, yet they may exist according to their estimation at some times. Then that creation at that time is an example of the creation of God.

As for the third question, its answer: His saying 'as His creation' does not entail generality, because it refers to the form (al-muṣawwar); so it suffices for acting upon it that it is established in one instance. So if the servant's creation is an example of God's creation once, and it is true of him at that time that he created like God's creation, then he is included under the text. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage presents two objections (the second and third) to an earlier argument that human actions are not truly created because creation is estimation (taqdīr). The author first defines creation as precise estimation suited to purpose, which only God, who is omniscient and omnipotent, can achieve. Humans often estimate but produce contrary results, so their acts are not truly 'creation' like God's. The third objection cites the Quranic verse 'They create as His creation' (implying anyone whose creation is equal to all God's creations), but humans do not meet that standard. Then the answers are given: The answer to the first question (not stated) is that movement is defined as a substance changing location; if both God and the servant do the same movement, then the servant's creation matches God's. The second answer concedes that though humans often fail, they sometimes succeed in acting according to their estimation; in such cases their creation resembles God's. The third answer argues that 'as His creation' does not require universal equality; it refers to a specific form, so even one instance suffices for the verse to apply. The passage defends the possibility of human creation being comparable to God's in some respects, but the overall context (from other arguments) still denies that humans are true creators in the absolute sense.

خلق
كخلقه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if the Quran says some create like God, then human creation is exactly like God's creation.
Correction: The passage argues that the verse requires full equality with all of God's creation, which is impossible for humans.

Checkpoint
According to the third objection, why does the verse 'They create as His creation' not include human actions?
A) Because humans cannot create anything at all.
B) Because the verse refers only to angels.
C) Because 'as His creation' requires equality with all of God's creation, which humans lack.
D) Because the verse is metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic comparison demands full equality, which humans cannot meet, so the verse does not support human creation being like God's.

Answers to the Three Questions on Human Creation
Answers the three questions defending human creation's comparability to God's.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three answers provided to the objections, showing how human actions can be comparable to God's creation in certain respects.

Core Idea
Human creation can be like God's in specific instances, not universal equality.

Having presented the objections, the author now offers answers. He deals with three questions: the nature of movement, the occasional match of estimation and action, and the interpretation of the Quranic verse. These answers defend the possibility that human actions can, in some sense, be considered creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the answers passage
Read the Arabic passage containing the answers to the three questions. Note how each answer builds a case.
The answers are responses to objections not fully stated here.
2
Understand the answer to the first question
Movement is defined as a substance moving from one space to another. If both God and the servant do the same movement, then the servant's creation matches God's in that instance.
This relies on a specific definition of movement.
3
Understand the answer to the second question
Though humans often fail to match estimation, they sometimes do. In those cases, their creation is like God's.
This concedes occasional similarity.
4
Understand the answer to the third question
The verse 'as His creation' does not require universal equality; it refers to a specific form (al-muṣawwar), so even one instance of similarity suffices for the verse to apply.

Defending Metaphorical Interpretation of Human Agency
The author answers two objections about language and authority in interpreting the verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the metaphorical interpretation of verses attributing acts to humans against objections from language and grammar.

Core Idea
Metaphor is preferable to homonymy in resolving linguistic objections.

In this passage, the author tackles two objections that challenge the standard interpretation of verses describing human acts. One objection claims that the Qur'anic attribution of worship and carving to humans implies they are creators; the author defers the rational answer while providing a linguistic response. The second objection disputes a grammatical point, which the author refutes by appealing to the authority of the grammarian Sibawayh and the principle that metaphorical reading is superior to assuming multiple meanings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two objections
Read the passage and find the two objections raised by the opponents. Notice that they concern both rational interpretation and grammatical analysis.
The first objection is about attribution indicating origination; the second is about the word 'ما' not being an infinitive.
2
Analyze the responses
Observe how the author responds: the first answer is deferred to later discussion of intellectual doubts, while the second is answered immediately by citing Sibawayh and advocating metaphorical interpretation.
The author prioritizes grammatical authority over speculative objection.
3
Understand the principle of metaphor vs. homonymy
The author states that metaphor (majaz) is better than homonymy (ishtirak). Reflect on why this principle is used to defend the interpretation of the contested verses.
This principle comes from usul al-fiqh and shows preference for a single flexible meaning over multiple rigid meanings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : أما قوله : إضافة العبادة والنحت إلى العباد يدل على كون العبد محدثاً فجوابه : سيجيء عند الجواب عن شبههم العقلية . وقوله : لا نسلم أن هذه الكلمة في تقدير المصدر ، قلنا : هذا مذهب سيبويه وقوله حجة . وأما الآيات التي أوردوها فتحملها على المجاز . وقد بينا في أصول الفقه : أن المجاز خير من الاشتراك ، وقولهم يقتضي كون هذا اللفظ مشتركاً بين المصدر وبين المفعول . والله أعلم
The answer: As for his saying: 'The attribution of worship and carving to the servants indicates that the servant is an originator,' the answer to it will come at the reply to their intellectual doubts. And his saying: 'We do not accept that this word is in the estimation of the infinitive,' we say: This is the doctrine of Sibawayh, and his statement is an argument. And as for the verses they adduced, they are interpreted metaphorically. And we have shown in the principles of jurisprudence that metaphor is better than homonymy, and their statement necessitates that this word is homonymous between the infinitive and the object. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a response to two objections. The first objection claimed that the Qur'anic attribution of worship and carving to humans shows that humans are the originators of those acts. The author defers the full answer to the section on intellectual doubts, suggesting that a rational argument will refute it. The second objection denied that the word 'ما' could be considered an infinitive. The author retorts that this is the position of Sibawayh, the renowned grammarian, and his view is authoritative. Furthermore, any verses the opponents cite to support their interpretation should be understood metaphorically. The author references the principle from usul al-fiqh that metaphorical interpretation (majaz) is preferable to assuming homonymy (ishtirak). The opponents' demand that the word be taken as a homonym between infinitive and object is less acceptable than interpreting it flexibly. Thus the linguistic argument is reinforced.

إضافة
المجاز
الاشتراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: The author is evading the first objection by deferring it, implying weakness.
Correction: The author defers the rational answer but immediately provides a linguistic response, showing that the objection can be answered on multiple fronts.

Checkpoint

A) Literal meaning
B) Metaphor
C) Context
D) Grammatical analysis

Key Takeaway

The Meaning of 'Fi' and Rebuttal of an Objection about the Lam
The author defines the common meaning of 'fi' and rebuts an objection about the lam of benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the common meaning of the Arabic preposition 'في' and how a specific objection regarding the lam of benefit is rebutted using a Qur'anic counterexample.

Core Idea
The word 'في' means containment; the lam does not always indicate benefit.

This passage delves into the linguistic principle that words often have a common core meaning. The author argues that 'في' signifies containment, covering both physical and metaphorical uses. He then addresses an objection that the lam (ل) indicates benefit, using a verse about painful punishment to show that lam can also indicate possession in harmful contexts.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle of default usage
The author begins by stating that the default is no homonymy (اشتراك); metaphor requires a common meaning. Understand that words are not assumed to have multiple unrelated meanings unless evidence suggests otherwise.
This is a key principle in usul al-fiqh and Arabic linguistics.
2
Identify the common meaning of 'في'
The common meaning is containment: the contained is inside the container, whether it permeates (like an accident in its substance) or is placed (like water in a jug). Notice the two examples.
Accident (عرض) refers to a non-essential attribute; substance (محل) is the subject.
3
Apply the meaning to the Qur'anic verse
The author states that this common meaning must include all types of 'في', referencing the verse 'خلق لكم ما في الأرض جميعاً' (He created for you all that is in the earth). This shows the broader application.
The verse is used to demonstrate the generality of the meaning.
4
Analyze the objection and its rebuttal
An opponent claims that the lam (ل) in the verse indicates benefit (لام لعود المنفعة). The author rebuts by citing 'ولهم عذاب أليم' (And for them is a painful punishment), where the lam (لهم) indicates possession but not benefit. Thus the lam does not always signify benefit.
This rebuttal uses a counterexample to invalidate the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في المحل . والأصل عدم الاشتراك . والمجاز. يوجب كون هذا اللفظ موضوعاً بإزاء القدر المشترك بينهما . وذلك القدر المشترك هو أن يكون المظروف حاصلاً في داخل الظرف ، سواء كان سارياً فيه ، كما في العرض والمحل ، أو موضوعاً في جوفه ، كما في الماء والكوز . وإذا جعلنا لفظ « في » بإزاء هذا المعنى المشترك ، وجب أن يدخل تحته جميع أنواعه ، رعاية لعموم قوله : ( خلق لكم ما في الأرض جميعاً ) . قوله : ( اللام لعود المنفعة ) ، قلنا : هذا يشكل بقوله تعالى : ( ولهم عذاب أليم ) . والله أعلم .
In the locus. The default is no homonymy. And metaphor requires that this word be assigned to the common meaning between them. That common meaning is that the contained occurs inside the container, whether it permeates it, as in an accident and its subject, or is placed in its hollow, as in water and a jug. If we take the word 'fi' for this common meaning, it must include all its types, in observance of the generality of His statement: (He created for you all that is in the earth). [Then] his statement: (The lam is for the return of benefit), we say: This is problematic by His statement the Exalted: (And for them is a painful punishment). And God knows best.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage defines the meaning of the Arabic preposition 'في' (fi) and rebuts an objection regarding the lam in a Qur'anic verse. The author states that originally, words do not have multiple independent meanings (اشتراك) but can be used metaphorically (مجاز). Here, 'في' is assigned a common meaning: containment (being inside a container), whether the contained permeates (like an accident in its substance) or is simply placed inside (like water in a jug). This common meaning applies to all uses of 'في', including the verse 'خلق لكم ما في الأرض جميعاً' (He created for you all that is in the earth). An opponent objects that the lam in 'خلق لكم' indicates that the creation is for the benefit of humans (لام لعود المنفعة), implying a purpose. The author rebuts by citing the verse 'ولهم عذاب أليم' (And for them is a painful punishment), where the lam (لهم) cannot indicate benefit—it indicates possession in a harmful context. Thus, the lam does not always signify benefit, so the objection fails. The author concludes with 'والله أعلم' (God knows best), acknowledging that ultimate knowledge belongs to God.

Key Terms
في
الاشتراك
المجاز
القدر المشترك
اللام لعود المنفعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The lam (ل) always indicates benefit for the recipient.
Correction: The lam can also indicate possession in a harmful context, as in 'ولهم عذاب أليم' (And for them is a painful punishment), so it does not always signify benefit.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the common meaning of the preposition 'في'?
A) The contained is inside the container, whether permeating or placed.
B) It is used only for physical objects.
C) It indicates benefit for the recipient.
D) It has multiple unrelated meanings by default.

Key Takeaway
The preposition 'في' has a common meaning of containment, and the lam does not always indicate benefit, as shown by the verse on painful punishment.

The Two Meanings of 'Ja'ala'
Author distinguishes two meanings of 'ja'ala' as a preamble.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two distinct meanings of the Arabic verb 'ja'ala' and why this distinction is essential for interpreting Quranic verses about divine creation.

Core Idea
The verb 'ja'ala' shifts meaning depending on its grammatical pattern.

Before diving into theological proofs, the author clarifies a critical linguistic point. The verb 'ja'ala' (to make) can mean either 'to create ex nihilo' or 'to make an essence become described by a quality.' Understanding this distinction prevents misinterpretation of key Quranic verses that use 'ja'ala'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Notice the two examples given for each meaning.
Pay attention to the syntactic structure: transitive to one object vs. two objects.
2
Identify the two meanings
The author states that 'ja'ala' when transitive to one object means bringing into existence/creation; when transitive to two objects means giving a quality to an essence. Identify the Quranic examples for each.
Verse 6:1 for creation; verses 19:30 and 2:128 for bestowing qualities.
3
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider how the meaning of 'ja'ala' affects the interpretation of verses about divine actions. This distinction will be used to argue that God creates actions.
This preface is a key methodological tool.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث في التمسك بالايات المشتملة على لفظ البصل، وما يجي ميره اعلم : أن صيغة جعل ، قد تجيء متعدية إلى مفعول واحد ، ويكون معناها: الإحداث والتكوين. قال تعالى : وجعل الظلمات والنور (١) وقد تجيء متعدية إلى مفعولين ويكون معناها : جعل الذات موصوفة بصفة . قال تعالى : وجعلني نبي (٢) واجعلنا مسلمين لك (٣) إذا عرفت هذه المقدمة ، فلنذكر الدلائل :

Know that the form of 'ja'ala' sometimes comes transitive to one object, and its meaning is bringing into existence and creation. He said the Exalted: 'And He made the darkness and the light.' And sometimes it comes transitive to two objects, and its meaning is making the essence described with a quality. He said the Exalted: 'He made me a prophet' and 'Make us Muslims to You.' When you have recognized this preamble, let us mention the proofs.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage introduces a new chapter (Chapter Three) dealing with verses that contain the verb 'ja'ala' (to make/appoint). The author begins with a linguistic clarification: the verb 'ja'ala' has two possible syntactic patterns. First, it can be transitive to one object, meaning to create or bring into existence, as in the verse 'And He made the darkness and the light' (6:1). Second, it can be transitive to two objects, meaning to make the essence described with a quality, as in 'He made me a prophet' (19:30) and 'Make us Muslims to You' (2:128). This preface is necessary because the correct interpretation of such verses depends on understanding which construction is used. After this preamble, the author states that he will proceed to mention the proofs (which follow in the text).

Three Arguments Against Interpreting the Verse as Request for Divine Aids
Refutes three attempts to interpret the verse as a request for divine aids.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why interpreting the verse as a request for divine aids (al-altaf) is invalid according to three rational and textual arguments.

Core Idea
Asking for divine aids is either already fulfilled or irrelevant — so the verse must mean direct creation of faith.

The opponent suggests that the verse "Rabbana wa-j'alna muslimayni" should be understood as a prayer for divine aids (altaf) rather than asking Allah to directly create faith. But the author responds with three decisive objections. This lesson unpacks those objections, showing how each one dismantles the alternative interpretation and reaffirms the apparent meaning of the verse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three arguments
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of the rebuttal.
The passage is a direct refutation with enumerated reasons.
2
Understand the first argument: Violation of apparent meaning
The word 'ja'ala' is directly attached to Islam in the verse; diverting it to 'divine aids' contradicts the literal wording.
This is a linguistic argument based on the structure of the verse.
3
Grasp the second argument: Requesting what is already obtained
According to the opponent's own theology, God has already done all possible divine aids, so asking for more is impossible (tahsil al-hasil).
This argument uses the opponent's premises against them.
4
Examine the third argument: Effect of divine aids
Divine aids either have an effect on the balance of action/omission or they don't. If no effect, they are irrelevant; if effect, they become causally problematic.
This argument forces a dilemma on the opponent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: و لما لا يجوز حمله على طلب الألطاف ؟ ، قلنا: هذا أيضاً مدفوع لوجوه: الأول: إن لفظ الجعل مضاف إلى الاسلام، فصرفه إلى غيره مخالفة للظاهر. الثاني: إن عندكم كل ما أمكن فعله من الألطاف، فقد فعله الله فحمل هذا السؤال على طلب الألطاف يكون طلباً لتحصيل الحاصل. وهو محال. الثالث: إن تلك الألطاف. إما أن يكون لها أثر في ترجيح جانب الفعل على جانب الترك، أو لا يكون. فإن لم يكن لها أثر في هذا الترجيح، كان أمراً أجنبياً عن الإيمان، جارياً مجرى نعيق الغراب، وصرير الباب. وأما إن كان ...
His saying: And why is it not permissible to carry it on the request for divine aids (al-altaf)? We say: this too is rebutted with respects: The first: that the word 'ja'ala' is attached to Islam, so turning it to other than it is a violation of the apparent meaning. The second: that according to you, every possible divine aid that can be done, God has done it, so carrying this question on request for divine aids would be a request for obtaining the already-obtained (tahsil al-hasil), and that is impossible. The third: that those divine aids either have an effect in tipping the balance towards action or towards omission, or they do not. If they have no effect in this tipping, then it is something extraneous to faith, running in the course of the caw of a crow and the creaking of a door. And if they have effect...
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a rebuttal of the opponent's suggestion that the verse 'Rabbana wa-j'alna muslimayni' should be interpreted as a request for divine aids (الألطاف) rather than the direct creation of faith. The author gives three arguments:

1. The word 'ja'ala' (جعل) is directly attached to Islam (الإسلام) in the verse; diverting it to mean something else (like divine aids) contradicts the apparent wording (مخالفة للظاهر).

2. According to the opponent's own theological position (that God does everything possible for divine guidance), God has already done all possible divine aids. So asking for more divine aids would be requesting something already obtained (تحصيل الحاصل), which is impossible.

3. Divine aids (الألطاف) either have an effect in tipping the balance towards performing an action or refraining from it, or they do not. If they have no effect, they are irrelevant to faith, like the meaningless sounds of a crow or a creaking door. If they do have an effect (the text is cut off, but the implication is that they would then be part of the causal chain leading to faith, which the opponent denies), the argument continues.

Thus the interpretation as a request for divine aids is invalid.

Key Terms
الألطاف
تحصيل الحاصل
مخالفة للظاهر
ترجيح جانب الفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse can be understood as a request for divine aids (altaf) rather than direct creation of faith.
Correction: This interpretation fails because (1) it contradicts the literal wording, (2) it requests what is already obtained, and (3) divine aids are either ineffective or causally problematic.

Checkpoint

B) It leads to requesting what is already obtained (tahsil al-hasil).

D) It contradicts the consensus of linguists.

Key Takeaway
The interpretation of the verse as a request for divine aids is invalid because it violates the apparent meaning, requests what is already obtained, and renders divine aids either irrelevant or causally problematic.

The Meaning of 'Islam' With and Without the Article
The author explains the third point about the word 'Islam' and its contextual meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the word 'Islam' can have different meanings depending on whether it is used with or without the definite article, and how it relates to faith and submission.

Core Idea
Islam means faith when alone, but submission when coupled with 'laka'.

This lesson examines a subtle but important linguistic point in the Qur'an: the meaning of the word 'Islam' can shift based on its grammatical context. The author distinguishes between its general use (meaning faith) and its use with the letter lam (meaning submission and compliance). Understanding this distinction helps avoid misinterpretation of key verses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and the literal translation together. Let the author's words introduce the two contexts.

الثالث : إن لفظ الاسلام إذا ذكر خالياً عن الصلات ، أفاد الإيمان ، أما إذا قرن بحرف اللام ، كقوله : ( مسلمين لك ) فالمراد الاستسلام والانقياد والرضا ، بكل ما قضاه الله. فيحتمل : أن يكون المراد بقوله : ( اجعلنا مسلمين لك ) : ما ذكرناه . والله أعلم

This passage is a brief linguistic and theological clarification about the word 'Islam' in the Qur'an. The author presents a third point (likely continuing an earlier list). The passage ends with 'wa Allahu a'lam' (and God knows best), indicating humility in interpretation.
