First Answer: Literal Meaning of 'Aghfalna' for Creation
Al-Razi gives the first answer to the objection, arguing for literal meaning of creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's first answer to the objection of ambiguity, including his principle that homonymy is exceptional and his three proofs for taking the literal meaning as creation.

Core Idea
Ambiguity is not the default; frequency and immediate understanding reveal the literal meaning.

When a word can mean two things, how do we decide which is the real meaning? Al-Razi confronts this question about the word 'aghfalna'. He argues that we cannot simply assume ambiguity; instead, we must use evidence to determine which meaning is literal and which is metaphorical. In this passage, he presents his first answer with three compelling proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing for analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the argument flow naturally before you dissect it.
Notice the three numbered aspects (الوجه الأول, الثاني, الثالث) that structure the proof.
2
Identify the main claim
Al-Razi states that homonymy (ishtirak) is contrary to the original principle, so we must decide which meaning is literal. Find the sentence where he says making the word literal for creation is more appropriate.
The claim is: 'وجعله حقيقة في التكوين، مجازاً في الوجدان : أولى من العكس'.
3
Examine each of the three proofs
Work through each proof one by one: (1) frequency of usage, (2) immediate understanding (mubadarat al-fahm), (3) rational order of literal and metaphorical. Understand how each supports the claim.
The third proof uses an epistemological principle: knowledge depends on the known.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Ask yourself: Why is al-Razi so concerned about the literal meaning? How does this connect to the broader theological discussion about divine action and creation?
This argument defends a specific interpretation of the Qur'anic verse about forgetfulness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الأول من وجهين : الأول : إن الاشتراك خلاف الأصل، فوجب أن يعتقد أن وزن الأفعال حقيقة في أحدها ، مجاز في الآخر. وجعله حقيقة في التكوين ، مجازاً في الوجدان : أولى من العكس . ويدل عليه وجوه : الوجه الأول : إن مجيء هذا البناء بمعنى التكوين أكثر من مجيئه بمعنى الوجدان . والكثرة دليل الرجحان . الثاني : إن مبادرة الفهم إلى التكوين ، أكثر من مبادرته إلى الوجدان . أعني : من هذا اللفظ ، ومبادرة الفهم دليل كونه حقيقة فيه . الثالث : إنا إن جعلناه حقيقة في التكوين ، أمكن جعله مجازاً عن الوجدان ، لأن العلم بالشيء تابع لحصول المعلوم ، فجعل اللفظ حقيقة في الأصل، مجازاً في التبع ، موافق للعقول . أما لو جعلناه حقيقة في الوجدان ، مجازاً في الوجود، لزم جعله حقيقة في التبع ، مجازاً في الأصل ، وإنه على خلاف المعقول
And the answer to the first [objection] is from two aspects: The first: That homonymy (al-ishtirak) is contrary to the original principle, so it is necessary to believe that the verbal form of أفعل (af‘ala) is literal (haqiqah) in one of them, and metaphorical (majaz) in the other. And making it literal for creation (al-takwin) and metaphorical for finding (al-wijdan) is more appropriate than the reverse. And it is supported by [several] aspects: The first aspect: That the occurrence of this construction with the meaning of creation is more frequent than its occurrence with the meaning of finding. And frequency is evidence of preponderance. The second: That the immediate understanding (mubadarat al-fahm) of creation is more frequent than the immediate understanding of finding—I mean, from this word—and the immediate understanding is evidence of it being literal in it. The third: That if we make it literal for creation, it is possible to make it metaphorical for finding, because knowledge of a thing is dependent on the attainment of the known; so making the word literal for the original [meaning] and metaphorical for the derivative is in accordance with reason. But if we make it literal for finding and metaphorical for existence, it would necessitate making it literal for the derivative and metaphorical for the original, and that is contrary to reason.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two answers to an objection regarding the word 'aghfalna' (أغفلنا). The objection likely claimed that the word could mean either 'We created forgetfulness' or 'We found him forgetful', and therefore it is ambiguous. Al-Razi's first answer argues against ambiguity (ishtirak) being the default assumption. He states that homonymy is contrary to the original principle (خلاف الأصل), so one must determine which meaning is literal (haqiqah) and which is metaphorical (majaz). He then argues that making the word literal for creation and metaphorical for finding is more appropriate. Three supporting proofs are given: (1) The word occurs more frequently in the sense of creation, and frequency indicates preponderance. (2) The immediate understanding (mubadarat al-fahm) of the word points to creation more often, which is evidence that it is the literal meaning. (3) If we take creation as the literal meaning, we can easily derive the metaphorical sense of finding, since knowledge depends on the known; thus, making the literal meaning the original and the metaphorical meaning derivative is rationally sound. Conversely, making finding literal would invert this logical order, which is irrational. The passage follows a standard kalam method of linguistic analysis to defend a theological position.

Key Terms
اشتراك (ishtirak)
حقيقة (haqiqah)
مجاز (majaz)
تكوين (takwin)
وجدان (wijdan)
مبادرة الفهم (mubadarat al-fahm)
كثرة (kathrah)
رجحان (rujhan)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that ambiguity (ishtirak) is a normal and acceptable state that requires no resolution.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that homonymy is contrary to the original principle; we must always try to determine which meaning is literal based on evidence.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi's first answer, why is it more appropriate to take 'أفعل' as literal for creation rather than for finding?
A) Because creation is more common in usage and the mind immediately understands it that way.
B) Because finding is always metaphorical in Arabic.
C) Because the Quran uses it only for creation.
D) Because the objector himself admitted it.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's first answer uses frequency, immediate understanding, and rational order to argue that 'af'ala is literal for creation and metaphorical for finding.

?

Defending 'Wa' Over 'Fa': Necessary vs. Non-Necessary Consequences
Al-Razi responds to the objection that the verse should have used 'fa' instead of 'wa' if the meaning is creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Razi defends the use of 'wa' instead of 'fa' in the verse, based on the concept of necessary consequence.

Core Idea
Not all divine acts produce necessary effects; forgetfulness may lead to hesitation, not necessarily to following desires.

In this passage, al-Razi addresses a grammatical and theological objection. The objector claimed that if the verse meant creation of forgetfulness, the conjunction 'fa' (so) should have been used to indicate a necessary causal link. Al-Razi replies by distinguishing between necessary and non-necessary consequences, using the analogy of breaking and shattering.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Focus on the objection and al-Razi's response.
Notice the key terms 'fa' and 'wa'.
2
Understand the objection
The objector argues that 'fa' should have been used instead of 'wa' if forgetfulness necessarily causes following desires.
3
Examine al-Razi's analogy
Al-Razi compares the relationship to that of shattering and breaking, where breaking is a necessary consequence. He argues that forgetfulness does not necessitate following desires.
The analogy shows that not all causal relationships are necessary.
4
Reach the conclusion
Since forgetfulness can lead to hesitation rather than desire, 'wa' is appropriate, and the objection fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما قوله : ولو كان المراد ذلك ، لكان الواجب ذكر الفاء، لا ذكر الواو . فجوابه : إن هذا إنما يلزم لو كان اتباع الهوى من لوازم الإغفال عن ذكر الله ، كما أن الإنكسار من لوازم الكسر . ومعلوم أنه ليس الأمر كذلك ، لأنه لا يلزم من صيرورة الإنسان غافلاً عن ذكر الله ، صيرورته متبعاً للهوى . لاحتمال أن يكون الغافل عن ذكر الله ، لا يصير متبعا للهوى . بل يبقى متوقفا ، لاباً في حيز الحيرة . فقط ما قالوه
As for his statement: 'If that were intended, it would be necessary to mention the letter fa (so), not the letter wa (and).' The answer is: This would only be necessary if following desire were a necessary consequence (lāzim) of being made forgetful of the remembrance of Allah, just as breaking is a necessary consequence of shattering. And it is known that this is not the case, because it does not follow from a person's becoming forgetful of the remembrance of Allah that he becomes a follower of desire. For it is possible that the one who is forgetful of the remembrance of Allah does not become a follower of desire, but rather remains hesitant, un-? in the realm of confusion. That is all they said.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection about the Arabic conjunction used in the verse. The objector argued that if 'aghfalna' meant creation of forgetfulness, the verse should have used the particle 'fa' (so) to indicate a necessary consequence, instead of 'wa' (and). Al-Razi replies that the use of 'wa' is correct because being made forgetful does not necessarily lead to following one's desires; it is not an automatic causal consequence like breaking follows from shattering. A person might become forgetful but still not follow his desires; he might remain in a state of hesitation or confusion. Thus, the conjunction 'wa' is appropriate, as it does not imply necessary sequence. The objection is therefore invalid.

واو (wa)
لازم (lāzim)
انكسار (inkisār)
كسر (kasr)
متبوع (mutba‘)
حيرة (ḥayrah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if Allah creates forgetfulness, it must necessarily lead to following desires, requiring 'fa'.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that forgetfulness does not necessitate following desires; a person may remain hesitant, so 'wa' is appropriate.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Razi say the use of 'wa' instead of 'fa' is acceptable?
A) Because 'wa' is more common in the Quran.
B) Because forgetfulness does not necessarily lead to following desires; there could be hesitation.
C) Because the objector misunderstood the grammar.
D) Because 'fa' is only used for physical causation.

Key Takeaway
The conjunction 'wa' is correct in the verse because forgetfulness does not necessarily cause following desires; a person may remain hesitant or confused.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you become forgetful of Allah in a moment of weakness, do you assume that sin is inevitable, or do you allow yourself a moment of hesitation to turn back? How can you actively choose hesitation over surrender?
?
Think of a time you used a small mistake as an excuse to fall into a bigger sin, reasoning 'since I already slipped, I might as well continue.' How does al-Razi's argument that consequences are not always necessary challenge that pattern in your life?
?
If you truly believed that forgetfulness does not force you to follow your desires, what would you do differently tomorrow when you feel distant from Allah? What specific action will you take to prove that hesitation can be a turning point?

Are Hasana and Sayyi'a Homonyms or Univocal?
Author defends univocality of hasana/sayyi'a against homonym objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the terms hasana and sayyi'a are considered univocal (mutawati) rather than homonymous (mushtarak) according to the author.

Core Idea
Hasana and sayyi'a share a core meaning; context specifies the type of benefit.

In this passage, the author responds to an objection that the terms 'hasana' and 'sayyi'a' are homonyms, meaning they refer to two completely different things. The author argues instead that they are univocal—they refer to a single concept that is then specified by context. Understanding this distinction is crucial for proper theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part where the objector claims the words are homonyms. Note the difficulty: if the terms are homonyms, they cannot apply to both worldly and otherworldly benefits simultaneously.
A homonym like 'bank' has two unrelated meanings.
2
Read the author's rebuttal
Read the author's response. He denies the terms are homonyms and asserts they are univocal. He explains that there is a shared meaning: anything good in itself is hasana.
Univocal means the term applies with the same core meaning to different instances.
3
Examine the specification
Notice how the author then distinguishes two types of benefit: if it benefits in the Hereafter, it is obedience; if in this world, it is present benefit. Both are covered by the same word because they share the core attribute of being good.
This shows that the word 'hasana' is not ambiguous but rather broad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : و لفظ الحسنة والسيئة ، بالنسبة إلى المعنيين مشترك ، فلم يمكن حمله عليها ، قلنا : لا نسلم أنه مشترك ، بل متواطىء . وذلك لأن كل ما كان به فهو حسنة . ثم إنه إن كان منتفعا به في الآخرة فهو الطاعة ، وإن كان منتفعا به في الدنيا ، فهو النفع الحاضر. فثبت : أن هذا اللفظ بالنسبة إلى هذين الأمرين متواطىء ، لا مشترك ، فكان متناولاً لكل واحد من القسمين
His statement: 'And the word hasana (good) and sayyi'a (evil), with respect to the two meanings, is a homonym (mushtarak), so it cannot be applied to both.' We said: We do not accept that it is a homonym; rather it is univocal (mutawati). That is because everything that is good in itself is hasana. Then if one benefits from it in the Hereafter, it is obedience; and if one benefits from it in this world, it is present benefit. So it is established that this word with respect to these two matters is univocal, not homonymous, so it encompasses each of the two categories.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer on the semantics of 'hasana' and 'sayyi'a'. The opponent claims these terms are homonyms (mushtarak) — i.e., they refer to two distinct meanings with no common core — and therefore cannot simultaneously refer to both worldly benefits and moral acts. The author responds that the terms are actually univocal (mutawati): they share a common meaning of 'that which is good in itself'. This general goodness is then specified by context: if it benefits in the afterlife, it is an act of obedience; if in this world, it is a worldly benefit. Thus the same word applies to both categories because there is a single underlying concept.

Misconception: Hasana and sayyi'a are homonyms with completely separate meanings.
Correction: They are univocal: they share a core meaning of being good or evil, and context specifies the type of benefit.

Checkpoint

A) They always have the same meaning
B) They share a core meaning of goodness/evil that is then specified
C) They are used in exactly the same way
D) They are metaphorical

Key Takeaway
Hasana and sayyi'a are univocal terms that share a common meaning, with context determining whether the benefit is worldly or otherworldly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If the core of 'hasana' is intrinsic goodness, how does your life reflect that you truly believe moral acts are good in themselves, even when they bring no immediate worldly gain? What one change can you make today to live this truth?

The Terms Hasanah and Sayyi'ah Are Univocal (Mutawāṭi’)
Sanusi refutes objections and establishes univocality of hasanah and sayyi'ah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi argues that the Quranic terms hasanah and sayyi'ah are univocal (mutawāṭi') rather than ambiguous (mushtarak), and how they encompass both worldly and religious meanings.

Core Idea
Hasanah is anything good—whether worldly benefit or religious obedience.

Sanusi here defends a key principle for interpreting Quranic terms: words like hasanah and sayyi'ah are not ambiguous but have a single core meaning that applies to both worldly and religious contexts. This passage presents objections from opponents who limit these terms to material benefits, and Sanusi refutes each, culminating in a precise logical definition that shows the terms are univocal. Understanding this argument is crucial for correctly grasping Quranic ethics.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the back-and-forth structure of objections and responses.
Pay attention to the sequence: each objection is introduced by 'قوله' and Sanusi's reply by 'قلنا'.
2
Identify the three objections
Find each objection the opponent raises: (1) restricting the terms to worldly benefits/harms, (2) claiming the verse was only about fertility/infertility, (3) arguing that it's improper to say acts of obedience 'befall' a person.
The fourth objection about ambiguity is the final one, which Sanusi refutes with his univocality argument.
3
Analyze Sanusi's responses
For each objection, note Sanusi's refutation: (1) arbitrary restriction, (2) consider general wording not specific occasion, (3) permissible usage examples, (4) definition of hasanah as 'everything good' leading to univocality.
The final response is the most important for the lesson's objective.
4
Synthesize the univocality argument
Understand Sanusi's definition: everything by which something is good is hasanah. Then it is specified as either worldly benefit (present benefit) or religious benefit (obedience). This shows the word is univocal, not ambiguous.
Contrast this with mushtarak (homonymy) where two meanings are unrelated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( المراد من الحسنة والسيئة ; المنافع والمضار ، قلنا : قد بينا أن لفظ الحسنة والسيئة ، كما يتناولان المنافع والمضار ، فقد يتناولان الطاعات والمعاصي . فتخصيصه بأحدهما تحكم محض
قوله : الآية إنما نزلت في المنافع والمضار، والخصب والجدب ) قلنا :
اللفظ ، لا بخصوص السبب
•
العبرة بعموم قوله : « لا يقال في الطاعات والمعاصي، إنها أصابتني ، قلنا : لا نسلم . فإنه يجوز أن يقال : أصابني توفيق من الله ، وعون من الله . وفلان أصابه خذلان من الله . والمراد من ذلك : التوفيق والعون لتلك الطاعة ، أو ما يرجح جانبه اعلى جانب المعصية
قوله : و لفظ الحسنة والسيئة ، بالنسبة إلى المعنيين مشترك ، فلم يمكن حمله عليها ، قلنا : لا نسلم أنه مشترك ، بل متواطىء . وذلك لأن كل ما كان به فهو حسنة . ثم إنه إن كان منتفعا به في الآخرة فهو الطاعة ، وإن كان منتفعاً به في الدنيا ، فهو النفع الحاضر. فثبت : أن هذا اللفظ بالنسبة إلى هذين الأمرين متواطىء ، لا مشترك ، فكان متناولاً لكل واحد من القسمين .
His statement: 'The intended meaning of hasanah and sayyi'ah is benefits and harms.' We said: We have already shown that the word hasanah and sayyi'ah, as they encompass benefits and harms, they also encompass acts of obedience and acts of disobedience. So specifying it to one of them is pure arbitrary judgment. His statement: 'The verse was only revealed concerning benefits and harms, fertility and infertility.' We said: Consideration is given to the generality of the word, not the specificity of the occasion. He said: 'It is not said concerning acts of obedience and disobedience that they have befallen me.' We said: We do not accept that. For it is permissible to say: 'A success from God has befallen me, and an aid from God.' And 'So-and-so has been struck by abandonment from God.' And what is meant by that is the success and aid for that act of obedience, or what weighs its side over the side of disobedience. His statement: 'And the word hasanah and sayyi'ah with respect to the two meanings is ambiguous (mushtarak), so it cannot be applied to them.' We said: We do not accept that it is ambiguous; rather it is univocal (mutawāṭi'). That is because everything by which something is good is hasanah. Then if it is benefited thereby in the hereafter, it is obedience; and if it is benefited thereby in this world, it is present benefit. So it is established that this word with respect to these two matters is univocal, not ambiguous, so it encompasses each of the two categories.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is a series of objections and answers defending the position that the Quranic terms 'hasanah' (good) and 'sayyi'ah' (evil) are not limited to worldly benefits and harms but also include acts of obedience and disobedience. The author first responds to the opponent who restricts these terms to material benefits, stating that the words also apply to religious acts, so restricting them is arbitrary. Next, the opponent argues the verse was revealed only about worldly matters; the author replies that the general wording, not the specific occasion, is authoritative. The opponent then claims it is improper to say acts of obedience 'befall' a person; the author rejects this, giving examples like 'success from God has befallen me.' Finally, the opponent argues the words are ambiguous (mushtarak) between worldly and religious meanings, making their application unclear. The author refutes this by explaining the words are univocal (mutawāṭi'): anything beneficial is called hasanah; if its benefit is in the afterlife, it is obedience; if in this world, it is worldly benefit. Thus the term covers both categories without ambiguity.

Key Terms
حسنة
سيئة
متواطىء
مشترك
المنافع والمضار
الطاعات والمعاصي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a word can apply to two different things, it must be ambiguous (mushtarak).
Correction: The word can be univocal under a broader genus. Sanusi shows that hasanah means 'everything beneficial' and then context specifies whether it is worldly or religious, so it is one meaning with two subtypes.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the word 'hasanah' not ambiguous (mushtarak) between worldly benefit and religious obedience?
A) Because the word is used in two completely different senses.
B) Because the word has a single meaning (everything good) and then divides into types.
C) Because the word is derived from a root that only applies to worldly matters.
D) Because the word was revealed only about acts of obedience.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi shows that 'hasanah' means anything beneficial, and its specific application—worldly or religious—depends on context, so the term is univocal not ambiguous.

?

?
When you hear the word 'good deed' (hasanah), do you automatically limit its meaning to religious obedience, or do you also see worldly benefits as good? Which view aligns with Sanusi's univocal definition, and what does that mean for how you evaluate your daily actions?
?
Can you recall a time you assumed a term in Islamic discourse was ambiguous, only to later realize it had a single core meaning? How might this lesson equip you to be more precise in understanding Islamic terms?

The Objection and First Answer: Abraham's Example
Sanusi presents the first answer to reconcile the verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses Abraham's statement to show that attributing evil to humans does not contradict divine creation.

Core Idea
Attributing something to oneself does not negate God as the Creator.

In reconciling two seemingly contradictory verses, Sanusi first addresses the objection that interpreting 'hasanah' and 'sayyi'ah' as acts leads to contradiction. He then presents the first answer, citing the example of Prophet Abraham, who attributed illness to himself but healing to God. This shows that human attribution does not negate divine causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage that includes the objection and the first answer. Note how Sanusi sets up the problem.
The objection is that interpreting the terms as acts leads to a contradiction between the two parts of the verse.
2
Identify the objection
Understand the opponent's claim: if 'hasanah' and 'sayyi'ah' are acts of obedience/disobedience, then attributing evil to humans contradicts 'All is from God'.
3
Analyze the first answer
See how Sanusi uses Abraham's statement 'When I am ill, it is He who cures me' to show that attributing evil to oneself does not deny God creating both illness and healing.
This is a key point: attribution and creation are compatible.
4
Reflect on the core idea
Consider how this answer resolves the apparent contradiction without denying human responsibility or divine power.
The core idea is that attributing something to oneself does not negate God as the ultimate Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : « لو حملنا الحسنة والسيئة مهنا على الطاعات والمعاصي ، لزم التناقض بين أول الآية وبين آخرها ، قلنا : لا نسلم . أما قوله تعالى : ( فمال هؤلاء القوم لا يكادون يفقهون حديثاً ) فنقول : وجه تمسك الخصم به : أن المدح والذم لا يصحان ، إلا إذا كان العبد موجداً . وسيأتي الجواب عن هذه الشبهة ، إن شاء الله تعالى . وأما قوله تعالى : ( وما أصابك من سيئة فمن نفسك ) فالجواب عن تمسكهم به من وجوه :
الأول : إنه تعالى قال حكاية عن إبراهيم - عليه السلام ـ أنه قال : وإذا مرضت فهو يشفين ) أضاف المرض إلى نفسه ، والشفاء إلى الله . ولم يقدح ذلك في إقراره بأن خالق المرض والشفاء، هو الله تعالى . فكذا ههنا . أضاف السيئة إلينا ، والحسنة إلى نفسه . ولا يقدح ذلك في كونه تعالى خالقاً للكل .
الثاني : إن أكثر المفسرين . قالوا في تفسير قول إبراهيم - عليـه السلام - : ( هذا ربي ) : أنه ذكر هذا استفهاماً على سبيل الإنكار . كأنه قال : هذا ربي ؟ فههنا يحتمل أن يكون المراد ذلك . كأنه قيل : الإيمان الذي وقع على وفق قصده : من الله . والكفر الذي وقع خلاف قصده : من نفسه . وهذا محال . لأنه لو وقع بإيقاعه ، لما وقع إلا ما قصده واختاره . فلما حكمنا بأن الإيمان الذي وقع على وفق قصده : من الله ، لا منه . فكيف يمكن أن يقال : الكفر الذي وقع على خلاف مقصده : منه ؟
والحاصل : إن قوله : ( وما أصابك من سيئة ، فمن نفسك ) : محمول على الاستفهام، على سبيل الإنكار ، حتى يلزم التناقض بين أول الآية وآخرها .
His statement: 'If we interpreted hasanah and sayyi'ah here as acts of obedience and disobedience, a contradiction would arise between the beginning of the verse and its end.' We said: We do not accept that. As for the statement of the Most High: 'So what is the matter with these people that they scarcely understand any statement?' we say: The opponent's way of clinging to it is that praise and blame are not valid unless the servant is the agent. And the answer to this specious argument will come, God willing. And as for the statement of the Most High: 'And whatever evil befalls you, it is from yourself,' the answer to their clinging to it is in ways: First: The Most High related from Abraham, peace be upon him, that he said: 'And when I am ill, it is He who cures me.' He attributed illness to himself and healing to God. And that did not detract from his acknowledgment that the Creator of illness and healing is God Most High. So likewise here: He attributed evil to us and good to Himself. And that does not detract from His being the Creator of all. Second: Most of the commentators said regarding the interpretation of Abraham's statement 'This is my Lord' that he mentioned this as an interrogative denoting denial, as if he said 'This is my Lord?' So here it is possible that the intended meaning is that. As if it were said: Faith that occurred in accordance with His purpose is from God, and disbelief that occurred contrary to His purpose is from the person himself. And this is impossible. Because if it occurred by His causing, only what He intended and chose would occur. Since we have judged that faith which occurred in accordance with His purpose is from God, not from the person, how can it be said that disbelief which occurred contrary to His purpose is from the person? The conclusion: His statement 'And whatever evil befalls you, it is from yourself' is to be interpreted as an interrogative denoting denial, so that a contradiction between the beginning of the verse and its end would be necessary.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer section where the opponent claims that interpreting 'hasanah' and 'sayyi'ah' as acts of obedience/disobedience leads to contradiction between two parts of a verse: 'Say: All is from God' and 'Whatever evil befalls you, it is from yourself.' The author responds by giving two answers. First, he cites Abraham's statement attributing illness to himself and healing to God, without negating that God creates both illness and healing. Similarly, attributing evil to humans does not deny God's creation of all things. Second, many exegetes interpret Abraham's 'This is my Lord' as an interrogative of denial, explaining that faith coming from God and disbelief from the person is impossible if God causes everything—since only what God intends occurs. Therefore, the verse 'Whatever evil befalls you, it is from yourself' is to be understood as an interrogative of denial, meaning the questioner is asking in disbelief. This removes the contradiction because it is not a statement of fact.

Key Terms
التناقض
إبراهيم
خالق المرض والشفاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think attributing evil to the human agent undermines divine causation.
Correction: The author shows it is compatible, as with Abraham's example: attributing illness to oneself does not deny God creating illness and healing.

Checkpoint
What is the first way the author answers the objection that attributing evil to humans contradicts 'All is from God'?
A) He argues the verse is abrogated.
B) He cites Abraham's attribution of illness to himself and healing to God.
C) He denies the authenticity of the verse.
D) He says the verse only applies to polytheists.

Key Takeaway
The first answer shows that attributing evil to humans does not deny God's creation, as seen in Abraham's words.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent difficulty or failure you experienced. Did you attribute it entirely to yourself, or did you see God's hand in it? How does Abraham's example of attributing illness to himself but healing to God challenge the way you view such events? Will you begin to see every difficulty as an opportunity to acknowledge God's ultimate control?
?
When you achieve something good, do you quickly credit your own efforts or do you remember that 'whatever good befalls you, it is from God'? How can you cultivate a habit of attributing all good to God, even in small everyday successes?

What specific situation in your life right now are you most prone to forget that God is the Creator of both the good and the bad? Take a moment to rephrase that situation using the language of this lesson: 'When I am [situation], it is from myself; but the outcome is from God.' Will you commit to saying this aloud today?

The Second Answer: Interrogative of Denial
Sanusi presents the second answer and concludes the reconciliation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how interpreting 'evil from yourself' as an interrogative of denial resolves the apparent contradiction.

Core Idea
The verse is a rhetorical question denying independent causation.

The second answer builds on the interpretive tradition of Abraham's statement 'This is my Lord' as an interrogative of denial. Sanusi extends this to the verse in question, arguing that the phrase 'from yourself' is not a statement of fact but a rhetorical question meant to deny that evil originates from the self independently of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the second answer
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'Second' through the conclusion. Focus on how the author interprets Abraham's statement.
Pay attention to the logical argument about faith and disbelief.
2
Understand the interrogative of denial
Learn that commentators interpreted Abraham's 'This is my Lord' as a rhetorical question of denial. This is key to the answer.
The interrogative of denial rejects the attributive claim.
3
Grasp the logical implication
Notice the argument: if God causes all, then only what He intends occurs. Therefore, faith from God and disbelief from the self cannot be literal statements; rather, the verse is a rhetorical denial.
This shows that the apparent contradiction arises from misinterpreting the verse as a factual statement.
4
Connect to the conclusion
The author concludes that the verse 'Whatever evil befalls you, it is from yourself' is to be interpreted as an interrogative of denial, removing the contradiction.
This is the final resolution of the apparent conflict.

قوله : « لو حملنا الحسنة والسيئة مهنا على الطاعات والمعاصي ، لزم التناقض بين أول الآية وبين آخرها ، قلنا : لا نسلم . أما قوله تعالى : ( فمال هؤلاء القوم لا يكادون يفقهون حديثاً ) فنقول : وجه تمسك الخصم به : أن المدح والذم لا يصحان ، إلا إذا كان العبد موجداً . وسيأتي الجواب عن هذه الشبهة ، إن شاء الله تعالى . وأما قوله تعالى : ( وما أصابك من سيئة فمن نفسك ) فالجواب عن تمسكهم به من وجوه :
الأول : إنه تعالى قال حكاية عن إبراهيم - عليه السلام ـ أنه قال : وإذا مرضت فهو يشفين ) أضاف المرض إلى نفسه ، والشفاء إلى الله . ولم يقدح ذلك في إقراره بأن خالق المرض والشفاء، هو الله تعالى . فكذا ههنا . أضاف السيئة إلينا ، والحسنة إلى نفسه . ولا يقدح ذلك في كونه تعالى خالقاً للكل .
الثاني : إن أكثر المفسرين . قالوا في تفسير قول إبراهيم - عليـه السلام - : ( هذا ربي ) : أنه ذكر هذا استفهاماً على سبيل الإنكار . كأنه قال : هذا ربي ؟ فههنا يحتمل أن يكون المراد ذلك . كأنه قيل : الإيمان الذي وقع على وفق قصده : من الله . والكفر الذي وقع خلاف قصده : من نفسه . وهذا محال . لأنه لو وقع بإيقاعه ، لما وقع إلا ما قصده واختاره . فلما حكمنا بأن الإيمان الذي وقع على وفق قصده : من الله ، لا منه . فكيف يمكن أن يقال : الكفر الذي وقع على خلاف مقصده : منه ؟
والحاصل : إن قوله : ( وما أصابك من سيئة ، فمن نفسك ) : محمول على الاستفهام، على سبيل الإنكار ، حتى يلزم التناقض بين أول الآية وآخرها .
His statement: 'If we interpreted hasanah and sayyi'ah here as acts of obedience and disobedience, a contradiction would arise between the beginning of the verse and its end.' We said: We do not accept that. As for the statement of the Most High: 'So what is the matter with these people that they scarcely understand any statement?' we say: The opponent's way of clinging to it is that praise and blame are not valid unless the servant is the agent. And the answer to this specious argument will come, God willing. And as for the statement of the Most High: 'And whatever evil befalls you, it is from yourself,' the answer to their clinging to it is in ways: First: The Most High related from Abraham, peace be upon him, that he said: 'And when I am ill, it is He who cures me.' He attributed illness to himself and healing to God. And that did not detract from his acknowledgment that the Creator of illness and healing is God Most High. So likewise here: He attributed evil to us and good to Himself. And that does not detract from His being the Creator of all. Second: Most of the commentators said regarding the interpretation of Abraham's statement 'This is my Lord' that he mentioned this as an interrogative denoting denial, as if he said 'This is my Lord?' So here it is possible that the intended meaning is that. As if it were said: Faith that occurred in accordance with His purpose is from God, and disbelief that occurred contrary to His purpose is from the person himself. And this is impossible. Because if it occurred by His causing, only what He intended and chose would occur. Since we have judged that faith which occurred in accordance with His purpose is from God, not from the person, how can it be said that disbelief which occurred contrary to His purpose is from the person? The conclusion: His statement 'And whatever evil befalls you, it is from yourself' is to be interpreted as an interrogative denoting denial, so that a contradiction between the beginning of the verse and its end would be necessary.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer section where the opponent claims that interpreting 'hasanah' and 'sayyi'ah' as acts of obedience/disobedience leads to contradiction between two parts of a verse: 'Say: All is from God' and 'Whatever evil befalls you, it is from yourself.' The author responds by giving two answers. First, he cites Abraham's statement attributing illness to himself and healing to God, without negating that God creates both illness and healing. Similarly, attributing evil to humans does not deny God's creation of all things. Second, many exegetes interpret Abraham's 'This is my Lord' as an interrogative of denial, explaining that faith coming from God and disbelief from the person is impossible if God causes everything—since only what God intends occurs. Therefore, the verse 'Whatever evil befalls you, it is from yourself' is to be understood as an interrogative of denial, meaning the questioner is asking in disbelief. This removes the contradiction because it is not a statement of fact.

Key Terms
الاستفهام الإنكاري
إبراهيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Misinterpreting the verse as a factual statement rather than a rhetorical question.
Correction: The author shows that the verse is an interrogative of denial, not a factual attribution of evil to the self independently of God.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude about the verse 'Whatever evil befalls you, it is from yourself'?
A) It is a statement of fact.
B) It is an interrogative of denial.
C) It was abrogated.
D) It applies only to prophets.

Key Takeaway
The apparent contradiction is resolved by understanding the verse as a rhetorical question denying that evil comes from the self independently.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'And whatever evil befalls you, it is from yourself,' does your heart immediately interpret it as a blame or a lesson? How would your view of trials change if you heard it as a rhetorical question denying that you are the independent cause of your own suffering?
?
Have you ever attributed your own failures or sins solely to yourself, forgetting that ultimately all is from Allah? How does this lesson challenge you to re-examine your sense of agency and reliance on God?
?
The passage uses Abraham's statement as a model. When you face confusing verses, do you seek the interpretative tradition of the scholars, or do you jump to your own conclusions? What would it cost you to ignore the deep study of Quranic rhetoric?
